Sera accepted that with a grimace. "Fine."

Mira brought up the continuity model. "Memory has its own problem. It sounds psychological."

"Yes."

"Ancestor becomes family metaphor."

"Yes."

"Continuity becomes abstract."

"Yes."

"Relation becomes vague."

"Yes."

"You are enjoying this."

"No. I am trying to prevent enjoyment."

Anika's voice came from the feed. "I like them."

Keeps-Water-Calm's translator clicked. "Dangerous."

Tavo said, "Which part?"

"Being liked for the blow, not the meaning."

Anika pointed at her camera. "Fine. I respect the meaning and also like the blow."

Brenn marked something. Mira suspected it was not official.

"What word do you use?" Mira asked.

Keeps-Water-Calm produced a pressure pattern, slower than before, designed for the translator to fail productively. The membrane trembled. Chemical sensors flashed. The speaker displayed fragments:

held route

remembering water

not past

owed continuation

ancestor function

answering maintenance

future receives

It rejected the assembled phrase three times, then offered: CONTINUING REMEMBERING.

Keeps-Water-Calm pulsed in visible dissatisfaction.

"Too smooth?" Mira asked.

"Too human."

"All English will be."

"Yes. Use it while knowing the wound."

Brenn looked up. "Continuing remembering is awkward but perhaps useful."

Sera snorted. "That is the most legal praise possible."

"It keeps action in the noun," Brenn said. "Archive is a thing. Memory is a possession or process. Continuing remembering indicates active maintenance."

"And sounds like something a tired committee would invent at 03:00," Sera said.

"That too."

Mira tested it silently. D-17 is not an archive but a living ecology of continuing remembering. The phrase resisted elegance. Good. Elegant language often slid too easily into policy.

"What about sovereignty?" she asked.

The tank darkened.

Brenn stopped writing.

Keeps-Water-Calm moved back from the membrane. "Humans hear sovereignty and look for a ruler, boundary, flag, vote, document, capital, negotiable representative. Orison sovereignty is not absent from those ideas, but D-17 does not hold sovereignty like a person holds a tool."

"How does it hold it?"

"By making interruption a political act."

Mira felt the sentence land hard enough to become structure.

Anika said, quieter, "That one I understood."

Tavo looked down at his hands.

Keeps-Water-Calm continued. "If cutting D-17 changes what a people can receive from those before, then cutting is not resource use. It is governance over Orison continuation. Humans performed governance without standing to govern."

Brenn wrote quickly. "That can support jurisdictional restraint."

"Say human law must stop ruling what it cannot understand well enough to rule," Sera said.

"I cannot write that exactly."

"Coward."

"Employed coward, for now."

Mira looked at the model again. "Continuing remembering as sovereign function?"

Keeps-Water-Calm considered. "Not bad."

The room seemed to notice. Not bad from Keeps-Water-Calm was practically a festival by current standards.

Anika said, "Should we clap?"

"No," everyone said, in different tones.

The lesson continued for two hours. They dismantled human terms and kept the pieces that cut least. Archive became an inaccurate analogy, permitted only in historical explanation and barred from operative findings. Memory became acceptable when paired with living maintenance and when explicitly not treated as stored data. Ancestor remained in plain-language report but not as a biological classification. Continuing remembering became the operative concept for the injunction draft: a living, maintained relation through which Orison lineage, route, obligation, and future capacity remain active.

Brenn wrote a definition that made Sera groan and Mira nod.

Continuing remembering: an active ecological, cultural, and relational process by which living Orison-associated systems maintain route knowledge, lineage obligation, adaptive response, and future continuity; not reducible to stored information, burial, monument, individual cognition, or inert archive.

Keeps-Water-Calm read it through the translator.

"Cracked vessel," they said.

"But holds some water?" Mira asked.

"Enough for this crossing."

Brenn attached the definition to the draft injunction.

On the Drex feed, Anika looked down for a long moment. When she spoke, the roughness in her voice surprised everyone. "Can a human family have continuing remembering?"

Keeps-Water-Calm's colors shifted. "Yes. But humans often move it into objects after breaking the practices that keep it alive."

Anika nodded. "My sister sends me her son's school sounds. Not pictures. Sounds. The pump in their dome, his spoon on the table, lesson bell. Says if I forget the noise of the place, money becomes the only reason I am out here."

No one spoke.

"Is that stupid?" she asked.

Keeps-Water-Calm answered, "No."

Anika's jaw tightened. "Good."

Sera looked at her display very hard. Mira pretended not to notice.

Tavo said, "Contracts broke that too."

"Yes," Brenn said. "Debt can sever continuing remembering."

Anika glared at him. "Do not put my nephew in your definition."

"I will not."

"But do put oxygen in the remedy."

"I will."

Mira understood then why the word lesson felt too small. Keeps-Water-Calm was not teaching vocabulary. They were forcing human law to feel the analogies it wanted to use. If humans could recognize, even dimly, that their own lives held practices not reducible to data or property, they might stop treating Orison continuity as decorative complexity.

Might.

The word was thin and necessary.

Near the end, Keeps-Water-Calm touched the membrane with one lobe. "Now your hard question."

Mira had not asked it aloud. She had been waiting, avoiding, perhaps hoping the Orison would refuse first.

"The Treaty Pool," she said.

Sera's head came up. "No."

"You do not know what I am asking."

"I know the chapter title."

Mira blinked.

"Sorry," Sera said. "Sedative aftereffects. Continue."

Brenn did not even look up. Either he had missed it or had reached a new discipline of ignoring impossible meta-comments. "What about the Treaty Pool?"

Mira looked at Keeps-Water-Calm. "The injunction hearing needs more than my model, Sera's imprint, and chart evidence. Board counsel will argue everything depends on technical interpretation and subjective witness. The symbiont events help but are vulnerable to hazard framing. If the court can experience controlled relation under Orison terms-"

"No," Sera said again.

Keeps-Water-Calm did not answer.

Mira made herself finish. "If the court can experience a constrained sensory exchange in the Treaty Pool, with withdrawal conditions and medical limits, Brenn may be able to write protection that does not rest entirely on our language."

Anika said from the feed, "You mean hurt people to prove hurt?"

"Maybe," Mira said.

She hated herself for the honesty and trusted it more than any softer answer.

Keeps-Water-Calm withdrew from the membrane. "The pool was built for exchange, not proof."

"I know."

"Do you?"

Mira had no safe answer. "Not enough."

Brenn closed his slate. "The court will not compel Orison participation or human exposure."

"Need pulls," Keeps-Water-Calm said.

"Yes."

"You remember."

"I do."

Sera stepped away from the wall. "I already did the dangerous human exposure. Use mine."

Mira turned. "No."

"Why not?"

"Because you are not a universal adapter."

"That is almost funny."

"It was not meant to be."

Brenn intervened before the argument became personal enough to wound. "A Treaty Pool protocol would require separate consent, medical review, Orison withdrawal power, nonuse limits, and a clear statement that sensory experience is not standing by itself."

Keeps-Water-Calm said, "Also the pool chooses some things you will not control."

Sera stared. "The pool chooses?"

"Bad human sentence. Useful warning."

Anika muttered, "I hate when the warning is useful."

Mira rested both hands on the rail. "If you refuse, we do not do it."

Keeps-Water-Calm's colors moved slowly. "If we refuse, humans may say nothing could be shown."

"Brenn's order can bar that inference."

"Orders leak."

"Yes."

For a long time only the tank pumps spoke.

Then Keeps-Water-Calm said, "We will discuss with boundary channels."

Mira let out a breath she had not meant to hold. "Thank you."

"Do not thank before knowing cost."

"I know that much."

"No," the Orison said. "You know the sentence."

The room accepted the rebuke because it had been earned by centuries, not just by Mira.

When the meeting ended, Brenn stayed behind to refine the definition. Tavo returned to platform duty. Anika signed off after making Brenn read back the oxygen remedy note. Sera lingered by the door, waiting until only Mira and the tank remained.

"You cannot be the person who asks everyone to risk themselves," Sera said.

"I know."

"No, you do not. You are getting good at asking because the case needs it."

Mira looked at her.

Sera's voice softened and became more dangerous. "Be careful. Need makes a very clean knife."

Then she left.

Mira stood alone on the human side of the treaty tank and looked through the membrane at water that did not belong to her, at a diplomat who had spent too long turning harm into speech humans might survive hearing.

Archive was a hungry word.

So was evidence.

So was help.

She added that to her private notes and did not put it in the report.


Chapter 24: The Treaty Pool

The Treaty Pool was older than Cael Station's court dome and wiser than its walls.

It had been carved into a warm brine chamber where natural mineral heat softened the boundary between ice shelf and ocean. Human engineers had added platforms, rails, sealable walkways, medical equipment, lighting, recorders, translation couplers, emergency drains, and enough disclaimers to choke any honest ritual. Orison participants had added nothing visible for the first year, then seeded the pool with organisms that altered water texture, chemical responsiveness, and acoustic return until human engineers realized the chamber had never been neutral and had only become less dishonest.

Mira had entered it twice in her career.

Both times she had left with a headache, revised assumptions, and the sense that her skin had been used as a poor translation device by a patient universe.

Now she stood on the upper platform with Brenn, Sera, Thane, a medical team, and two empty witness chairs. Below them, the pool glowed faint green-blue under low lights. Keeps-Water-Calm moved in the water near the far wall. Two Orison boundary channels held deeper positions, visible only as pressure shadows and intermittent color. Keeps-Embers-Low had not entered the pool but had sent red-gold symbionts to the outer exchange grates, a refusal and a guarantee at once.

The hearing record was open but limited. Board counsel objected to every limitation. Brenn had allowed the objections to accumulate in a side file titled, with uncharacteristic bite, PRESERVED WITHOUT INTERRUPTION.

"Participants," Brenn said, reading from the protocol order, "are here under voluntary and revocable participation. The court recognizes need pressure and material coercion as relevant constraints on consent. No sensory experience generated in this pool shall, standing alone, establish final legal status. No failure of Orison translation shall be construed as absence. No human distress shall be construed as proof of Orison aggression. No withdrawal shall be construed as concession."

Sera whispered, "He memorized the good parts."

Mira whispered back, "You helped write them."

"Yes, but I was angry. That is different."

The human participants had been argued over for three hours. Mira would participate because the protocol needed scientific continuity and because she would not ask others to enter a risk she avoided. Brenn would participate because he refused to adjudicate sensory evidence only through other people's bodies. Anika had demanded to participate and been denied on worker-coercion grounds; she had called that protection with a muzzle. Tavo had supported the denial and then let her yell at him in private, which Mira counted as one of his better leadership choices. Sera had been medically excluded and was still furious enough that the medical team had placed her far from the stairs.

Drex had not been invited.

Not yet.

"Medical baselines," Thane said.

Mira let the diagnostic arch scan her. Heart rate high. Blood pressure acceptable. Stress chemistry elevated. Human, under circumstances. Brenn's readings were worse; he looked at them with professional betrayal.

"Adjudicator," Sera said, "your body is objecting to law."

"It often does."

Keeps-Water-Calm surfaced near the central pool, though surfaced was a human word for a body that did not break water so much as alter its relation to air. "The pool will not show D-17. It will not make you Orison. It will not give you memory to own. It will place your bodies in a small relation shaped by continuing remembering and injury. Leave if you want proof more than accountability."

Brenn nodded. "Understood."

"No."

He paused. "Accepted as not understood."

"Better."

Mira removed her outer jacket and stepped down the first ramp. Warm vapor touched her face. The pool smelled of salt, iron, and something faintly sweet that no human analysis had ever usefully named. She wore a contact suit, thin enough to let pressure and temperature reach skin, sealed enough to keep her alive if her body reacted badly. Brenn descended the opposite ramp, moving with the care of someone who had discovered stairs were a jurisdictional hazard.

At the lower platform, Mira looked once at Sera.

Sera's jaw was tight. "Do not be noble."

"I will aim for competent."

"Aim lower if necessary. Survive."

Mira stepped into the pool.

Warm brine closed around her calves, knees, waist. The contact suit transmitted pressure in filtered bands. The first sensation was simply heat. Then buoyancy. Then the pool noticed her.

Not a mind. She refused the shortcut. The pool's organisms responded to salt, heat, movement, electrical field, chemical stress, the tremor in her muscles, the shape of her breathing. The water thickened by almost nothing. Her skin registered a slow pressure pattern at the wrists and ribs. Rise. Hold. Falling braid. Familiar now, but changed by the pool's living medium.

Brenn entered up to the chest and inhaled sharply.

"Medical?" Sera asked.

"Within tolerance," Thane said.

Keeps-Water-Calm moved between them, not touching either. "First relation: your bodies alter water. Water alters you. No one is observer alone."

Mira let the sentence work on her before translating it into method. No observer alone. The foundation every extractive report had denied.

The pool darkened.

Sound withdrew.

Cael Station did not vanish; it became irrelevant around the edges. Mira felt the water's warmth move through pressure cues into a pattern that approximated D-17's route relations without reproducing them. It was deliberately incomplete. She could feel the incompleteness as mercy: a missing weight, a space not taken from the reef. The pool carried the shape of continuing remembering as analogy made honest by admitting itself as analogy.

Then came injury.

Not pain. Pain would have been easier, dramatic, human-centered. Injury entered as lost possibility. A route that should have offered several pressures offered one. A chemical preference returned no answer. A future body's passage narrowed before the body existed. Mira felt her mind reach for grief and the pattern changed, not rejecting grief but making it insufficient. Grief belonged to the witness. The loss belonged to the relation.

Across the pool, Brenn made a sound.

Mira could not see him clearly through vapor and tears she had not decided to shed.

Keeps-Water-Calm's voice came through water and speaker together. "Do not close it."

Mira did not know whether the instruction was for her, Brenn, the court, or history.

The pool shifted again.

This time it offered the old chart.

Not visually. No glowing lines. No human map. Instead, widening. The sensation of caution made spatial: room left around a thing not understood, not as emptiness but as respect for what might answer later. Then narrowing: not a line moving on a display, but possibilities being declared irrelevant by systems that could not sense them. A route no longer asked about. A relation converted into low confidence. A living uncertainty made administratively quiet.

Brenn raised his left hand.

Sera's voice cut in. "Pause."

The pool did not stop instantly. Living systems rarely obeyed buttons without cost. But Keeps-Water-Calm moved, the pressure pattern eased, and the water returned to warmth.

"Adjudicator?" Thane asked.

Brenn's voice was rough. "Not abort. Marking classification impulse."

"State impulse," Sera said.

"To convert experience into finding too quickly."

Mira almost laughed and almost sobbed. Sera said, softer, "Good catch."

The protocol resumed.

The third movement was boundary.

Red-gold pressure from outside the pool. Blue restraint at the moonpool rim. Workers standing in no-motion zones. Empty hands. Foam in a machine elbow. Continuing remembering did not become sentimental unity; it held differences in tension. Human labor was not Orison ancestry. Orison boundary was not human worker debt. The reef's injury did not erase worker harm. Worker harm did not justify reef injury. The pool refused the moral convenience of making everything the same kind of suffering. It made relations without merging them.

Mira understood, with a clarity that hurt, why help was a hungry word.

Helping could become taking if it demanded gratitude.

Protection could become ownership if it demanded obedience.

Evidence could become extraction if it took experience and left obligation behind.

Then the pool began to pull away.

Not suddenly. It released them by degrees, leaving heat, salt, breath, the ache of muscles held too tight. Mira found herself standing chest-deep in water, one hand gripping the rail hard enough to hurt. Brenn stood opposite, eyes closed, face wet with pool water and perhaps not only pool water.

Keeps-Water-Calm floated between them.

"The pool withdraws," the Orison said. "Not absence."

Brenn opened his eyes. "Withdrawal as boundary."

"Yes."

Sera called from above, "Medical stable. Both participants within tolerance. Mira, unclench your hand before you damage the rail and make this legally weirder."

Mira let go.

They exited slowly. Medical wrapped both in heated blankets. Sera came to Mira first, scanned the monitors, looked at her face, and apparently decided against every sharp sentence she had prepared.

"Current?" she asked.

"Current," Mira said.

Brenn sat on the bench and stared at his hands.

"Adjudicator?" Thane asked.

He looked up. "Give me three minutes before anyone asks for law."

Keeps-Water-Calm's translator clicked. "Good."

No one spoke for three minutes. That was perhaps the most disciplined act of the day.

When Brenn stood, he did not look enlightened. Mira trusted that. Enlightenment was too often a human word for having felt something and wanting credit. He looked burdened in a more accurate direction.

"For the record," he said, "the Treaty Pool protocol did not show me an archive, message, person, grave, or mineral resource. It demonstrated, within acknowledged limits, that the D-17 relation cannot be experienced without also experiencing the inadequacy of those categories. It also demonstrated that injury to continuing remembering is not reducible to loss of biomass or stored information. This is not final standing. It is sufficient sensory corroboration for emergency injunction analysis."

Brenn stopped, swallowed, and added, "And no failure of words in that statement should be treated as failure of the relation."

Keeps-Water-Calm drifted near the surface. "Cracked vessel."

"Yes," Brenn said.

Sera looked at Mira. "That might hold some water."

Mira pulled the blanket tighter around her shoulders. She was shaking now, late and inconvenient. "Maybe enough."

Anika's voice came through the worker feed. She had clearly been listening despite being denied participation. "Does enough stop the board?"

Brenn turned toward the camera. "It gives me the basis to try."

"Honest answer," Anika said.

Sera, Mira, Tavo over the platform feed, and even Brenn all said, "Horrible trend."

The laughter that followed was small and exhausted and did not dishonor the room.

Outside the Treaty Pool, red-gold symbionts remained at the exchange grates. Blue symbionts held the moonpool rim. D-17 waited below glass, not passive, not safe, not yet protected in any way that could be trusted. But the court had now entered a relation it could no longer pretend was only reported by others.

That did not make law wise.

It made ignorance harder to file.

For the moment, that was the work.


Chapter 25: Worker Fault

The stabilizer failed because Tavo made it fail honestly.

That was the sentence he kept returning to while lying on his back beneath the secondary pylon control rack with one shoulder wedged against a coolant conduit and his left hand numb from holding a diagnostic bridge too long. Honest failure. It sounded like a joke a tired engineer told before court. It was not sabotage, not exactly. It was not compliance either. It was the machine being forced to admit, in the only language command software respected, that it had been unsafe before anyone decided to make safety politically inconvenient.

The secondary pylon stabilizer had been drifting for nine days.

Tavo had logged it as watch condition because that was what crew chiefs did when every repair budget was already bleeding and every system on a platform lived inside a hierarchy of not-yet-fatal. The drift was small. Within tolerances. Ugly end, but within. The same ugly end where they had kept the bore arm elbow before the test cut. The same ugly end where people who needed work learned to stand until the floor gave way.

This time, he did not let the machine hide there.

He routed the diagnostic bridge across the stabilizer's old analog sensor path, bypassed the smoothing filter, and instructed the system to report raw oscillation to court record, worker safety, and command at once. He did not introduce oscillation. He removed the lie that made it look harmless. The stabilizer saw itself without makeup and screamed.

Alarm cascaded through Drex Platform.

Pylon Two stabilization: unsafe variance.

Pylon balance: degraded.

Bore deck heavy-system readiness: locked.

Exterior motion: barred pending manual inspection.

No one could move the platform's heavy tools with that alarm active. No board counsel could call it relation poetry. No vendor escrow could thread around it as readiness preservation. The stabilizer had become a fact.

Tavo closed his eyes for one second under the rack.

Then Security Lead Pell said from above, "Chief."

He opened them.

Her boots were visible beside the rack. So were two other pairs. Security. Not maintenance. The platform had learned to make distinctions dangerous again.

"Come out slowly," Pell said.

Tavo slid the diagnostic bridge free, capped the port, and pushed himself out from under the rack. His shoulder protested. His hand tingled as blood returned. Pell stood over him with her jaw set and her sidearm sealed in its holster. Two security officers stood behind her, both looking as if they would rather fight a pump fire.

"You logging this?" Tavo asked.

"Yes."

"Good."

"Do not make this harder."

"Pell, I am lying on the floor. Harder would require ambition."

Her mouth did not move. "Director's authority is suspended outside D-17 operational matters. Interim corporate security order identifies unauthorized diagnostic manipulation affecting platform value."

"Unauthorized by whom?"

"Board security counsel."

"Court order bars board action affecting D-17 systems."

"They argue stabilizer is platform safety, not D-17."

Tavo sat up. "The stabilizer locks bore readiness."

"Yes."

"So it affects D-17."

"Yes."

"And?"

Pell looked tired enough to be honest. "And the order says I detain the person alleged to have manipulated it until Brenn rules."

The alarm continued overhead. It had a pure, irritating tone. Tavo loved it with part of himself he did not trust.

"Alleged," he said.

"Tavo."

He stood slowly. Workers gathered at corridor edges. Anika pushed through them with murder in her eyes and a wrench in one hand.

Pell saw the wrench. "Voss."

Anika lifted it. "Maintenance."

"Put it down."

"It is my emotional support tool."

"Put it down before I have to become stupid."

Anika looked at Tavo.

He nodded once.

She set the wrench on the floor with exaggerated care. "This is garbage."

"Yes," Pell said.

That stopped her more than argument would have.

Tavo held out his hands. "No restraints unless you enjoy paperwork."

Pell did not restrain him. She did take his tool cuff and seal his console access. That hurt more than cuffs would have. A crew chief without tools was a title waiting to be mocked.

The worker channel erupted. Jae demanded the raw logs. Ren asked whether this triggered debt breach. Medical asked whether anyone had been injured. Drex called from wherever the board had functionally exiled him inside his own platform. Brenn's court channel opened six seconds later. Sera joined three seconds after that and said something that began with "Absolutely" and ended with a word the court recorder flagged for review.

Pell escorted Tavo to the maintenance bay, not the brig. Drex Platform did not have a brig because it had always considered that a sign of bad labor relations. Instead it had a secure inventory office with a locking hatch and no chairs designed for comfort. Tavo sat on a crate of gasket seals while the whole platform argued around him through speakers.

Brenn appeared on the wall feed. "Crew Chief Rusk, state what you did."

"Bypassed smoothing filter on secondary pylon stabilizer diagnostic and routed raw variance to mandatory channels."

"Did you introduce false data?"

"No."

"Did you alter the stabilizer's physical condition?"

"No."

"Did you intend to prevent heavy-system readiness?"

"Yes."

Anika, visible over the worker feed from the corridor, hissed, "Tavo."

He ignored her. Lies were useful until they became structural. Then they killed people.

Brenn's expression remained neutral. "Why?"

"Because the stabilizer variance was real, because heavy-system readiness near D-17 is barred and unsafe, because board counsel was routing around restrictions, because workers and Orison boundary bodies were being forced to serve as last-resort brakes, and because the system's smoothing filter hid a risk that should have locked readiness earlier."

Sera's voice came from Cael Station. "I have the raw stream. He's right. The variance predates the diagnostic bridge."

Board counsel Quell appeared as text first, then forced live audio by emergency petition. "The admitted intent to prevent readiness establishes sabotage regardless of data accuracy."

Anika shouted from the corridor, "The machine was bad!"

Quell ignored her. "Crew Chief Rusk deliberately manipulated reporting pathways to create operational lock and impair asset value."

Tavo said, "I manipulated reporting pathways to stop the asset from pretending it was safe."

"Your subjective motive is irrelevant."

Brenn said, "It is not."

"Adjudicator, you cannot bless worker interference as safety whenever it aligns with your desired outcome."

"No. But I can distinguish falsification from removal of suppression filters."

Quell's voice sharpened. "The distinction is technical hair-splitting."

Sera laughed once. "Welcome to engineering."

Brenn muted her for five seconds. Everyone saw him do it. Sera's silent outrage filled the corner of the feed.

Mira entered the channel from Cael Station, hair damp from the Treaty Pool and face pale with fatigue. "Yul, the stabilizer variance couples into pylon vibration. If heavy-system readiness reinitiates, the variance could propagate into the blue symbiont contact zone. Tavo's route exposed a real D-17-adjacent risk."

"Confirmed," Sera said when unmuted.

Quell said, "Convenient."

Mira's voice went cold. "Yes. Reality is inconvenient when it stops a machine."

Pell stood by the office hatch, arms folded, expression unreadable.

Brenn looked at her. "Security Lead Pell, why was Rusk detained in maintenance rather than removed to corporate holding?"

Pell straightened. "Platform has no corporate holding."

"And if it did?"

"I would have refused until local court ruled."

"Reason?"

"No violence. No false data established. Active safety dispute. Crew stability risk."

Quell said, "Security insubordination is becoming epidemic."

Pell looked at the wall pickup. "No, counsel. It is becoming documented."

Anika whooped in the corridor. Someone else clapped once and stopped.

Brenn entered another note. "Crew Chief Rusk remains temporarily relieved of tool access pending review, not arrested. Detention is converted to safety hold. Security will not transfer him to board authority. Raw stabilizer variance is entered into court record. Heavy-system readiness remains locked pending independent inspection."

Quell began to object.

Brenn spoke over her. "Object in writing."

The live audio cut.

Tavo realized he had been holding his breath. He let it out slowly.

Anika pushed into the office before Pell could stop her. "You idiot."

"Which part?"

"The honest confession part."

"It was true."

"Truth is not a suicide pact."

"No. It is a maintenance schedule. Ignore it long enough, it becomes one."

She glared at him, then turned away because her eyes had gone wet and she hated that in public. "I am still mad."

"Good."

Mira's voice softened over the feed. "Tavo, why not tell us before doing it?"

He looked at the sealed tool cuff on the table. "Because if I asked, it became everyone's decision. It needed to be my fault."

Anika turned back. "That is heroic nonsense."

"No. It is chain of custody."

Sera said, "Unfortunately, that is the kind of emotionally repressed answer that may hold up."

Tavo almost smiled.

Brenn did not. "Fault is not ownership of the whole event. The court will not allow you to become a convenient single cause."

"I did it."

"Yes. And the platform drift existed. The smoothing filter existed. Board pressure existed. D-17 injury existed. Worker coercion existed. Orison boundary existed. Fault can be real without being solitary."

Tavo looked at him. That sentence found a place he had not known was waiting for it.

Pell unsealed the office hatch but kept his tool access locked. "Safety hold. You can remain here or sit in the galley under observation."

"Galley," Anika said. "If you sit alone in here getting noble, I will make it worse."

"How?"

"Creatively."

Tavo stood.

As he entered the corridor, workers made way. No applause. No cheers. This was not that kind of act. But Ren touched two fingers to his own tool cuff. Jae nodded from the crane lock feed. A pump tech muttered, "Raw data, Chief," like a blessing.

Tavo did not feel brave. He felt tired and partially foolish and relieved that the stabilizer alarm still screamed overhead.

Worker fault, the board would call it.

Maybe they were right, in the narrow way dangerous people loved.

A worker had forced the machine to tell the truth.

If that was fault, Tavo could live under the charge for one more day.


Chapter 26: Chorus in the Pipes

The station pipes began repeating Sera's imprint pattern while she was asleep.

This was rude on several levels.

Sera had finally surrendered to Medical after Tavo's safety hold converted from arrest to review, mostly because Mira stood in the doorway and said nothing until surrender became less exhausting than resistance. The bed in Cael Station Medical had the softness of a bureaucratic apology. Sera hated it for three minutes and then fell into a sleep so abrupt it felt less like rest than deletion.

She woke to the walls remembering for her.

Not sound at first. Taste.

Cold copper, brine, pressure behind the teeth.

Her eyes opened before she knew where she was. Medical ceiling. Rust stain near the air return. Diagnostic band on her wrist. Blanket. Her own hands. Present facts. Good. Then the wall pulsed.

Rise. Hold. Falling braid.

Then the route.

Sera sat up too fast and nearly vomited.

The medical monitor objected. A nurse looked over from the supply cabinet. "Kint?"

"Water systems," Sera said. Her voice scraped. "Call Mira. Now."

The nurse did not ask why. Pelagos had improved everyone.

By the time Mira arrived, still wearing the same clothes from the Treaty Pool, the pattern had moved through three station systems: Medical bath supply, hydroponics mist loop, and the treaty tank's outer circulation. Sera sat on the edge of the bed with a basin in her lap and a sensor slate in one hand because she had threatened to stand if they did not give her something useful.

"You should be lying down," Mira said.

"The pipes are doing my imprint."

"You should be sitting exactly as you are."

"Thank you."

Mira looked at the slate. The pattern repeated across water-linked systems, not identical but unmistakable to anyone who had watched Sera's protocol logs: shutdown return, route orientation, old boundary widening, later narrowing, the pressure equivalent of a path closed by a map. It was weaker than the Treaty Pool exposure and stronger than the first station pipe event. It also carried a new layer: the stabilizer alarm's raw variance, folded into the relation like a machine finally confessing.

"Is this live from D-17?" Mira asked.

Sera shook her head carefully. "Do not know. It may be station water carrying residual protocol plus new pylon variance. It may be D-17 responding through established pathways. It may be both, because why would anything be one thing."

The wall pulsed again.

The nurse whispered a word in a language Sera did not know.

Mira opened the station channel. "Thane, status."

The administrator answered immediately. "Reports from hydroponics, Medical, lower brine exchange, crew showers, and one coffee dispenser."

Sera blinked. "Coffee?"

"Apparently bitterly."

"That is not funny."

"No one laughed," Thane said. "Pressure stable. Chemistry stable except for trace Orison-compatible signaling compounds in outer loops. No contamination markers. People are frightened. Again."

Brenn joined from the legal archive. "Is this hazardous?"

Mira looked at Sera.

Sera swallowed. The copper taste had faded but left memory like a fingerprint. "Not physically at current levels. Psychologically, anyone exposed to the Treaty Pool or prior imprint protocol may experience sensory echo. Anyone else may hear or feel pressure pulses. Do not isolate water abruptly."

"Why?" Thane asked.

"Because abrupt isolation might turn a relation event into a distress event. Also because I do not know, and this moon punishes confident plumbing."

Thane said, "I will put that in administrator phrasing."

Keeps-Water-Calm entered the channel. Their voice sounded strained. "The station carries what humans tried to carry away."

Mira closed her eyes. "Did we cause harm?"

"Yes."

Sera put the slate down.

Keeps-Water-Calm continued, "And the relation answers through the harm. Do not make one word do both."

Brenn asked, "What action is required?"

"Listen without chasing," the Orison said. "Do not force repeat. Do not silence by panic. Do not call it proof alone. Do not call it attack."

Sera laughed weakly. "Our entire operating manual is becoming a list of what not to call things."

"Progress," Keeps-Water-Calm said.

The pattern moved through Medical again. This time Sera did not resist the route sensation. She let it pass as event, not ownership. Cold under ice, widened margin, narrowed map, stabilizer truth. Then something else: worker bodies in no-motion zones, blue symbionts around a tool, red-gold boundary held below station, Brenn pausing before law, Mira gripping the Treaty Pool rail. The station water was not replaying Sera. It was incorporating human attempts to carry relation and showing the cost of carrying badly.

Her eyes filled with tears, which annoyed her.

Mira saw. "Abort?"

"There is no harness."

"Medical sedation?"

"No. If I sleep through this, I will spend the rest of my career pretending data happened elsewhere."

"That is not a medical reason."

"It is a Sera reason."

Mira looked at the nurse. "Monitor closely."

The nurse nodded.

Brenn opened a public station channel, voice controlled. "Cael Station personnel, this is Adjudicator Brenn. We are experiencing a non-damaging water-system relation event connected to ongoing D-17 proceedings. There is no current pressure or contamination hazard. Do not flush, strike, chemically isolate, or tamper with water systems. Report symptoms to Medical. Do not interpret the event publicly as attack, consent, proof, or settlement. We will provide updates."

Thane followed. "This is Administrator Thane. Listen to that. Also stop gathering in corridors near pipes. You are blocking safety teams."

Anika's voice came from Drex Platform, patched through by worker channel. "We are getting it too."

Mira straightened. "Platform systems?"

"Suit wash, coolant monitor, moonpool rim. Not in drinking water. Blue symbionts brighter. Red-gold still holding."

Tavo, still under safety hold but apparently in the galley, added, "Stabilizer alarm changed. Same variance, lower amplitude."

Sera grabbed the slate again. "Send me logs."

Mira said, "You are in Medical."

"So is the slate."

The logs arrived. Sera layered platform pylon data over station pipe patterns and felt the room tilt in a purely intellectual way. "The raw stabilizer variance is being damped."

"By what?" Brenn asked.

"Blue symbiont contact along the moonpool rim and pylon skin, maybe. Or the platform is settling after coolant shutdown. Or both."

Keeps-Embers-Low entered with a red-gold pulse. "Blue ones hold tool quiet. Red ones hold boundary. Water carries worker fault into ancestor hearing."

Tavo said, "Worker fault?"

"Your word was used against you," Keeps-Embers-Low replied. "We return it altered."

The galley feed showed Tavo sitting very still.

Sera whispered, "Damn."

Brenn said, "Can this be recorded reliably?"

"Already recording," Sera and Thane said at once.

"Can it be repeated?"

Keeps-Water-Calm answered before Sera could. "Do not ask repetition from a living response as if it were a machine performing credibility."

Brenn exhaled. "I know. I also need evidentiary durability."

"Then record occurrence, conditions, non-coercion, withdrawal, effects, and failure of forced repeatability."

Sera stared at the speaker. "That is... actually a protocol."

"Yes."

"You could have said that earlier."

"You were asleep."

"Uncalled for."

The pattern reached the coffee dispenser again. Somewhere down the corridor, a maintenance tech yelped, "It made the good one bitter!"

Thane's channel clicked. "Everyone leave the coffee alone."

For some reason, that was what steadied the station. People could handle cosmic legal-ecological terror if someone told them specifically not to touch the coffee machine.

Mira sat beside Sera's bed. "What are you feeling now?"

"Embarrassed that my imprint became plumbing."

"Sera."

She breathed in, out. "Less invaded than before. More... implicated. The pattern is not me. It includes my contact because my contact was part of the relation. That is different from being used."

"Good."

"Maybe. Also horrible."

"Both."

Brenn's voice came through the legal channel. "The event strengthens injunction basis."

Keeps-Water-Calm said, "And increases obligation."

"Yes."

"Say both in order."

"I will."

The chorus in the pipes lasted forty-two minutes.

It moved from Medical to hydroponics, through the treaty tank, across lower brine exchange, into Drex Platform's moonpool rim, and back through Cael Station's water systems in fading passes. It never breached pressure safety. It never produced chemical harm. It frightened three technicians into nausea, caused one hydroponics worker to cry over lettuce, made the coffee briefly undrinkable, and left behind a dataset no board counsel could easily call subjective because too many machines had been unwilling witnesses.

When it faded, silence returned in layers.

Sera lay back at last, exhausted beyond argument.

Mira adjusted the blanket. "Now sleep."

"If the pipes start litigating, wake me."

"I will handle preliminary objections."

"You are not funny."

"Sleep."

Sera closed her eyes. "Mira?"

"Yes?"

"Do not let them make it mine."

Mira's hand paused on the blanket.

"I will not."

"It passed through me. It is not mine."

"I know."

"Say it in the order."

Brenn's voice came from the doorway. He had arrived without either of them noticing, hardcase in hand, face drawn and intent. "I will."

Sera opened one eye. "You look awful."

"I am told this is how adjudicators are made."

"No. Adjudicators get objections. You get electrolyte."

Mira laughed before she could stop herself.

The sound was small and human and badly timed. It did not fix anything. It did not dishonor anything either.

Outside Medical, the station pipes cooled. Below the ice, D-17 continued. In the record, the chorus became another cracked vessel: not proof alone, not attack, not consent, not archive, not message, not Sera, not human.

A living response had passed through human systems and left them with less room to lie.

For now, that had to be enough.


Chapter 27: Brenn's Refusal

Brenn refused the evidence at 05:40, after reading every line of it twice.

He did not refuse the event. He did not refuse the injury, the old chart, the Treaty Pool, the chorus in the pipes, the symbiont restraint, the stabilizer fault, or the worker record. Those remained in the docket like stones no one could pretend had not been thrown into water. What he refused was Mira's proposed evidentiary phrase:

Sensory experience entered as direct corroboration of continuing remembering.

He crossed it out with a stylus so hard the slate clicked.

Mira was too tired to be surprised and too angry to be calm. They were in Breakwater Court again. The dome beyond the transparent wall showed dark water and drifting flecks of life. Sera sat beside Mira under medical supervision she had negotiated down to "visible to a nurse who can sprint." Tavo attended from Drex Platform's galley because his tool access remained locked. Anika was there too, unofficially visible over his shoulder. Drex attended from the command deck but spoke rarely. Keeps-Water-Calm floated in the treaty feed. Keeps-Embers-Low remained a red-gold pressure channel below the station.

"You cannot cross out the thing the pool demonstrated," Mira said.

"I can refuse to enter it in a form that will collapse under challenge," Brenn replied.

"It will not collapse because it is true."

"Truth is not self-formatting."

Sera said, "That sentence should be punishable."

Brenn ignored her. "The Treaty Pool experience, Sera's imprint, and the pipe chorus are admissible as observed events, physiological and environmental responses, and method-limited sensory protocols. They are not admissible as direct proof of continuing remembering unless repeatability standards can be stated."

Keeps-Water-Calm's feed darkened. "You ask the living relation to behave as tool."

"I ask the order to survive appeal."

"Appeal is far. Harm is near."

"Yes."

"Then why serve far?"

For the first time that morning, Brenn's composure cracked. Not loudly. His hand stilled. His face went still enough to show the effort underneath.

"Because far arrives with enforcement," he said. "If I write an order that cannot survive far, near loses what little shield I can make."

Anika leaned into the platform camera. "And if near dies while you polish the shield?"

"Then I have failed."

No one spoke after that because the answer had not protected him.

Mira looked down at the crossed-out line. She understood him. That made her angrier. If he were timid, she could push. If he were corrupt, she could expose. But he was trying to build something that would hold, and the material kept splintering between urgency and durability.

"What repeatability standard would satisfy you?" she asked.

"Not identical repetition. That would be wrong."

Sera blinked. "Progress."

"I do occasionally listen. I need independent mapping of the relation using a method not designed by Cael Station, not controlled by Drex Platform, not mediated only through Orison diplomatic channels, and not dependent on one human imprint participant."

Drex spoke. "Independent from everyone in the room."

"As much as possible."

"Convenient," Quell's text objection flashed, still read-only. "Impossible standard."

Brenn opened it this time, read it aloud, and said, "For once, counsel identifies the difficulty without improving it."

Keeps-Water-Calm moved in the tank. "You want a third shore."

"Yes."

"There may be one."

Mira turned to the feed. "Who?"

"Choral Nomads carry acoustic maps outside Compact sequence."

Sera straightened. "Independent cartographers?"

"Not independent. Differently entangled."

"Of course."

Keeps-Water-Calm continued. "Nadi Or is near Pelagos. Their ship maps songs of ice routes, migration pressure, and treaty hazards. They do not serve Drex. They do not serve Cael Station. They trade with Orison channels and human stations and make enemies by correcting both."

Brenn's stylus hovered. "Can they arrive before the injunction hearing?"

"Perhaps."

Mira looked at the schedule. The next Choral Nomad courier window had been discussed for legal packet transfer, but she had assumed it meant a buoy, not a ship close enough to matter. "Why did you not mention them before?"

"Because calling outside witness changes relation."

"Everything changes relation."

"Yes. Some changes are doors. Some are cuts."

Sera exhaled. "Will Nadi Or come?"

Keeps-Water-Calm's colors shifted. "If asked correctly and paid in something they respect."

Drex said, "Money?"

"Sometimes. Not first."

Anika said, "Of course the one useful outsider does not want money first."

"What do they want?" Brenn asked.

"Access to unedited acoustic record, permission to map without Compact filters, Orison right to mark harm in their output, and human promise not to own the map."

Quell's objection window lit before Brenn could respond.

Sera read it. "Board counsel hates all of that. Strong recommendation."

Brenn leaned back. "An independently held map that no party owns may be hard to admit."

"But harder to bury," Mira said.

"Yes."

The room shifted again, toward possibility and its costs.

Brenn opened a new order draft. "The court will invite Nadi Or as external acoustic cartographer under limited witness authority."

Keeps-Water-Calm interrupted. "Do not invite as court tool."

Brenn stopped. "Then how?"

"Request witness as relation bearer. Court receives, does not command."

"I cannot write relation bearer as a legal role."

"You can write invited nonparty cartographic witness."

"I can."

"And know it is smaller."

"I will."

Mira watched him type. His refusal had not ended the case. It had forced it outward. That was not comfort, but it was motion. Evidence needed another witness. The reef needed not to be translated solely through the people already implicated by harm.

Sera said quietly, "I hate that he might be right."

Mira looked at her. "About what?"

"About not making my imprint carry too much. About not making the pool experience direct proof. About needing another map."

"I hate that too."

"Good."

The order took shape. It preserved the sensory events but limited their evidentiary use. It refused direct proof language. It named the need for independent acoustic corroboration. It requested Nadi Or. It barred parties from interfering with the mapping. It required Drex and Cael Station to provide raw records. It required Orison channels to state withdrawal or participation terms. It stated, at Keeps-Water-Calm's insistence, that no map would constitute ownership of what it described.

Brenn signed.

Legal silence did not follow this time.

The station began moving.

Thane called traffic control. Sera assembled raw acoustic packets with a possessive fury she denied. Mira drafted a scientific cover note that said as little as possible and disclosed as much as necessary. Drex forwarded platform logs and board objections, making Quell visible in the packet because he had apparently developed a taste for inconvenient transparency. Tavo sent stabilizer raw data under worker witness seal. Anika sent one line:

If your map makes workers invisible, I will know.

Brenn looked at it. "Can we include that?"

"You had better," she said.

He did.

The request left Cael Station through an acoustic burst, a tight-beam packet through the melt-bore relay, and an Orison pressure channel Mira did not pretend to understand. It traveled into Pelagos's local traffic web, toward a ship or habitat or moving archive of sound called by a person who might help or might refuse.

After it sent, Brenn sat down heavily.

Mira remained standing across the table.

"I am still angry," she said.

"Good."

"Not rhetorically. Personally."

"Also good."

"Do not approve my anger."

"I am not approving it. I am noting that if no one is angry at an evidentiary limit, the limit is probably hiding who it hurts."

Sera closed her eyes. "Stop saying useful things. It disrupts the moral clarity."

Keeps-Water-Calm's translator clicked once.

Drex, on the command feed, said, "What if Nadi Or refuses?"

Brenn looked at the crossed-out line, then at the old charts, the pipe chorus logs, the Treaty Pool record, the worker fault alarm, the symbiont imagery. "Then I write with what we have and accept the weakness."

"And the board?"

"Attacks."

"And the reef?"

Brenn's face tightened. "Waits under a shield I do not yet trust."

Keeps-Embers-Low spoke from the red-gold channel. "Boundary does not wait forever."

"I know," Brenn said.

"No. You count."

He did not defend himself.

Mira looked through the court wall into water thick with motion too small to name. Brenn had refused her line. She still believed it was true. She also saw, with reluctant clarity, that if the line failed later, it would not fail alone. It would drag Sera's imprint, the Treaty Pool, and perhaps Orison willingness into the category of moving but legally insufficient experience. A bad victory could poison the next fight.

Sometimes refusal was cowardice.

Sometimes it was engineering.

The cruelty lay in having to decide before the structure was tested.


Chapter 28: Nadi Or Arrives

Nadi Or arrived with a ship that looked like an argument against straight lines.

It came up under Pelagos's glass shelf on six folding hydrofoils, a pressure hull, and a forest of acoustic vanes that unfurled through the water like black reeds. Human traffic control identified it as the courier-cartographer vessel Bell Without Metal. Drex Platform's old mineral software identified it as debris, then amended to unauthorized survey craft, then gave up when Sera fed it three corrective terms and one insult. Orison channels identified the ship in pressure pulses that made the station walls hum.

Keeps-Water-Calm translated the arrival simply:

"Nadi Or comes listening."

The ship docked at Cael Station's lower brine collar two hours after Brenn's request, which was either astonishing speed or evidence that Keeps-Water-Calm had known Nadi Or was closer than they had admitted. Mira did not ask. Some omissions were strategy. Some were boundary. Some were both.

Nadi Or entered through the wet lock wearing a pressure cloak, acoustic beadwork, and an expression of profound irritation at the existence of dry floors. They were human, mostly, or human-descended enough for Compact forms to stop asking impolite questions after the third generation of Choral Nomad adaptation. Their skin was deep brown, their hair shaved close except for a silver braid behind one ear, and their left cheek carried a line of implanted resonance studs that clicked faintly when station pumps changed frequency. They walked with a cane that was not for balance but for listening; its tip tapped the deck and returned small truths.

"Who is responsible for the phrase non-attacking defensive witness contact?" they asked before greeting anyone.

Brenn looked at Sera.

Sera pointed at Brenn.

Nadi Or stared at him. "It has the smell of court."

"It was accurate."

"Accuracy is not absolution."

Sera whispered to Mira, "I may love them."

Mira whispered back, "Dangerous."

Keeps-Water-Calm's translator clicked from the tank feed. "Yes."

Nadi Or turned toward the Orison feed and touched two fingers to the resonance studs on their cheek. "Keeps-Water-Calm. You are thinner in this room."

"Human rooms shave water from speech."

"Then we will not stay in it long."

Brenn stepped forward. "Nadi Or, I am Adjudicator Yul Brenn. Thank you for answering the court's request."

"I answered a relation disturbance. The court is a container along the way."

"Understood."

Nadi Or looked at him. "You say that like a person who has recently learned not to say understood."

Sera made a small triumphant noise.

Nadi Or's gaze moved to her. "Sera Kint."

Sera stiffened. "Yes."

"Your imprint file is loud."

"Sorry?"

"Do not apologize for being passed through. Apologize only if you turn passage into property."

Sera's face changed. "I am trying not to."

"I heard."

Mira understood then that Nadi Or had already reviewed more than the request packet. Choral Nomad listening networks moved in ways Compact law treated as rumor until it needed them, at which point it called them local expertise and hoped no one noticed the hypocrisy.

Drex attended by feed from the platform. Tavo beside him, tool access still restricted. Anika visible in the back. Nadi Or glanced at the screen.

"Drex Maritime," they said.

Drex inclined his head. "Director, procedurally disputed."

"Good. Certainty would be vulgar."

Anika laughed once. Drex accepted the hit with more grace than Mira expected.

Brenn opened the witness protocol. "Your conditions were received. The court can grant raw record access, nonexclusive map custody, public notation of Orison harm marks, and non-ownership language. Mapping must avoid active pulses that could affect D-17 without Orison consent."

Nadi Or tapped the cane twice. The resonance studs clicked. "I do not map by shouting at wounds."

Sera mouthed the sentence silently, perhaps for later use.

"What do you need?" Mira asked.

"Access to the Quiet Shelf, Drex pylon recordings, moonpool symbiont logs, Treaty Pool limited record, pipe chorus raw data, old chart layers, worker no-motion telemetry, and the unedited board objection stream."

Quell's text objection appeared immediately from read-only counsel access:

OBJECTION: BOARD COMMUNICATIONS ARE PRIVILEGED AND IRRELEVANT TO ACOUSTIC MAPPING.

Nadi Or read it. "Also that. It has rhythm."

Brenn said, "Board objection stream will be made available for governance context, not acoustic ecology, unless you can justify otherwise."

Nadi Or tapped the cane against the floor. "Human command is vibration in machines. Machine readiness is vibration in water. Water is relation. Your separations are polite fiction."

Brenn sighed. "I will mark that as justification."

"Good. I dislike having to repeat obvious things in dry rooms."

They left for the lower dock within twenty minutes. No one had time to build a procession, which improved the event. Nadi Or's crew deployed from Bell Without Metal: three humans or human variants, two Orison-adapted interface bodies, and a swarm of passive bead sensors that looked like dull seeds until they entered water and unfolded listening hairs. They brought no active sonar. No cutting tools. No mineral scanners. Their equipment seemed both old and unnervingly precise, built by people who trusted water to carry information if they stopped bullying it first.

Mira joined them on the dock with Sera and Brenn. Keeps-Water-Calm traveled below through brine channels. Nadi Or refused a court escort and accepted worker telemetry from Tavo with a nod that did not flatter.

"This is clean," Nadi Or said, reviewing the no-motion data.

Tavo's feed showed him blinking. "Thank you?"

"Not praise. Condition."

"I am learning not to clap."

"Good."

The first mapping pass began under Cael Station and moved west toward Drex Platform. Bell Without Metal glided beneath the ice, vanes open, bead sensors trailing in a cloud. Cael Station received the feed in Breakwater Court: not images at first, but layered acoustic fields. The old chart boundary appeared as pale contour. Current route braids emerged in green. D-17's injured relation pulsed blue-black. Platform vibration traces appeared in dull red. Worker no-motion telemetry, astonishingly, registered as a damped human pattern in the deck-water interface where symbionts had attended.

Nadi Or narrated while the ship moved.

"First-contact boundary was not crude. It was humble. It widened around uncertainty that later surveys could not hear because they narrowed their instruments before narrowing the map."

Brenn wrote. "Say again for record."

"I am always for record. Your record is merely slower."

"Please."

Nadi Or repeated it.

Sera leaned over the display. "Can you distinguish D-17 relation from generic reef resonance?"

"Yes."

"How?"

"Generic is a word used by people far from consequence. But yes: D-17 holds route maintenance signatures corresponding to old continuity margin, current Orison response, and injured lobe disturbance. It is not interchangeable carbonate-silicate architecture."

Mira felt the sentence open a door in her chest. Independent enough. Differently entangled, yes, but not theirs. Not Cael Station's. Not Drex's. Not only Keeps-Water-Calm's translation.

Drex's voice came from the platform feed. "Does the map show the test bore injury?"

Nadi Or did not soften. "Yes."

"Show me."

"You have seen enough to know."

"Show me anyway."

The display shifted. D-17's outer relation field appeared as layered green-blue. Near the test bore, a dark disruption cut across a route braid. It did not look like a wound because maps did not bleed. It looked like a silence shaped by force.

No one on the platform feed spoke.

Anika said at last, "That is what the tool did?"

Nadi Or answered, "That is one way the tool continues doing it."

Drex closed his eyes.

Brenn looked up sharply. "Continues?"

"Injury is not finished when motion stops. The relation reroutes, compensates, loses options. Some loss is active. Some can be repaired. Some becomes future difficulty."

Mira typed too fast. "Can you map repair paths?"

"With Orison consent and no board shouting."

Keeps-Embers-Low entered the channel. "Boundary permits repair map. Not extraction map."

Nadi Or touched their resonance studs. "Heard."

Quell objected again. Brenn did not bother opening it.

The second pass moved toward Drex Platform. Blue symbionts lined the moonpool rim. Red-gold boundary organisms held lower and farther out. Bell Without Metal did not approach closer than the interim boundary allowed. Its vanes folded partially, listening through platform noise, worker movement, disabled machinery, and the quiet pylon line.

Nadi Or said, "The tool is stopped. The tool's intention remains in governance channels. The water hears both."

Sera looked at Brenn. "Board objections have acoustic relevance."

Brenn's face said he hated that this was true. "Noted."

Tavo's voice came over the feed. "Does worker refusal remain in the relation?"

Nadi Or studied the map. "Faintly. Not as claim. As interruption of tool path."

Anika said, "Empty hands load case."

"Yes," Nadi Or said. "Better phrase."

Jae, somewhere off-screen, shouted, "Told you!"

The third pass crossed the old boundary edge. Here the map became most important. Nadi Or overlaid first-contact continuity margin, later narrowed boundaries, and current acoustic relation. The old wide line tracked living route uncertainty far better than any later map. The later maps had not refined. They had silenced.

Brenn stood very still.

"Can you certify this?" he asked.

"I can certify that the original continuity zone corresponds materially to current relation structures and that later exclusions cannot be justified by acoustic evidence available now or by the stated methodology of the narrowing surveys."

"Can you certify that D-17 requires protection from extraction?"

"That is a legal word wearing an ethical coat. I can certify extraction would further damage continuing remembering and that the claim boundary excludes relation in a way my mapping does not support."

Brenn nodded slowly. "That may be enough."

Keeps-Water-Calm spoke from below the ice. "Cracked vessel, stronger."

Nadi Or replied, "Still cracked."

"Yes."

"Good. Uncracked vessels are usually lies."

Sera whispered, "Definitely love them."

Mira did not hush her.

When Bell Without Metal returned to Cael Station, it carried a map no party owned. Nadi Or placed copies in three custodial streams: court record, Orison relation channel, and Choral Nomad public ledger. Drex received access but not control. Cael Station received access but not control. Board counsel received notice and objected so hard the docket briefly lagged.

Brenn read the certification aloud in Breakwater Court. Each sentence seemed to add weight to the floor.

Independent acoustic cartography confirms D-17's current relation structures materially correspond to first-contact continuity margins.

Later boundary narrowing substituted body-presence and mineral-confidence measures for relation review.

The test bore injury affects continuing remembering beyond biomass loss.

Further extraction or readiness activity risks irreversible relation damage.

Nadi Or stood dripping on the court floor and looked bored by the drama of being useful.

"Does this satisfy your repeatability problem?" Mira asked Brenn.

"It satisfies enough of it to let me write."

"The injunction?"

"Yes."

The word moved through every feed: court, station, platform, Orison channels, worker galley, board counsel's muted window, the water below.

Keeps-Embers-Low spoke one line:

"Seeing becomes stopping?"

Brenn looked at the map, then at the wounded relation field, then at the workers visible on the platform feed, then at Mira.

"If I do my job," he said.

Nadi Or tapped the cane once. "Then do it in ink that can survive distance."

Brenn opened a new order.

For the first time all day, no one interrupted the first sentence.


Chapter 29: Descent into Lattice

The submersible was named Patient Error, which Sera called either reassuring or slanderous depending on which warning light was blinking.

It sat in Cael Station's lower launch cradle like a seed with opinions: reef-safe hull, passive vanes, soft pressure skin, no active sonar, no cutting tools, no hard lights, no mineral scanner, no manipulator arm strong enough to pretend curiosity was harmless. Nadi Or had lent two acoustic bead arrays and then stood over the installation until the station techs stopped calling them sensors and started calling them witnesses. Keeps-Water-Calm had approved the route with six conditions, four refusals, and one sentence the translator rendered as: "If the vehicle tries to become a mouth, leave it behind."

Sera had stared at the submersible after that and said, "Do we know whether it has aspirations?"

Now she sat in the rear operator cradle, pale but medically cleared, checking the passive boards with hands that had steadied since the pipe chorus but not forgiven anyone for noticing. Mira took the forward science couch. Keeps-Water-Calm occupied the brine chamber integrated into the submersible's center, a transparent oval of warm water linked to the outer sea by controlled exchange pores. The Orison had refused to be passenger, sample, guide, or diplomatic cargo. The launch manifest listed them as participating relation witness.

Brenn had written the phrase himself and looked embarrassed by it.

The adjudicator stood outside the hatch now with his hardcase tucked under one arm as if law might leak out if he set it down. "Final conditions," he said.

Sera closed her eyes. "I can recite them in my sleep, which I am no longer allowed to have."

"No active pulses," Brenn said anyway. "No penetrating scans. No unscheduled contact with D-17 substrate. No sample retrieval. No data compression before review. Orison withdrawal terminates descent. Medical abort terminates descent. Nadi Or map anchor remains external. Drex Platform stays locked. Board counsel remains muted unless actual emergency law occurs."

"Define actual emergency law," Sera said.

"I will know it by the headache."

Mira checked her suit seals. "We are not going down for spectacle."

"I know," Brenn said.

"Do you?"

He accepted the question. "No. I know enough to worry about what I do not know."

Keeps-Water-Calm's translator clicked from inside the brine chamber. "Better."

Nadi Or leaned on their listening cane near the launch rail. Their ship Bell Without Metal waited outside the station, vanes folded in the dark. "Follow the route braid. Do not chase bright returns. Brightness is often panic in a pretty coat."

Sera looked up. "That is useful and rude."

"Most useful things are."

Anika's voice came from Drex Platform feed. "You all coming back?"

"Yes," Mira said.

The word felt like theft from the future.

Anika heard it. "Try to make that true."

Tavo's voice followed, quieter. "Bore systems remain disabled. Worker vigil holds. Tool stays asleep."

Drex did not speak, but his feed showed him at the command deck, no longer in the chair, standing behind Tavo with removal notices scrolling past him like weather he had chosen not to obey.

The hatch sealed.

Patient Error dropped.

The launch cradle released them into a vertical sleeve of warm water beneath Cael Station. For the first twenty meters, human structure surrounded them: guide ribs, service lights dimmed to reef-safe levels, emergency tether, launch markers, the underside of a habitat built by beings who liked boundaries sealed with bolts. Then the sleeve opened into Pelagos's ocean, and every human line became conditional.

Dark water took them.

The submersible did not hum. Its passive vanes adjusted by slow flexion. Its ballast changed with soft chemical bladders. Its displays showed what could be known without shouting: pressure, temperature, ambient vibration, chemical gradients, living-contact probability, Nadi Or's route overlay, pylon distance, D-17 relation field. Without active scan, the world did not appear in crisp heroic outline. It emerged reluctantly, through differences and delays.

Mira preferred that. Crispness had done enough harm.

"Descent rate stable," Sera said. "External acoustic field quiet-ish. Station noise fading. Platform pylon line at low amplitude. Blue symbiont pattern present. Red-gold boundary farther north."

"D-17?" Mira asked.

"There, but not a there in the usual way."

Keeps-Water-Calm floated in the brine chamber, body elongated, attendants close. "Good. Do not make first sight into possession."

The route braid appeared as a green shimmer on the forward display, not because it was green but because Sera had to pick a color and had chosen one that did not look like blood, money, or board warning. It curved beneath the ice shelf and down toward the pale branching shadow of the reef. Nadi Or's map marked areas of relation density, injury distortion, and silence-with-cause.

Mira looked for D-17 and saw instead water choosing paths through mineral and living pore.

The reef rose from below.

From orbit it had looked like frozen lightning. From the Quiet Shelf, like pale architecture. From the submersible, entering the outer lattice, it became impossible to hold in one metaphor. Ribs of carbonate-silicate scaffold curved through darkness, thick as station corridors in places, thin as fingers in others. Living film shimmered across them in translucent sheets. Pores opened and closed with pressure changes. Small bodies moved through channels, some Orison, some not, some perhaps not separable from the lattice except by human impatience. Blue-black threads traced older routes through the structure. Chemical clouds drifted like weather that remembered where it had been needed.

Sera whispered, "Oh."

No one mocked the word.

Patient Error slowed at the first threshold. The route braid narrowed through a field of pale fans that folded away from the submersible's pressure skin before contact. Mira's board registered living response, no distress spike. Keeps-Water-Calm extended filaments into the brine exchange pore. The submersible's water changed. Not dangerously. Intentionally.

"We ask passage," the Orison said.

The lattice answered without voice.

At least, that was how the human part of Mira wanted to phrase it. The boards showed pressure equalization, chemical relaxation, a lowering of micro-current turbulence ahead. The reef did not open a gate. It ceased refusing a path.

"Passage condition partial," Sera said, voice professional with effort. "No contact. No distress. Route permits one-body equivalent plus soft hull. That phrase is terrible. Logging anyway."

They entered.

The outer lattice swallowed scale. The submersible drifted through channels that looked too narrow until they became sufficient, past living surfaces that retreated by millimeters and returned after they passed. Mira felt the contact suit translate water pressure through the couch. Not direct imprint. Not the Treaty Pool. A careful, distant participation. She sensed movement not as map but as permission continuously renegotiated.

"This is not interior," Keeps-Water-Calm said.

Mira looked at the brine chamber. "We are inside the lattice."

"Inside is a human victory word. Say among."

Sera typed something. "Correcting route log: descent among lattice."

"You are learning," the Orison said.

"Under protest."

"Most learning."

They reached the injury shadow twenty minutes later.

Nadi Or's map had shown the test bore damage as a dark disruption. In the lattice, it appeared as absence in motion. A route braid approached the affected lobe and thinned. Small carrier organisms gathered near the disturbed area and dispersed, gathered and dispersed, unable to reestablish a pattern that had once required no deliberation. The living film on one scaffold section had darkened to bruised blue. It was not dead. It was repeating repair attempts and losing some of them.

Mira's hands curled around the couch grips. "Can we help?"

Keeps-Water-Calm's body tightened. "Bad first question."

She closed her eyes. Breathed. "What should we witness?"

"Better."

Sera's voice was small. "The cut changed the route."

"Yes," Keeps-Water-Calm said.

"Not removed. Made more expensive."

"Yes."

"For whom?"

"Those not yet here."

The answer moved through the submersible like cold.

Mira watched the damaged route braid. Future bodies, future passages, future choices narrowed by a micro-bore that had lasted seconds and by legal categories that had taken decades to become sharp. She thought of Anika's nephew, oxygen purchased by absence. Debt and reef injury were not the same. The lattice made that clearer, not less. Harm could rhyme without becoming interchangeable.

Patient Error drifted lower.

The channel opened into a chamber of porous columns. Water moved vertically here, drawn by thermal gradients from deeper vents. The columns pulsed with slow acoustic response. Sera's display filled with layered returns she did not command. The submersible was being listened around, not scanned.

"We are at the memory lattice edge," Sera said, then winced. "Continuing remembering lattice edge?"

Keeps-Water-Calm said, "All words crack. That one cracks toward better."

Mira smiled despite everything.

At the chamber's center, a pale sheet of living film stretched between two columns, threaded with blue-white points. It did not resemble a screen, organ, archive, or brain. It resembled a negotiation among materials: mineral holding shape, film holding chemistry, water holding pressure, organisms tending the difference. Around it moved small Orison bodies, or reef-associated bodies, or ancestor-maintenance bodies. Mira could not name them cleanly. That was becoming a skill.

"This is where Sera's route touched?" Mira asked.

Sera looked startled. "It feels like it."

"Instrumentally?"

"Give me a second."

She compared imprint logs, Nadi Or map, current passive field. "Yes. Within uncertainty. Within honesty."

Keeps-Water-Calm moved inside the brine chamber until their body aligned with the pale sheet. "We do not enter further today."

Mira nodded. "Why?"

"Because you have not yet learned how to leave."

No one argued.

They held position for twelve minutes, recording without active probe. The lattice carried currents around them. Mira marked every impulse to interpret. Archive. No. Memory. Cracked. Organ. Partial. Ancestor. Function, not relic. Sovereign. Political interruption. Relation. Too easy to make vague. Continuing remembering. Awkward enough to make room.

Then the pale sheet pulsed.

The submersible did not move. The displays did not spike. But Mira felt every hair under her suit rise.

"What was that?" Brenn asked from the distant court channel, voice delayed by caution relays.

Sera looked at the data. "Relation event. Low amplitude. No distress. It incorporated our hold-position noise."

Keeps-Water-Calm said, "The lattice knows you are not cutting."

Mira looked at the pale sheet, the dark injury shadow behind them, the route braid ahead they were not permitted to follow.

"Is that acceptance?"

"No."

"Acknowledgment?"

"Closer."

Sera whispered, "Maybe the tool is known asleep."

Keeps-Water-Calm's colors shifted. "Maybe."

Patient Error began ascent after that. Not because they had learned enough, but because staying longer would become taking. The lattice released them by increments. Passage narrowed behind them without hostility. The damaged lobe receded. The outer reef became pale architecture again only at a distance, proving how much distance lied by simplification.

As Cael Station's launch sleeve rose above them, Mira felt the human world reassemble: court, injunction, board counsel, worker remedy, debt, oxygen, orders, records. Necessary crates. Cracked vessels. Tools that could stop other tools if held correctly and watched constantly.

The submersible docked.

When the hatch opened, Sera stayed seated for a moment longer.

Mira looked back. "Current?"

"Current," Sera said. "But larger in inconvenient directions."

Keeps-Water-Calm drifted in the brine chamber, attendants loose.

Mira placed one hand on the submersible wall, not quite touching the water exchange port. "Thank you for passage."

"Do not thank the reef," the Orison said.

Mira paused.

"Tell the court what passage cost."

She nodded.

Behind them, Patient Error's hull ticked softly as it cooled, a small human seed returned from among the lattice without pretending it had entered a place made for it.


Chapter 30: The Memory That Is Not a Memory

Mira's report from the lattice began with a refusal.

D-17 did not show us a memory.

She typed the sentence, then sat back while Cael Station's legal archive hummed around her. It was late, or early, or some station fiction in between. Sera slept in Medical because this time she had not argued quickly enough. Brenn slept at his desk with one hand on the injunction draft and woke every six minutes to write a phrase before sleep caught him again. Keeps-Water-Calm rested in the treaty tank. Nadi Or's ship held outside the lower shelf, vanes folded. Drex Platform remained disabled, vigil reduced but not ended. The world, for one fragile interval, was not actively catching fire.

Mira continued.

It maintained relation in our presence.

Better. Still small.

She opened the data from Patient Error: passive route mapping, pressure events, chemical gradients, relation field shifts, injury shadow, pale sheet pulse, passage conditions. The numbers were clean in the way good field data could be clean without being simple. They did not prove a message. They did not prove a stored scene. They did not prove the reef had intended to communicate with human law. They proved something stranger and more demanding: D-17 continued in ways that made human categories not merely incomplete but behaviorally dangerous.

Every time the report tried to say memory, it dragged ownership behind it.

Memory of first contact.

Memory of route.

Memory of injury.

Each phrase made the reef sound like a container holding recoverable content. But what Mira had witnessed among the lattice was not content. It was continuity enacted. A route remained because organisms, scaffold, pressure, and Orison practice kept making it remain. Injury mattered because future continuation had to route around loss. The old chart mattered because earlier humans had made room for uncertainty that later systems administratively erased. Sera's imprint mattered because a human nervous system had been briefly drafted into relation and had nearly been made into proof by people who should have known better.

Mira deleted memory from the section heading.

She wrote:

Continuity Without Storage.

Sera's voice came from the doorway. "That sounds like a philosophy paper that would make me skip lunch."

Mira turned. "You are supposed to be asleep."

"Everyone keeps saying that like sleep is not where the pipes get ideas."

"Medical cleared you?"

"Medical lost focus."

"That is not the same."

Sera came in anyway, wrapped in a blanket again, carrying two electrolyte bulbs. She set one beside Mira. "Hydrate or I tell Anika."

Mira drank.

Sera looked at the report. "D-17 did not show us a memory."

"No."

"Good."

"You agree?"

Sera lowered herself into the chair beside her. "The imprint was not memory either. I keep wanting to say it was because humans understand memory as the least ridiculous option. But it was more like being given an orientation that only exists because a path keeps being maintained. If maintenance stops, the orientation is not lost like a file. It becomes impossible to inherit."

Mira copied the sentence into the report.

"Hey."

"Too useful."

"At least fix the part where I sound awake."

Mira smiled faintly and edited. "If maintenance stops, the orientation is not erased as stored data; it ceases to be available as lived continuity."

Sera read it. "Less me. More court. Fine."

They worked side by side for an hour. Sera added technical notes where Mira grew too lyrical. Mira added caution where Sera grew too sharp. Together they built a report that refused the wrong gift of clarity. D-17's continuing remembering was not equivalent to data storage, not reducible to subjective experience, not validated solely by Orison assertion, not captured by human sensory protocol, and not dismissible as metaphor. It existed as a maintained ecological relation whose interruption changed future capacity.

Brenn arrived halfway through, hair flattened on one side, eyes bright with the particular madness of exhausted legal thought. He read the title.

"Continuity Without Storage," he said.

Sera braced.

"Good," he said.

Sera narrowed her eyes. "Suspicious."
