"The bloom is holding workers safe from itself. Shell-That-Returns is holding the deeper chamber closed. If collar releases while both hold alone, the wound chooses them."

"What else is there?"

Keeps-Water-Calm turned toward her.

Mira knew the answer before the translator spoke.

"Witness must carry enough relation for the court to taste why law is late."

Brenn heard it. "No."

Sera said, "Absolutely no."

Nadi Or said nothing, which was worse.

Mira stood in the lower cradle with one hand on Patient Error's interface and the other still feeling the echo of distress in her bones. Chapter by chapter, choice by choice, she had moved from observer to witness to participant. Each step had seemed forced by necessity. That did not make the steps false. The bloom could not be ordered. The court could not understand fast enough through documents alone. The reef was wounded because humans had demanded proof in forms that always arrived after harm.

"What would it require?" she asked.

Brenn's voice sharpened. "Mira."

Keeps-Water-Calm did not look away. "Controlled imprint. Public record. Court present. Not proof taken from Sera. Not spectacle. Your consent. My mediation. Nadi Or witness. Patient Error carrier. Enough for the collar release to happen inside hearing, not extraction."

Sera whispered, "That is the next threshold, isn't it?"

No one understood the phrasing except perhaps the part of Mira that knew stories were just consequence with memory.

On the platform, Drex waited with emergency authority burning down its own legitimacy second by second.

In the reef, the jammed head shuddered.

The bloom held workers, machines, fault, and repair in one trembling threshold.

Mira looked at Brenn's channel light.

"Adjudicator," she said, "keep the public record open."


Chapter 37: Mira Chooses Witness

Brenn did not answer immediately.

The pause was small. In ordinary time, it might have been respect. In the lower cradle, with the jammed cutting head shuddering in D-17's fractured lobe and emergency authority burning through Drex's hands on the platform, it felt like another system deciding whether a human choice was permitted.

Then Brenn said, "Public record remains open."

Sera's voice cut across him. "Under objection."

"Noted," Brenn said.

"No. Not noted like a footnote. Noted like someone writes that this is medically reckless, epistemically dangerous, and exactly the trap we have been avoiding since the Treaty Pool."

Brenn's breath roughened. "Recorded."

Mira stood beside Patient Error's interface and listened to her own pulse trying to become argument. The lower cradle was cold enough to make every wet surface steam. Beyond the window, water moved in bands around the docked submersible, disturbed by the bloom's spread through the platform and by Shell-That-Returns holding the deeper chamber closed around damage humans had made. The witness beads on Patient Error's hull waited, small and inert until they were not. Keeps-Water-Calm lay in the mobility sling, attendants gathered close, gray-green colors flickering under the membrane.

"Mira," Sera said, softer now. "You are not an instrument."

"No."

"Do not let them use you as one."

Mira looked at the interface. "That is why I have to choose it."

"Choice under emergency is compromised."

"So is every worker choice on that platform. We still have to tell the difference between coercion and responsibility."

Sera said nothing. It was worse than disagreement.

Brenn's voice came from the archive, and beneath the legal control she heard fear. "Dr. Cael, for the record: do you understand that a controlled imprint may produce neurological stress, memory intrusion, sensory confusion, impaired judgment, and unknown long-term effects?"

"Yes."

"Do you understand that this court cannot compel you to undergo it?"

"Yes."

"Has any Orison representative, corporate actor, worker, court officer, or Choral Nomad witness threatened you into participation?"

Mira almost laughed. The world was made of threats. The trick was naming the ones with hands.

"No."

"State your reason."

She looked toward Keeps-Water-Calm. The Orison had asked for relation, not heroics. The difference mattered. Heroics made a person large enough to hide everyone else. Relation made everyone harder to edit out.

"The court has admitted documents, maps, testimony, acoustic correlation, biological data, and public record of injury," Mira said. "Those are necessary. They are not sufficient to guide the collar release without widening the wound. The defensive bloom and Shell-That-Returns are responding to relation, threat, tool behavior, and witness context. If the collar release occurs as another human operation on damaged living relation, it may trigger more defense or force the reef to carry the cost alone. I consent to a controlled, mediated imprint so the release can be witnessed as repair under court record, not extraction."

Brenn let the statement stand in silence long enough to become part of the room.

Then he said, "Keeps-Water-Calm, for the record, can you mediate without taking from Dr. Cael more than she consents to give?"

The translator paused.

Keeps-Water-Calm answered, "No."

Sera made a small sound.

The Orison continued. "I can refuse taking. I can narrow touch. I can stop if distress exceeds threshold. I cannot promise relation will remain within human consent after humans have cut relation and asked it to be understood quickly."

Brenn said, "That is not reassuring."

"Reassurance is often a false instrument."

"Can you make it safer?"

"Yes."

"How?"

Keeps-Water-Calm's attendants lifted the sling membrane and adjusted the brine exchange ports. "No full Treaty Pool. No ancestral depth. No old promise route, because it is broken. We touch only the present wound edge, worker repair intention, tool sleep, and court hearing. Nadi Or anchors exterior map. Patient Error carries witness beads. Sera monitors human neural stress and may call abort. Brenn holds legal boundary. Mira may withdraw words, hand, or breath."

"Breath?" Brenn asked.

Sera answered before the Orison. "She controls the respiratory sync. If she changes pattern sharply, we abort."

"Can she change pattern if impaired?"

"That is why I am monitoring."

"You are medically compromised."

"Yes, and still the best person here, which should make everyone ashamed."

Mira adjusted the cradle interface to accept a human contact loop. The system was not designed for ritual, only data. She found the manual haptic pads, disabled compression, and routed raw sensor flow to local record, Nadi Or relay, and Brenn's court channel. Sera walked her through three settings with crisp professionalism and only one curse. Nadi Or added an external timing anchor from Bell Without Metal. Brenn read emergency procedural language aloud until even he sounded tired of human systems needing permission to notice life.

On Drex Platform, Tavo and the pylon teams held counter-tension. Their camera feeds showed workers braced against manual wheels, suit joints trembling, the defensive bloom wrapped around rig surfaces inches from their gloves. Anika stood at pylon three's threshold, talking to a red-gold filament as if it were a nervous worker with a wrench.

"No cut," she said. "No burn. We are holding. You hold. Everyone holds."

Drex remained at the executive access panel, one hand on the collar release queue, face drained by emergency light. "Authority window is degrading. Board is trying to revoke the wafer."

"How long?" Brenn asked.

"Maybe five minutes. Maybe less."

Mira placed her left hand on Patient Error's contact pad.

It was cold.

Keeps-Water-Calm extended one filament from the sling membrane and stopped a centimeter above her wrist.

"Last refusal," the Orison said.

"I consent."

"Not enough. Say what you refuse."

Mira looked at the water. "I refuse archive. I refuse spectacle. I refuse to become proof that replaces the reef. I refuse to let my experience excuse delay after it ends. I refuse to carry more than present wound, repair intention, tool sleep, and court hearing."

Keeps-Water-Calm's colors shifted faintly. "Better."

Sera said, "Abort word?"

"Enough."

"Too noble. Pick something ugly."

"Invoice."

Sera laughed once, startled and pained. "Accepted."

Brenn said, "Court recognizes abort word: invoice."

"This is the worst legal sentence ever spoken," Sera said.

Mira put her right hand under the hovering filament.

Contact was not touch.

Touch would have been surface, pressure, skin reporting a boundary. This entered by relation between systems that did not share a single language for body. The contact pad warmed beneath her left palm. The witness beads outside Patient Error woke into low acoustic shimmer. Keeps-Water-Calm's filament settled against her wrist with almost no weight. Sera began counting her breaths over the channel, steady and unforgiving.

"In four. Hold two. Out six. Again."

Mira followed.

The lower cradle disappeared badly.

Not gone. Never gone. She remained standing. Her knees ached. Her hand was cold. Sera counted. Brenn recorded. But the room lost its claim to being the only arrangement of consequence.

She felt the present wound edge.

Not pain as metaphor. Pain as broken work. Living film pulling chemistry away from torn scaffold. Carrier organisms arriving with packets that no longer matched the route they expected. Boundary bodies holding back machines they did not hate and humans they did not understand. A deeper chamber closed by Shell-That-Returns, not because it wished to hide from witness but because witness had arrived with teeth before and called itself survey.

She felt worker repair intention.

Hands on manual wheels. Tavo's anger shaped into instruction. Anika turning fear into plain speech. A mechanic refusing to let go though her shoulder burned. A security guard with a bruised wrist standing beside a worker he had been ordered to block, both watching a blue symbiont cover the same conduit they needed alive. Drex's hand over release, guilt not forgiven, intention not erased by guilt.

She felt tool sleep.

Drive power cut. Residual torque. Metal caught in living structure. Collar tension translating human delay into widening damage. A machine unable to mean harm and unable to stop being harm because intention had been placed elsewhere, in board rooms and legal phrases and one man saying execute limited preservation.

She felt court hearing.

Brenn in the locked archive, one hand against the table because the distress wave had made his body evidence against his training. Sera watching Mira's neural trace with furious love disguised as irritation. Nadi Or holding exterior map open against relay suppression. Public receivers scattered across Pelagos catching fragments before corporate silence closed around them. Law late, cracked, embarrassed, necessary.

Mira did not see history.

She carried simultaneous obligations until the human mind tried to stack them and failed. Failure was the point. The reef was not a thing from which a single fact could be extracted. It was relation under pressure, and pressure changed every participant.

Her breath shortened.

"In four," Sera said sharply. "Hold two. Out six."

Mira obeyed.

Keeps-Water-Calm's voice came through, not outside, not inside. "Name repair."

Mira tried to speak. Her mouth did not understand its job. Sera counted. The room returned enough for words.

"Manual teams hold counter-tension," Mira said. "Drex releases guide collar only on court mark. Bloom does not strike workers holding noncutting tools. Shell-That-Returns keeps deeper chamber closed until head clears. Patient Error witnesses. Nadi Or maps. Brenn records. No one calls removal extraction."

Keeps-Water-Calm's filament tightened by a fraction.

"Name fault."

"Calyx stripped warning. Board ordered cut. Drex certified and executed. Human law failed to recognize relation before injury. The court is late."

Brenn said, quietly, "Recorded."

"Name limit."

Mira gasped. The wound edge pulled at attention the way gravity pulled at mass. There was so much more there. Not knowledge. Need. If she let it, every current would become a reason to stay until her body learned damage as its only language.

"Invoice," Sera said.

Mira realized she had not said it. Sera had, for her, because monitoring meant more than watching.

Keeps-Water-Calm withdrew the filament.

The lower cradle hit Mira all at once. Cold. Metal. Her right wrist burning. Her left hand numb against the pad. Her throat raw. She stumbled, and an attendant caught her before she struck the console.

Sera's voice was shaking. "Abort complete. Mira, say your name."

"Mira Cael."

"Where are you?"

"Lower cradle. Cael Station."

"What do you hate?"

"Bad categories."

"Good enough."

Brenn said, "Is the witness event usable?"

Sera snapped, "She is still standing."

"I did not ask if it was convenient. I asked because the collar release window is closing."

Mira held up one hand before Sera could answer with something that would become contempt. "Usable."

Keeps-Water-Calm's translator spoke through visible strain. "Usable. Narrow. Costly. Do not expand."

Nadi Or said, "Bell Without Metal confirms relation alignment transmitted to bloom perimeter. Defensive organisms at pylon three and six have opened partial work corridors."

Drex leaned toward his console. "Collar release ready."

Brenn's voice changed. It became court again, but court that had tasted its own insufficiency and did not pretend. "On public record, under emergency injunction, with Orison mediation, worker counter-tension, Choral Nomad mapping, and Dr. Mira Cael's controlled witness event: Director Drex, release the guide collar for the sole purpose of removing the jammed cutting head from D-17's fractured lobe. No extraction, no sample preservation, no claim activity."

Drex closed his eyes once.

"Releasing."

The collar opened.

For a second, the reef held all of them.


Chapter 38: What the Court Tastes

Yul Brenn had spent twenty-three years learning to distrust immediacy.

Immediate impressions made bad law. Immediate fear made broad exceptions. Immediate grief wanted punishment before causation. Immediate sympathy loved the first face it saw and forgot the absent. The work of adjudication, as he had practiced and defended it, was to slow the world's appetite for certainty until facts could survive being handled.

Then the guide collar released, and the court tasted salt.

Brenn was still in the locked archive. His body did not leave the chair. His hand remained on the open record control. The emergency writ lay in front of him, text assembled from jurisdiction, precedent, improvisation, and terror. Sera stood beside the medical partition with one hand on the wall and one eye on Mira's neural trace. None of that vanished.

But the court, because the court was now made of more than room and paper, entered relation by degrees.

Patient Error's witness beads carried the release event. Nadi Or held the exterior map open. Keeps-Water-Calm narrowed the contact. Mira's controlled imprint made a human bridge the court had not known how to ask for without making a person into evidence. The defensive bloom opened corridors around worker bodies. Shell-That-Returns held the deeper chamber closed. The jammed cutting head loosened.

Brenn tasted not memory, but consequence.

Metal under load.

Living scaffold flexing around an object it had not invited and could not simply reject without tearing itself wider.

Workers at pylon three and six leaning into manual wheels, bodies shaking, not heroes, not innocents, not instruments, but people whose debt had been used to put their hands on a wound and whose hands now mattered.

Orison defensive organisms restraining themselves while burned tissue carried current away from machines.

Drex at the executive panel, fault and repair intention occupying the same body without cancelling each other.

Mira on the lower cradle floor, breathing because Sera made her.

The taste became pressure. The pressure became relation. The relation did not become a neat legal category.

Brenn had expected, if he was honest in the privacy of his own panic, that direct experience might reveal the correct word. Person. Property. Ecosystem. Archive. Sacred site. Treaty partner. Evidence source. Some term, perhaps old and neglected, perhaps new and ugly, would step forward and let law begin its work.

No word stepped forward.

Instead every word showed its cost.

Person invited human likeness and made the reef beg for recognition by resemblance. Property was violence with a filing system. Ecosystem described interaction and evaded obligation. Archive implied storage and access. Sacred site helped only until someone asked whose religion counted. Treaty partner required a mouth where relation had a thousand edges. Evidence source was the worst of them, because it made injury useful.

The collar released another centimeter.

On the platform feed, Tavo shouted, "Hold! Hold there!"

Workers at pylon three braced. At pylon six, a gauge needle swung into red and a blue symbiont wrapped the housing, damping vibration at the cost of burning itself against hot metal. Anika yelled at the team to slow, not stop, because stop would let the head settle back into the wound. Drex adjusted collar angle manually, his executive authority timer flashing ninety seconds.

Brenn felt the difference between extraction and removal.

It was not the physical direction. Both moved material. Both involved human tools. Both risked more damage. The difference lived in relation: whether the tool's action organized the world around taking value or around reducing harm while being witnessed by those harmed and those made responsible.

He opened a new section in the order with hands that did not feel entirely like his and typed:

The court distinguishes emergency removal from extraction by purpose, consent context, witness relation, material destination, and repair obligation. No material removed from D-17 under this order may be owned, sold, classified as preserved claim evidence, or used to strengthen any extraction right. All contact is presumptively injury management under Orison-supervised repair.

Sera looked over at him. "You writing?"

"Yes."

"While tasting impossible law?"

"Especially then."

"Fine. Add that no one gets to call Mira repeatable."

He typed:

No human witness experience shall be treated as a reusable instrument, compulsory proof method, or substitute for nonhuman standing.

"Better," Sera said.

The head shifted.

For one terrible instant the fracture widened.

Brenn felt the widening as absence opening under his tongue. He gripped the table hard enough to hurt. In the platform feed, the workers cried out. The bloom surged toward the rig. Red-gold forms tightened around pylon joints. A security guard in the background dropped to his knees and covered his head, though nothing had struck him.

Mira's voice came from the lower cradle, raw but clear. "Drex, not up. Back along the cut angle. You are pulling against the repair flow."

Drex's eyes flicked across models. "The model says vertical clearance."

"The model is why we are here."

Tavo shouted, "Back angle two degrees!"

"That increases equipment loss," Drex said.

Anika, from pylon three, snarled, "Lose equipment."

Drex changed the angle.

The bore head moved back along the path it had made, not cleanly, not harmlessly, but with less tearing. Patient Error's beads captured the change. Shell-That-Returns tightened pressure around the deeper chamber. The bloom did not withdraw, but it stopped surging. Workers adjusted counter-tension in small increments, a choreography between people who had never rehearsed together and living organisms that did not forgive quickly but could recognize a changed vector.

Brenn recorded the angle correction and added another sentence before he could become afraid of it:

Human technical models are subordinate to live relation-field evidence where the model has already produced injury.

He stared at the sentence.

It was dangerous. It would be attacked. It might be too broad. It was also the first honest thing the order had said about expertise since the cut began.

Nadi Or's voice entered the archive, quiet. "Adjudicator, do you now understand why maps bow?"

Brenn swallowed. "No."

"Good. Bow anyway."

He did not know whether the court record should include that.

He included it.

The executive authority timer hit forty seconds.

Drex said, "Head is half clear. Collar load dropping. Need final counter-pull."

Tavo's team strained at pylon six. One worker slipped. A red-gold filament caught her harness before she fell into the tension wheel. She froze, eyes wide behind her visor.

"Say thank you later," Tavo barked. "Pull now."

At pylon three, Anika's team leaned together, workers and former guards shoulder to shoulder because fear had rearranged the room faster than politics could. The bloom held a corridor open behind them. Medical pumps flickered, then stabilized as blue symbionts released one conduit and covered another. The platform itself seemed to be deciding whether to remain a machine or become a confession.

Mira said, "Now."

Brenn said, "Court mark. Final counter-pull authorized."

Drex released the last collar tension.

The cutting head came free.

It did not rise triumphant. It sagged backward into its guide cradle, dead drive heavy with damaged living film and burned symbiont tissue. No one cheered. The absence of the tool inside the wound became its own pressure. D-17's fractured lobe shuddered, then settled around the gap where the head had been. Carrier organisms streamed toward the exposed surfaces. Red-gold boundary forms moved between the wound and the rig, not trusting absence yet. Blue symbionts detached from hot metal in ragged patches.

Shell-That-Returns loosened its hold on the deeper chamber by one narrow degree.

Brenn tasted relief and learned that relief could be guilty without being false.

Keeps-Water-Calm spoke from the lower cradle. "Tool sleeps outside wound."

Nadi Or confirmed. "Tool outside wound. Relation loss remains. Fracture contained for now."

For now.

Brenn typed the phrase into the record because law loved finality and the reef had just taught him to distrust it.

Drex's authority timer expired.

The platform screens flashed as board control tried to reassert itself over a rig now jammed with defensive bloom, worker lockouts, court order, public record, and a cutting head no longer in the lobe. Calyx counsel flooded every channel with objections. Most did not reach anyone because Nadi Or kept routing the contact promise over them in low, relentless repetition.

No claim. Holding position. We hear you. No cut. We leave clear.

Brenn stood.

His knees were not steady. He did not care. The archive door was still locked. The court was still provisional. The order was still being written inside an emergency that had not ended. But he had tasted enough to know that his earlier refusal had been correct and incomplete. Sensory experience alone was not evidence. But evidence that refused all sensory relation had been a door held open for harm.

"This court issues supplemental findings," he said.

Sera looked at him. "You are about to make enemies."

"That appears to be the one abundant resource."

He read into the public record:

"D-17 is not dormant mineral substrate. It is a living continuity ecology maintaining relation across Orison biological, cultural, and environmental practice. Injury to D-17 is injury to ongoing relation and future capacity, not mere disturbance of archived past. The 2384 contact promise, though not filed as treaty, placed human actors on notice that D-17 could not ethically or legally be treated as unclaimed inert matter. Calyx Meridian Resource Holdings proceeded after warning, court injunction, and Orison nonlethal defense. All extraction rights are suspended pending full hearing. All material displaced by the unauthorized cut is held under repair trust, not evidence ownership. Worker debt enforcement is stayed pending inquiry into coercive deployment. No further contact with D-17 may occur except under Orison-supervised repair, Choral Nomad mapping, worker safety authority, and court review."

He stopped, breathing hard.

The words were too few. Too much. Late. Necessary.

Mira's voice came through from the lower cradle. "Brenn."

"Yes."

"Add that the court felt it and that feeling does not make the reef real. It makes our categories accountable."

He closed his eyes.

Then he added:

"The court's mediated sensory participation is not the basis of D-17's reality. It is a corrective to human procedures that previously excluded relevant forms of relation from legal attention."

Sera whispered, "Good."

Keeps-Water-Calm said, "Less wrong."

In the archive, locked in by people who thought doors could contain jurisdiction, Yul Brenn saved the supplemental findings and transmitted them through every channel Nadi Or could keep alive.

Outside, beneath the reef of glass, the wound remained.

This time, it was not alone in the record.


Chapter 39: Olan's Defense

Olan Drex made his defense from a corridor floor outside pylon three, sitting with his back against a breaker cage he had opened too late.

The platform did not offer him a courtroom. The command deck had been sealed by board override and then half-disabled by defensive bloom. The archive remained locked around Brenn and Sera. The lower cradle held Mira, Keeps-Water-Calm, and Patient Error's witness array. The reef held the wound. The workers held corridors. The court, being provisional and embarrassed into honesty, held all of them by channel, record, and necessity.

Drex's suit had a burn mark across the left thigh where a hot conduit had brushed him during the counter-tension release. His right hand shook from the executive wafer's biometric shock. He could have hidden both by angling the camera higher. He did not.

Brenn opened the hearing with no ceremony. "Director Olan Drex, you remain under court order and investigation for unauthorized extraction, obstruction, confinement of audit personnel, and activation of emergency preservation protocol after notice of reef relation and contact promise. You asked to make a statement before counsel joins the record. Why?"

Drex looked toward the corridor beyond the camera. Workers moved past in slow, exhausted groups, some toward medical, some toward manual watch points, some simply away from places where the bloom had been too close. A red-gold filament lay across the far threshold like a line drawn by a patient warning.

"Because counsel will make me smaller in useful ways," he said.

Brenn's expression on the split display did not change. "Explain."

"They will say I relied on official classification. True. They will say board authority constrained local discretion. True. They will say worker safety required local presence inside the emergency protocol. Partly true. They will say the system exceeded my limited parameters. True. They will say any reasonable director under debt, oxygen, and investor pressure would have treated D-17 as a contested resource until a final court order. Maybe true. They will use every truth to avoid the shape they make together."

Mira listened from the lower cradle with her shoulders wrapped in an emergency blanket Sera had ordered someone to bring and then insulted for folding badly. The controlled imprint had left Mira hollow behind the eyes. She could still feel the present wound edge if she let attention slip. She did not let it slip.

Brenn said, "And what shape do they make?"

Drex looked at the camera. "Impossible categories do not govern worker survival. They govern who gets blamed after survival has already been made impossible."

Anika, seated just outside the frame with one arm bandaged, said, "That better not be your whole defense."

"It is not."

"Good, because I was about to throw something."

Brenn allowed the interruption to stand. Perhaps he was learning which disorder contained relevant evidence.

Drex continued. "The board gave me a mineable world and workers whose oxygen credit depended on production milestones. The Compact gave me a classification that said dormant mineral architecture. Orison treaty channels gave me caution without enforcement. Court channels gave me delay. Audit gave me uncertainty. Workers gave me fear. Every category arrived with an exception for later review. No one category said cut. Together they built a corridor where cutting looked like the only action that would keep the platform alive long enough for anyone to argue."

"You are arguing necessity," Brenn said.

"No."

Brenn leaned forward. "Then be precise."

"Necessity excuses. I am explaining architecture."

The corridor behind Drex hissed as a pressure seal settled. A blue symbiont withdrew from the seam, leaving a line of moisture where it had prevented a leak. Drex watched it for a second.

"When the first bore showed response," he said, "I should have stopped. When Mira showed worker-debt triggers tied to acceleration, I should have stopped. When Keeps-Water-Calm said archive was wrong, I should have stopped. When Sera nearly collapsed from the imprint, I should have stopped. When Nadi Or's map confirmed relation density, I should have stopped. When the contact promise surfaced, I should have stopped. Instead, each time, I asked which category gave me authority to stop without destroying the workers. I kept asking for permission from systems designed to make no one responsible until extraction was done."

Sera's voice from the archive was dry and faint. "That sounds dangerously close to insight."

"Do not worry," Drex said. "It arrived after the wound."

Mira closed her eyes.

Keeps-Water-Calm, from the lower cradle sling, said nothing. That silence made more room than translation would have.

Brenn asked, "Why did you certify instability?"

"Because conditions were unstable."

"You knew how the board would use that certification."

"I knew how they might."

"That distinction matters less after the cut."

"Yes."

"Why activate limited preservation?"

Drex's jaw tightened. "Because I believed if I kept local command attached to the queue, I could limit depth and protect workers from remote security."

"Was that belief reasonable?"

"It was informed. It was also arrogant."

"How?"

"I thought understanding the machinery meant I understood the decision. I thought being less careless than the board made my hand safer on the lever. I thought partial control was protection. The system used my partial control as a bridge."

Tavo's voice entered from pylon six, where he was still supervising manual stabilization. "You also thought workers would forgive anything called oxygen."

Drex did not look away. "Yes."

Anika's bandaged hand curled. "Say that again."

"I used worker survival as a reason to keep the claim alive. I told myself it was different from the board using worker debt as a weapon because I cared whether the workers lived. It was different. It was not different enough."

No one spoke for several seconds.

The court record held corridor fan noise, distant water, an Orison attendant's soft movement, Sera's monitor tones, Nadi Or's low acoustic relay, and the human discomfort of a confession that did not repair what it named.

Brenn said, "Calyx counsel will argue that your statement prejudices the company while you are under duress from alien defensive activity."

Drex almost smiled. "Counsel should have unlocked the archive faster."

"Are you under threat?"

Drex looked at the red-gold filament across the corridor. "Yes."

"From whom?"

"Everyone I have harmed, in different ways."

"Be legally useful."

"The bloom is present and may restrict movement. Workers are armed with tools or tool-adjacent resentment. The board is threatening liability and oxygen credit. The reef is wounded. None of those threats caused me to fabricate this statement."

Brenn nodded once.

Mira spoke before she planned to. "Olan, what do you want the court to do with you?"

His eyes shifted to her display. For a moment she saw the man from the early platform tour: controlled, exhausted, competent, already compromised and not yet broken enough to admit it. Then that version passed.

"Remove me from authority," he said.

"That is easy."

"No. It is not. If the court simply removes me and leaves the board's debt structure intact, they will replace me with someone cleaner who knows less and fears workers less personally. If the court makes me the single villain, Calyx survives the lesson. If the court forgives me because the system was impossible, the wound becomes accounting. I want neither."

"What then?"

"Make my authority evidence. Map every decision path. Freeze worker debt. Put platform control under a repair trust with worker veto and Orison boundary review. Preserve all board communications. Name the classification failure. Prosecute me if prosecution keeps the structure visible. Do not let punishment become a curtain."

Anika stared at him.

"That was almost useful," she said.

"I will try not to overvalue the achievement."

Brenn typed for a while. "Director Drex, did Calyx Meridian Resource Holdings directly threaten worker oxygen credit to secure compliance after the contact-promise evidence emerged?"

"Yes."

"Who transmitted the threat?"

"Board crisis counsel via encrypted executive channel. The message stated that failure to preserve claim-value substrate under emergency authority would trigger debt acceleration review, platform solvency reassessment, and oxygen-credit ration normalization."

Sera said, "Ration normalization is a phrase that should be buried in a shallow grave."

Brenn said, "Meaning?"

Drex answered. "Workers would lose subsidized oxygen credit and medical allotment protections tied to production milestones. Families off-platform would be billed at emergency rates. Some would default within two cycles."

Mira felt cold despite the blanket.

Keeps-Water-Calm's translator came on at last. "Humans made breath collateral."

Drex bowed his head. "Yes."

"And called reef cutting necessity."

"Yes."

"And now ask relation to distinguish workers from tools."

Drex looked up. "Yes."

The Orison's colors moved in small, difficult bands under the sling membrane. "This is why translation breaks. Words arrive as if one harm has one owner."

Brenn recorded that too.

The board finally broke through on an emergency legal channel, a voice bright with professional alarm. "Calyx Meridian Resource Holdings objects to this coerced and procedurally defective proceeding. Director Drex is not authorized to waive privilege, admit liability, or characterize internal worker support structures as debt coercion. All statements are made under alien interference and physical confinement."

Anika leaned toward Drex's camera. "He is sitting on the floor because your command deck tried to become a crime scene."

"Unidentified speaker, you are not party to this proceeding."

"I am Anika Venn, oxygen debtor, rig mechanic, and the person who knows which pipe keeps your asset from drowning. Spell my name right."

Brenn said, "The court recognizes Anika Venn as worker witness pending formal testimony."

The counsel voice sharpened. "This court lacks jurisdiction to restructure private labor contracts."

Brenn looked tired enough to be dangerous. "This court has jurisdiction over emergency conduct affecting extraction, worker safety, evidence preservation, and interspecies treaty obligations. If your labor contracts are the mechanism by which prohibited extraction proceeds after injunction, they have walked into my jurisdiction wearing bells."

Sera whispered, "Nice."

"Do not encourage me," Brenn said.

Drex looked down at his shaking right hand. "Counsel is right about one thing."

Mira tensed.

"I cannot waive everyone's privilege. I cannot speak for every worker. I cannot make my confession into their testimony. The court needs Tavo."

Tavo's channel clicked from pylon six. "I heard that."

Brenn said, "Crew Chief Rusk, are you prepared to testify?"

"No."

"Will you?"

The delay held the whole platform.

Tavo said, "Yes. But I am not doing it from pylon six with my hands on a wheel and a living warning wrapped around my boot."

Nadi Or said, "Bell Without Metal confirms stabilization holding. Defensive bloom remains watchful. Short pause possible."

Keeps-Water-Calm added, "Short."

Brenn nodded. "Then we move to worker testimony under emergency protection."

Drex started to stand.

Anika put one bandaged hand on his shoulder and pushed him back down.

"You already talked," she said. "Try listening while seated."

Drex stayed seated.

For once, no system rewarded him for standing.


Chapter 40: Tavo Testifies

Tavo Rusk removed his helmet before he testified.

It was a bad safety choice. Pylon six still smelled of hot insulation, salt, and the chemical bitterness of anti-fouling residue. The bloom had not withdrawn. Blue symbionts lay along the wall conduits in sheets so thin they looked painted until their edges moved. Red-gold forms held the upper tension housing in a loose net, not trusting the machine, not trusting the workers, perhaps not trusting themselves. The air was breathable but sharp.

Tavo removed the helmet anyway and set it on the deck beside the manual wheel.

"I want the record to see my face," he said.

Brenn answered from the archive. "The court can see you."

"Good. Then it can see I am not a martyr. I am tired, angry, and implicated."

"State your full name and role."

"Tavo Rusk. Crew chief, lower rig operations, Drex Platform. Ten years under Calyx contract, four on Pelagos, two under Director Drex."

"Do you consent to testify under emergency protection?"

"Yes."

"Are you under threat?"

Tavo looked at the red-gold net above the tension housing, then at the workers gathered behind him, then toward the camera link that still carried Calyx counsel despite everyone's preference. "Yes."

"From whom?"

"Debt office, board counsel, damaged reef, defensive bloom, probably three people in this corridor who think I should have acted sooner, and my own memory."

Brenn's face on the small wall display softened by a millimeter. "Are you able to testify truthfully?"

"I have been able for years. Willing is newer."

Anika stood near pylon three but appeared on the split feed, one arm in a sling, mouth set hard. Drex remained seated on the corridor floor, listening. Mira sat in the lower cradle now because Sera had threatened to climb through a second service hatch and sedate her by hand. Keeps-Water-Calm watched from the sling. Nadi Or kept the relay open against corporate interference by methods no one polite would ask them to explain.

Tavo began with oxygen.

"Every worker on the platform knows the official contract language," he said. "Base air allotment, productivity supplement, family offset, medical reserve, emergency ration conversion. It sounds like math. It is a leash. Your base allotment keeps you alive if nothing goes wrong and you never get sick and no dependent needs anything and you do not mind choosing between sleep heat and lung margin. Productivity supplement makes ordinary breathing feel earned. Family offset means your kid's clinic bill is tied to your shift output. Medical reserve is only generous until you read the default clause."

Brenn asked, "Were these terms disclosed?"

"Yes."

Calyx counsel cut in. "Then the workers accepted them knowingly."

Tavo laughed once. "There it is. The cleanest lie in labor law."

Brenn said, "Crew Chief, answer through the court."

"Fine. Yes, disclosed. No, not meaningfully negotiable. Calyx hires from stations where air, debt, and transport are already linked. You sign because the alternative is slower collapse somewhere with worse lighting."

"Did Director Drex know this?"

"Yes."

Drex did not dispute it.

"Did you?"

"Yes."

"Did you enforce it?"

Tavo's jaw moved. "Yes."

The word sat between him and the workers like a tool set down blade-up.

"How?" Brenn asked.

"Shift assignment. Overtime approval. Medical coding. Productivity ratings. I could make a worker's debt month survivable or impossible by calling a delay equipment-related instead of worker-related. I told myself I used that discretion to protect people."

"Did you?"

"Sometimes."

"And other times?"

"I kept the rig crew productive enough that Calyx kept the oxygen supplement flowing. I disciplined people for refusing unsafe acceleration because if production fell too far, everyone lost more. I made fear look like management."

Anika looked away.

Tavo saw it and did not protect himself from it.

"Anika warned me about the bore," he said. "Before Mira arrived. Before the first formal complaint. She said the reef response looked wrong. I told her wrong did not pay air. Then I filed her concern as equipment anxiety so it would not trigger a delay review."

Anika's head snapped back toward the feed. "You what?"

Tavo closed his eyes for one beat. "I buried it in maintenance language."

"You told me Drex's office downgraded it."

"Drex's office never saw the first one."

Drex looked up sharply.

Anika took one step toward the camera, stopped when a blue sheet tightened near her boot, and forced herself still. "Why?"

"Because if I filed biological anomaly, the board audit would freeze productivity supplement pending classification. If I filed equipment anxiety, I could keep you on shift and keep watching the bore without triggering debt review."

"You lied to me."

"Yes."

"To protect me?"

"Partly. To protect output. To protect the story where I was the person who knew how much truth the system could survive."

Mira felt that sentence move through the room like a second, smaller confession. It was Drex's argument translated downward into crew muscle. Impossible categories did not only live in board filings. They lived in the choices people made when every door charged rent.

Brenn asked, "Did you sabotage the rig stabilizer during the worker-fault incident?"

Sera made a strangled noise. "Adjudicator."

Brenn frowned. "What?"

"Never mind. Continue."

Tavo stared at the display. "I disabled stabilizer B-14 on the night before the accelerated cut."

"Why?"

"To buy time."

"For whom?"

"Mira. Anika. The workers. The reef. Myself. I did not sort motives cleanly at the time."

"Did disabling B-14 create risk?"

"Yes. If the rig had loaded under storm shear, pylon drift could have injured lower crew. I chose a low-load window and locked out the high-risk sequence, but it was still sabotage."

"Did Director Drex order it?"

"No."

"Did he know?"

"Not before."

Drex said quietly, "I suspected after."

"You suspected everything after," Tavo said. "That was your talent."

Brenn wrote. "Why not file formal emergency objection?"

Tavo's laugh was not humor. "Because formal objection routes through the same debt and productivity system. A crew chief can sabotage one stabilizer in three minutes. A formal objection takes twelve hours, flags the objector, notifies board counsel, freezes supplements if classified as work refusal, and returns with a request for more evidence after the cut is done."

Calyx counsel said, "The witness is characterizing standard safety-review process without foundation."

Tavo looked straight at the camera. "I have foundation. I ran the retaliation spreadsheet."

The corridor seemed to lose sound.

Brenn said, "Explain."

"Calyx does not call it retaliation. It is Workforce Continuity Risk Modeling. Crew chiefs receive dashboard recommendations after safety objections, medical delays, union language, family default, and productivity variance. The model suggests reassignment, supplement review, counseling, or contract acceleration. It never says punish. It says stabilize."

"Do you have access to those records?"

"Yes."

"Can you produce them?"

Tavo looked toward a worker behind the camera. "Lena?"

A young systems tech stepped into view, face pale and jaw set. "Already mirrored to local court cache before board lockout."

Calyx counsel's voice rose. "That is theft of proprietary workforce analytics."

Anika said, "Say workforce analytics one more time and I will need medical for my temper."

Brenn spoke over the channel noise. "The court orders preservation and production of all Workforce Continuity Risk Modeling records related to Pelagos, D-17 extraction, safety objections, medical allotments, oxygen credit, and productivity supplement enforcement. Proprietary objections are preserved but overruled for emergency review."

Tavo nodded once to Lena. "Send it."

The file transfer indicator appeared on the court display.

Nadi Or hummed approval. "Humans sometimes put maps in cages and call them private."

Sera said, "Do not let Brenn write that down."

"Too late," Brenn said.

Tavo continued. "The model flagged Anika three times. It flagged me twice. It flagged fourteen workers after the pipe chorus because they paused in corridors and missed shift targets. It flagged the med tech who cleared Sera's first imprint limits as productivity obstructive. It flagged anyone who used the word living in open channels."

Mira's stomach tightened.

"The word living?" she asked.

"Yes."

"Show me."

Lena pushed the relevant dashboard to the shared record. There it was, in corporate-neutral gray:

TERM RISK: living, memory, Orison warning, ancestor, promise, claim fraud.

Recommended management response: Reframe to approved classification language. Escalate persistent usage to supervisor review.

Keeps-Water-Calm's translator clicked once and then fell silent.

Brenn read the terms aloud, each word turning heavier. "Living. Memory. Orison warning. Ancestor. Promise. Claim fraud."

Tavo said, "We were not only cutting a reef. We were trained not to say what would make cutting harder."

Drex covered his face with one hand.

Anika looked at Tavo as if anger had opened into a larger room and she did not yet know how to stand in it. "Why did you not tell us?"

"Because I thought knowing would make everyone more afraid and less able to work."

"We were already afraid."

"I know."

"No," she said. "You managed it."

Tavo took that without defense.

Brenn asked, "Crew Chief Rusk, what do you want this court to understand?"

Tavo looked at the bloom, the workers, the camera, the reef data scrolling on the side display. "That worker survival was real. Do not let anyone in a clean chair say we should simply have refused. Refusal had prices they never put in the ethics memo. But do not let us hide behind that either. We made choices inside the trap. Some choices delayed harm. Some moved it onto someone else. Some kept air flowing by teaching us not to hear warning. If the court wants repair, it has to break the trap, not just praise the people who resisted after the reef bled."

Brenn said, "Specific remedies."

"Debt stay is not enough. Cancel extraction-linked oxygen debt. Protect family medical allotments. Put workers on paid repair and survey contracts under court and Orison supervision. Amnesty for safety delays and nonviolent sabotage tied to preventing reef harm, with testimony requirements. Prosecute assault and profiteering separately. Open the workforce model. Ban productivity supplements tied to disputed ecological or treaty sites. Give workers authority to trigger independent biological review without debt penalty. And put Anika on the repair council because she was right before the rest of us found language."

Anika blinked hard. "Do not volunteer me while confessing."

"I am testifying. Efficiency matters."

"I hate you."

"Yes."

"Not only."

"I know."

The bloom shifted along the pylon wall. A red-gold filament lowered toward Tavo's helmet on the deck. It did not touch the helmet. It rested beside it, forming a small barrier between the helmet and a slick of chemical residue spreading from the tension housing. Tavo looked down at it.

"Thank you," he said, awkwardly.

No one translated. No one needed to.

Brenn saved the testimony packet and attached the workforce model cache. The court record had become enormous, unwieldy, contradictory, alive with more truth than any order could comfortably hold. That did not make it useless. It made it harder to corrupt by summary.

Calyx counsel tried once more. "This proceeding is contaminated by emotional testimony and alien coercion."

Sera's patience ended. "Counsel, the workers are standing next to a machine your client used to cut a living reef after a court order, while the alien organisms you are afraid of are currently doing a better job of workplace safety than your risk model. Pick a smaller sentence."

Brenn said, "Dr. Kint."

"Fine. Strike my tone."

"The tone remains in the record."

"Good."

Tavo put his helmet back on. His hands shook when he sealed it. He did not pretend otherwise.

"I am done for now," he said.

Brenn nodded. "Crew Chief Rusk, you remain under emergency protection and possible liability review. Your testimony will be considered in worker remedy and obstruction findings."

"Understood."

Anika stepped into the pylon six feed after rerouting from pylon three, or perhaps every corridor had become connected by exhaustion. She stood close enough that the camera caught the sweat and salt on her face.

"Tavo," she said.

He turned.

She hit him once in the chest plate with her uninjured hand. Not hard enough to injure. Hard enough to make the sound carry.

"That is for lying."

"Accepted."

Then she gripped the same chest plate and leaned her helmet against it for one second.

"That is for opening the cage."

He did not move until she stepped back.

Mira watched from the lower cradle and felt the present wound edge ease by some immeasurable amount. Not heal. Ease. Maybe relation could include anger that did not cut.

Keeps-Water-Calm's translator remained silent.

That silence, too, entered the record.


Chapter 41: Keeps-Water-Calm Withdraws

At first, everyone treated the silence as evidence.

That was the human reflex by then: if something happened near the court channel, capture it, label it, preserve it before counsel could turn it into mist. Brenn marked the time. Sera noted the translator output had ceased without equipment failure. Nadi Or confirmed no Choral Nomad relay fault. Mira checked the lower cradle sensors and found the brine exchange stable, the mobility sling intact, the attendants physically calm but close enough to Keeps-Water-Calm that no human would have mistaken calm for ease.

The court waited for words.

The Orison did not give them.

"Keeps-Water-Calm," Brenn said from the archive, "can you hear the court?"

The Orison's body shifted inside the sling. One attendant touched the translator membrane. The membrane lit green. Functioning.

No answer.

Sera, voice thin with exhaustion, said, "Maybe do not ask the question as if the court owns hearing."

Brenn closed his eyes for one breath. "Keeps-Water-Calm, do you wish to continue translation?"

The translator remained silent.

Mira understood before the room accepted it. She had seen the silence settle after Tavo's testimony, not as collapse, not as medical failure, but as decision. Keeps-Water-Calm had translated through injury, insult, legal delay, category failure, the cut, the fracture, and the controlled imprint. They had given human procedure words it did not deserve and warnings it had treated as advisory until damage made them admissible.

Now they had stopped helping human law pretend the problem was vocabulary.

Mira lowered herself beside the sling. "You are withdrawing."

Keeps-Water-Calm's colors changed: blue-gray, reef-dark, a narrow band of white at the edges. The translator recognized input and displayed a status pulse. No words emerged.

"From translation," Mira said.

The Orison's attendants shifted in a pattern that might have been assent.

Brenn's voice came carefully. "If translation withdraws now, the order may be weaker."

Keeps-Water-Calm did not answer.

Sera said, "He knows."

"I know he knows," Brenn said.

"Then maybe say the real thing."

Brenn's hand moved on the record table. Mira imagined him in the locked archive, coat wrinkled, face gray with too little sleep and too much new knowledge, surrounded by the machinery of law and the smell of stale tea. "If translation withdraws now, I may write something wrong enough to harm D-17 again."

Keeps-Water-Calm remained silent.

Nadi Or spoke from Bell Without Metal. "The silence means the risk is already accepted."

"By whom?" Brenn asked.

"By the one refusing to carry the risk for you."

That landed harder than accusation.

Mira looked through the pressure window toward the dark water below the cradle. Patient Error rested in its dock with witness beads dimmed. Beyond station structure and platform steel, beneath glass and damaged current, D-17's fractured lobe held around a wound humans had finally stopped worsening but not yet begun to repair. The reef had become more present in every room and less reducible with every attempt to describe it.

"What does he want?" Anika asked from pylon six.

"Do not do that," Mira said.

Anika's face on the feed tightened. "Do what?"

"Make me turn silence into a demand because it makes us less uncomfortable."

Anika looked away. "Fair."

Drex, still seated near the breaker cage, said, "The board will exploit the silence."

"Yes," Brenn said.

"They will say Orison participation has ended."

"Yes."

"They will say the court lacks a counterparty."

Keeps-Water-Calm moved then.

Not much. One filament lifted from the sling and touched the brine membrane where the translator would have taken pressure pattern and made speech. The membrane brightened. The system waited.

No words.

Mira felt the refusal like a line drawn across her own habits. Counterparty. Participation. Translation. Testimony. Consent. Each useful word carried a small hook, and human law had been using those hooks to pull D-17 toward a shape it could administer.

"Not counterparty," she said.

Brenn did not interrupt.

"Not because D-17 is less than a party," Mira continued. "Because party may be the wrong starting word. It implies a court-facing agent who can appear, answer, waive, bargain, and be bound in the forms we recognize. Keeps-Water-Calm has been translating relation into those forms, and every time we accepted the translation as sufficient, we made him carry the category failure."

Sera said softly, "Mira."

"No. This is the point." She looked at the sling. "You are not refusing to help. You are refusing to let help become substitution."

The translator did not speak.

But the white band along Keeps-Water-Calm's body dimmed.

Brenn said, "If D-17 cannot appear as a party, what can I write?"

No one answered for the Orison.

The room became visibly different when humans stopped waiting for translation to rescue them. Tavo stood with helmet under one arm beside the pylon feed and looked at the bloom before he spoke. Anika leaned against a rail, one hand over her bandage, and did not turn impatience into demand. Drex watched the floor. Sera watched Mira's vital signs and the silent translator at once, her face telling everyone that concentration could be an act of anger. Nadi Or allowed the relay to hum without filling it.

Brenn tried again, but this time not to Keeps-Water-Calm.

"The court needs findings that do not depend on making D-17 human, Orison, corporate, sacred, or owned," he said. "It needs a legal handle that stops cutting, allows repair, binds workers and board, and survives review."

"Handle," Sera said. "Not hook."

"Fine. Legal handle that is not a hook."

Nadi Or said, "Maps bow. Handles open doors. Humans are improving objects today."

Anika gave a tired laugh, then stopped quickly as if laughter might disturb the bloom.

Mira turned the problem in her head and hated how familiar it was. Science had its own hooks. Species. Organism. System. Site. Ecology. Behavior. Response. She had used all of them. Each term was a tool that became a blade when allowed to pretend it was the living thing.

"Subject," she said.

Brenn looked up. "In what sense?"

"Legal subject. Not person. Not property. Subject of protection and obligation. A recognized holder of interests that can be represented, harmed, repaired, and defended without requiring human likeness or direct procedural speech."

Calyx counsel broke in at once. "Objection. The court cannot invent legal subjecthood for a mineral formation in an emergency proceeding."

Keeps-Water-Calm's colors flashed once, sharp as a struck line.

Mira said, "D-17 is not a mineral formation. That is established."

"Disputed."

Brenn's voice cooled. "Counsel, your client cut after warning, court order, Orison defense, contact-promise notice, and live sensory participation. Disputed is no longer a spell."

Sera whispered, "Finally."

Counsel continued, "Subjecthood requires a recognized bearer of rights or duties. D-17 cannot understand duties, receive notice, or participate in remedy."

Tavo's laugh came harsh over the pylon feed. "Neither could half the shell companies that own our air."

Brenn said, "Crew Chief."

"Sorry. No, I'm not."

Anika added, "Put that in the record."

"It already is," Brenn said.

Drex spoke quietly. "Trusts, estates, ships, habitats, insolvency pools, quarantine zones, protected minors, damaged AIs under stewardship review. Human law recognizes interests that cannot walk into court alone all the time. It becomes creative when money needs a body."

Calyx counsel snapped, "Director Drex is not authorized to make legal argument on behalf of the company."

"Correct," Drex said. "I am making it against the company."

The silent translator glowed steadily.

Brenn leaned over his table, typing now. "Provisional subject of relation. No. Too vague. Protected continuity subject. Better, maybe. D-17 recognized as a nonhuman continuity subject for purposes of interim protection, repair, representation, and claim suspension."

Mira heard the room hold its breath around the phrase.

Keeps-Water-Calm did not translate.

That was not rejection.

It was refusal to bless.

"Do not ask him if it is right," Sera said.

Brenn nodded slowly. "I will not."

Nadi Or said, "Ask whether it prevents the next blade."

Mira looked at the reef data. "It can, if the order also says representation is layered. Orison boundary, Choral Nomad mapping, independent science, worker safety, and court oversight. No single human office speaks for D-17. No company can settle away repair. No sensory witness can be compelled."

"And no translation duty," Sera said.

Brenn's fingers paused.

Mira looked back at Keeps-Water-Calm. "Yes. No Orison individual is required to translate, mediate, or render D-17 legible as a condition of protection."

The translator membrane dimmed.

For a moment Mira thought it had failed.

Then she realized Keeps-Water-Calm had touched the off control.

The green line went dark.

The court record captured the click.

It was the cleanest sound in the room.

Brenn said, "Let the record show Keeps-Water-Calm has withdrawn from translation. The court does not construe withdrawal as abandonment, waiver, consent to human categories, or termination of Orison relation to D-17."

Keeps-Water-Calm's attendants loosened by a fraction.

Anika exhaled. Tavo bowed his head. Drex closed his eyes. Sera's monitor tone steadied near Mira's wrist.

Mira stayed beside the sling and did not speak to fill the space.

Below them, the reef remained wounded.

For the first time since the micro-bore, human law had stopped asking the nearest Orison body to make that wound easier to file.


Chapter 42: The Injunction Draft

Brenn titled the document Pelagos Reef Injunction - Emergency Standing and Repair Order, then stared at the title until Sera asked whether he intended to intimidate the board by boring it into surrender.

"Titles matter," he said.

"Yes. That one matters like a corridor sign in a hospital during a fire."

"Suggest better."

Sera leaned against the archive table, one hand pressed to the edge because her body remained interested in gravity only as a hostile suggestion. "Pelagos Reef Injunction. Then stop. Everything after that can be a heading."

Brenn deleted five words.

"Thank you."

"Do not become dependent on my mercy."

The archive door was still locked from the outside, but the fact had become less important than it should have been. Calyx had lost practical control of the platform, if not legal control. The bloom held key corridors. Worker teams controlled manual safety points. Nadi Or kept public relay fragments alive. Drex had no command authority left worth naming, but the workers still used his rig knowledge because refusing useful guilt would have endangered them. Mira remained in the lower cradle under Sera's medical threats. Keeps-Water-Calm lay silent in the sling, translator dark.

The court had never been less formal.

It had never been more real.

Brenn opened a clean order section and began with findings, because conclusions without findings were invitations for appellate slaughter.

1. D-17 is not dormant mineral substrate.

He stopped.

Too small. Too reactive. It defined the reef by what Calyx had called it.

He changed it.

1. D-17 is a living continuity ecology maintained through mineral scaffold, symbiotic organisms, chemical and pressure-route processes, Orison relation practices, and inherited nonhuman response across time.

"Ugly," Sera said.

"Legally useful."

"I did not say remove it."

He continued.

2. Injury to D-17 includes harm to present biological function, relation-maintenance processes, future Orison access, and continuity routes not reducible to stored historical information.

3. Human actors had notice, before unauthorized cutting, that D-17 could not be treated as inert unclaimed matter: early survey caution marks, Orison warnings, Sera Kint's acoustic evidence, Nadi Or mapping, the 2384 contact-promise fragment, defensive symbiont conduct, and court-served injunction.

4. Calyx Meridian Resource Holdings proceeded with cutting after notice.

5. Worker dependence on extraction-linked oxygen credit and debt instruments materially constrained worker refusal and formed part of the mechanism by which prohibited extraction advanced.

6. Emergency removal of the jammed cutting head was repair action, not extraction, because it occurred under court mark, Orison-mediated witness, Choral Nomad mapping, worker counter-tension, no-ownership condition, and relation-preserving purpose.

He read the sixth aloud.

Mira's voice came from the lower cradle. "Add that emergency removal cannot be cited as precedent for contact permission."

Brenn added:

7. Emergency repair contact under this order creates no future right of access, sampling, extraction, survey, proof-taking, or compelled witness.

"Good," Sera said. "Now write the thing everyone will fight over."

Brenn flexed his fingers. "I know."

He wrote:

8. For purposes of interim protection, representation, repair, and claim suspension, D-17 is recognized as a nonhuman continuity subject.

The room changed around the sentence.

It was not dramatic. No alarm stopped. No door opened. No bloom withdrew in respectful acknowledgment. Calyx counsel immediately began saying words like jurisdiction, overreach, unprecedented, appeal, proprietary, chaos, and dangerous abstraction. But beneath the noise, every person on the live record understood that the sentence had crossed a boundary human law had been walking up to and away from all day.

Brenn kept typing before fear could improve him into uselessness.

9. Recognition as a nonhuman continuity subject does not classify D-17 as human, corporate, Orison person, property, archive, deity, settlement, or estate. It establishes that D-17 bears protectable interests in continued relation, repair, future capacity, and freedom from extraction or instrumental proof-taking.

10. D-17's interim representation shall be layered and nonexclusive: Orison boundary witnesses where voluntarily participating; Choral Nomad mapping witnesses; independent xenobiological and acoustic science; worker safety representatives; and court-appointed repair trustees. No single representative may waive D-17's protection, authorize extraction, settle away repair obligations, or compel Orison translation.

"Read that again," Anika said.

Brenn did.

From pylon six, Tavo said, "Worker safety representatives. Does that mean workers can stop repair if it risks us?"

"It means worker safety is part of the repair order, not an excuse to restart extraction or a cost to ignore."

"Put it less elegantly."

Brenn added:

Repair work shall not be purchased with worker injury, oxygen debt, unpaid exposure, or retaliation.

"Better," Tavo said.

Drex, still seated in the corridor, said, "Board will argue worker representatives create conflict because workers are economically dependent."

Anika turned toward his feed. "We are economically dependent because the board made breath a bill."

"Yes," Drex said. "That is their argument's weakness. Name it."

Brenn wrote:

Economic coercion created by Calyx or affiliated creditors shall not disqualify workers from safety representation; it is a subject of remedy.

Mira watched the order grow from the lower cradle. It was strange how small text looked on a screen beside a reef wound. Strange and not. A body could be larger than a law and still need the law to stop a knife. The order would not heal D-17. It might keep the next tool asleep long enough for repair to begin.

Keeps-Water-Calm's translator stayed dark.

Brenn moved to orders.

I. All extraction, claim-preservation cutting, sampling, active scanning, substrate handling, and value-preservation activity at or near D-17 is immediately enjoined.

II. All equipment that contacted D-17 shall remain disabled, sealed, and held under repair trust. No removed material may be owned, sold, analyzed for claim valuation, transferred, destroyed, or withheld from court and repair trustees.

III. Calyx Meridian Resource Holdings shall preserve and produce board communications, emergency protocol logs, executive authority records, classification acquisition files, worker debt instruments, oxygen-credit policies, Workforce Continuity Risk Modeling records, and all communications concerning terms flagged as living, memory, Orison warning, ancestor, promise, or claim fraud.

IV. Extraction-linked worker debt enforcement, oxygen-credit reduction, medical allotment retaliation, family offset acceleration, and productivity supplement penalties are stayed immediately.

V. Workers shall be offered paid transition into reef repair, platform stabilization, safety monitoring, evidence preservation, and independent survey work under court supervision, with refusal rights not tied to debt penalty.

VI. Orison participation in repair, translation, mediation, mapping, or boundary witnessing is voluntary. Refusal or withdrawal does not weaken D-17's protection.

VII. Choral Nomad vessels and maps are recognized as independent witness infrastructure for interim purposes and shall not be excluded as unauthorized alien interference.

VIII. The court appoints a provisional repair trust.

He stopped at VIII.

"Who?" Sera asked.

That was the problem. Every possible trustee was compromised by necessity. Mira had participated in the imprint and was medically shaky. Brenn was the court. Drex was implicated. Tavo and Anika were workers and witnesses. Keeps-Water-Calm had withdrawn from translation. Nadi Or refused ownership-shaped roles by instinct and practice. The board could not be allowed within sight of the word trust without adult supervision by fire.

"Layered," Mira said.

Brenn looked at her feed.

"Do not appoint one trustee. Appoint functions. Repair science lead, worker safety lead, Orison boundary seat held open and voluntary, Choral Nomad map witness, court officer, independent off-world reviewer when courier delay allows. No single chair."

"No sole authority," Sera murmured. "Different book, same disease."

Brenn did not know what she meant and ignored it productively.

He wrote:

VIII. The provisional repair trust shall operate by distributed function rather than sole chair: Dr. Mira Cael as interim repair science lead subject to medical clearance and replacement review; Anika Venn and Tavo Rusk as worker safety co-leads pending broader worker selection; Nadi Or or designated Choral Nomad witness for map custody if voluntarily accepted; an Orison boundary seat held open without penalty for nonparticipation; Yul Brenn or successor court officer for legal custody; and an independent xenobiology reviewer to be appointed upon courier confirmation.

Anika said, "I did not accept that."

"Do you refuse?" Brenn asked.

She looked around pylon six, at Tavo, the workers, the bloom, the corridor where security had stopped being a simple word. "No."

"Crew Chief Rusk?"

Tavo sighed. "No refusal."

"Nadi Or?"

"Bell Without Metal accepts map custody witness, not trust ownership, not human chair, not polite stationery."

Brenn wrote "map custody witness only."

Mira said, "Medical clearance matters."

Sera answered, "I will enjoy making you obey that."

Brenn added:

No witness, worker, or Orison participant may be required to continue beyond medical, safety, or relational withdrawal limits.

Calyx counsel, having apparently recovered enough to aim again, said, "This order destroys contract value without final adjudication."

Brenn looked directly at the camera. "The unauthorized cut destroyed the premise of contract value. The order preserves what remains."

"You are granting standing to a reef."

"Interim protection to a nonhuman continuity subject."

"That distinction will not survive review."

"Then review will have to say what word it prefers while the wound is still visible."

Counsel went silent for half a second. It was the best argument Brenn had made all day.

Drex spoke from the corridor floor. "The board will send investor command through old escrow routes. They will not wait for appeal."

"Can they restart equipment?"

"Not easily. The bloom and worker lockouts make local restart difficult. Remote financial forfeiture is easier. They can call the claim impaired, trigger debt acceleration, and attempt asset seizure through creditor court outside Pelagos."

Brenn added an emergency notice to external creditors and route authorities:

Any attempt to use financial default, creditor action, investor command, asset seizure, or labor debt acceleration to evade this injunction shall be treated as interference with court-protected repair and evidence preservation.

Sera read over his shoulder. "You know that sentence is a flare."

"Yes."

"Good."

The draft took shape over the next hour in fragments and objections. Worker remedy language from Tavo. Safety constraints from Anika. Technical boundaries from Mira. Map humility from Nadi Or, which Brenn translated into three clauses and one footnote Sera made him move into the main text. No language from Keeps-Water-Calm, and therefore several clauses protecting the right to silence. Even Drex contributed by naming corporate evasion paths, each one another small confession.

When Brenn finally reached the signature block, the order was ugly, overlong, vulnerable, and alive to more reality than the first law he had opened that morning.

He signed it.

Yul Brenn, Compact Mineral Adjudicator, Emergency Provisional Authority, Pelagos Local Docket.

The archive lights flickered.

For one bright second, the door lock changed from amber to green.

Sera stared. "Did your order just unlock us?"

The door opened ten centimeters and stopped, blocked by a red-gold symbiont stretched across the outer corridor.

Brenn looked at the organism. The organism did not move.

Nadi Or said, "The bloom has also reviewed your authority."

"And?"

"It appears to have granted partial appeal."

Sera laughed until she had to sit down.

Brenn saved the order, transmitted it to every public, court, worker, Orison, Choral Nomad, creditor, Compact, and corporate address he could reach, then printed one hard copy because some part of him remained old-fashioned enough to distrust any document that could not be dropped on a table.

Mira watched the transmission markers bloom across the network.

The reef did not heal.

Keeps-Water-Calm did not translate.

The board did not surrender.

But the next tool had a law standing in front of it now, awkward and late and named after the place it had failed to understand in time.


Chapter 43: Reef Repair

Repair began with a list of things no one was allowed to call repair.

Mira wrote it from a chair in the lower cradle because Sera had threatened three separate medical violations if she stood without permission. The chair had been dragged from an equipment alcove and was too low for the console, which meant Mira had to reach upward to type, which meant Sera could see every tremor in her hands, which meant Sera kept making noises that were technically not words and therefore harder to object to.

No extraction.

No sampling.

No claim preservation.

No proof-taking.

No touching the wound because humans needed reassurance.

No tool introduced without worker safety review, Orison boundary allowance if offered, Choral Nomad map anchor, and court record.

No person, Orison body, worker, witness, or organism used as expendable interface.

The last line made Anika say, "Good," from pylon six.

Drex said nothing.

Keeps-Water-Calm's translator remained dark. The Orison rested in the mobility sling beside the pressure window, attendants close, body colors low and difficult to read. They had not withdrawn from presence. That was the distinction human channels kept tripping over. Counsel called it nonparticipation. Brenn called it voluntary silent attendance. Nadi Or called it a reef-shaped patience humans should not flatter themselves by naming.

Mira called it a boundary.

"Repair action one," she said. "Stabilize disturbed flow around the fracture mouth without sealing it shut. D-17 is moving carrier organisms and chemical packets through temporary routes. If we block flow, we may preserve visible geometry and kill living function."

Sera leaned over the acoustic panel. "Passive mapping supports that. The fracture is not a hole. It is a bad intersection."

"Bad intersection goes in the plain-language notes."

"I was trying to be professionally elegant."

"You are often unsuccessful."

"Medical can still revoke your chair."

Mira added:

Plain-language note: The wound is less like a broken wall than a damaged crossing where living processes still need to pass.

Brenn, from the archive, said, "Usable."

Tavo, now stationed in a temporary repair command nook made of two crates and a cable drum, said, "What does stabilization use?"

"Not foam," Mira said.

"I did not say foam."

"Your platform has thirty-seven foam systems and all of them think they are helpful."

"Accepted. Not foam."

Nadi Or provided the first usable proposal: a soft current scaffold made from Patient Error's ballast vanes, the platform's disabled coolant circulation pumps at low manual rhythm, and Choral Nomad acoustic bead timing. It would not touch the lobe. It would shape water around the fracture so D-17's own carrier organisms could reestablish temporary paths without fighting turbulence from the rig, pylon wake, and human emergency systems.

Brenn asked, "Is shaping water contact?"

Everyone stared at his feed.

"I am asking because if I do not ask, counsel will," he said.

Mira answered carefully. "Yes. It is contact with the environment around D-17. It is not substrate contact. It is repair support. It must be reversible and continuously monitored."

"Add that."

She did.

The second proposal came from Anika.

"Manual shutter plates," she said.

Tavo frowned. "The pylon wake shutters are for storm load, not reef work."

"They are for changing flow."

"They move too fast."

"Not if we disconnect the powered actuators and hand-lock them at half degree increments."

Drex, still seated but no longer useless, lifted his head. "There are old worm gears behind the actuator housings. Pre-Calyx retrofit. Manual travel is slow enough."

Anika looked at him through the feed. "I was going to say that."

"You were."

"Stop being useful before I get used to it."

"I will try to disappoint you soon."

The bloom, still strung across corridor thresholds and pylon housings, shifted around the worker teams as they moved. It did not withdraw. It allowed. That difference mattered enough that Brenn made a note. Blue sheets released certain conduits and covered others. Red-gold forms stayed between hands and any device shaped like a cutter. A worker carrying a powered drill was blocked for four full minutes until he set it down and fetched a manual wrench. The bloom did not care that the drill was for a shutter panel. Pain had taught it categories sharper than intention.

"No powered drills," Tavo announced across worker channels.

A mechanic named Lio said, "It is a panel screw."

"Then we will remove it like our ancestors were bored."

"My ancestors had better tools."

"Then shame them by surviving."

The first repair movement began at 14:22 station time.

Patient Error detached from its cradle by three centimeters, still tethered, still crewless. Its passive vanes unfolded in the launch sleeve like cautious fins. Sera sent no active pulse. Nadi Or's bead timing moved through water as low pattern rather than command. Tavo's teams hand-adjusted pylon wake shutters in increments so small the platform systems flagged them as sensor drift. Anika stood at shutter bank two and counted turns aloud because worker bodies trusted voiced count more than screen count after too many screens had lied.

"One quarter. Hold. One quarter. Hold. Mira?"

Mira watched the fracture map.

At first, nothing improved.

That was almost comforting. Real repair did not behave like fiction or board dashboards. It did not reward the correct intention with immediate visible grace. Water moved. Turbulence reduced in one channel and worsened in another. Carrier organisms at the wound mouth continued their looping confusion. A red-gold boundary form slid closer to the fracture and then away again, unwilling to spend itself on a human-shaped experiment.

"Back two increments pylon six," Mira said.

Tavo relayed. "Back two."

"Patient Error vane three down four degrees."

Sera adjusted the remote. "Vane three down four. Still no active output."

"Nadi, your east bead timing is leading the carrier flow."

"Correcting by listening," Nadi Or said.

"That sounds smug."

"It is a technical mode."

Mira almost smiled. Then the fracture responded.

Not healing. Never that quickly. But one temporary carrier stream stopped looping and passed along the wound edge into a side channel Shell-That-Returns had kept open. Chemical packets reached a waiting cluster of small organisms, which moved them into the living film instead of dispersing into damaged water. The map showed a narrow line of continuity where there had been noise.

No one cheered.

Anika whispered, "There."

Keeps-Water-Calm's body changed color inside the sling.

Mira did not ask for translation. She took the change as witness and nothing more.

"Repair action one has partial success," Brenn recorded. "Temporary carrier stream reestablished along wound edge under noncontact flow stabilization."
