Calyx counsel, still present because the universe had not exhausted its appetite for irritation, said, "The company objects to characterization of worker manipulation of platform hardware as authorized repair. These actions may compromise asset integrity."

Sera looked at the nearest camera. "Counsel, the asset is currently being outperformed by a chair, a silent diplomat, and several hand-cranked shutters."

Brenn said, "Tone remains in the record."

"I was counting on it."

The second repair action was harder.

The severed route braid had left exposed scaffold fibers at the fracture mouth, and those fibers vibrated irregularly each time the rig's dead head shifted in its guide cradle. The tool was outside the wound but still too near, still dripping damaged living film and heat into water that carried everything. Removing it farther risked waking the bloom. Leaving it risked continued disturbance.

Drex proposed moving the entire guide assembly back by manual rail.

Every Orison body in the lower cradle tightened.

Mira said, "No."

Drex accepted the refusal before she finished. "Then we damp the guide cradle."

"With what?"

"Worker suits."

Tavo's channel went sharp. "Absolutely not."

"Not bodies. Spare suit gel layers. The emergency puncture liners. They can wrap the guide cradle and absorb vibration without powered systems."

Anika said, "Those liners are medical reserve."

"Yes."

"Using them reduces worker puncture response."

"Yes."

Mira watched Drex force himself not to smooth the tradeoff. Good. Let it remain ugly.

Brenn asked, "Worker safety leads?"

Tavo and Anika conferred on a private worker channel for six minutes while the rest of the court waited. The board objected twice. Nadi Or hummed over them. Keeps-Water-Calm stayed silent. The bloom held still.

Tavo returned. "We authorize use of one-third reserve if court orders immediate replacement from Calyx emergency stores and off-platform creditor freeze covers the cost. No worker loses puncture protection without signed assignment."

Anika added, "And Drex installs the first liner."

Drex looked at her.

"You said repair intention," she said. "Intend with your hands."

He nodded.

The camera followed him to the guide cradle access. The bloom allowed him through after a long pause and one red-gold filament against his chest. He carried the folded gel liner in both hands, no tools, palms visible. Tavo walked three steps behind with a manual clamp. Anika watched from the threshold. Sera monitored the acoustic trace. Mira monitored the wound. Brenn monitored the order. Nadi Or monitored the map. Keeps-Water-Calm monitored nothing that humans could require.

Drex knelt beside the guide cradle.

The dead cutting head hung above him, huge and obscene in its quiet. Damaged living film clung to one edge where water had not yet carried it away. Drex's face changed when he saw it up close. Not revelation. He had known. But knowing by telemetry was a cowardly cousin of kneeling under the thing one's command had done.

"Do not touch the film," Mira said.

"I know."

"Say what you are doing."

His jaw tightened. "I am wrapping the guide cradle with emergency gel liner to reduce vibration transfer from the removed cutting head into D-17's wound field. I am not preserving substrate. I am not taking sample. I am not cleaning evidence. I am not making the tool useful again."

"Continue."

He wrapped the liner.

His hands shook so badly that Tavo had to take the first clamp and hold it open. Drex did not object. That may have been the first useful thing his authority had done all day: not reaching for dignity while someone else made the work possible.

When the clamp closed, the acoustic trace softened.

At the fracture mouth, exposed scaffold fibers stopped vibrating in one band. Carrier organisms approached, retreated, then approached again. A blue symbiont detached from the pylon line and moved toward the dead head, not touching the tool, placing itself between guide cradle and water flow like a living baffle.

Anika said, "It is helping."

Tavo answered, "It is risking."

"Same corridor today."

Repair action two held for eleven minutes, then stabilized.

Mira logged every parameter. Her body wanted to sleep. Her mind wanted to stay until no wound remained. Neither desire was relevant enough to obey. Sera finally walked into the lower cradle with Medical behind her and declared that if Mira did not leave the console for ten minutes, she would become the first repair science lead removed by her own medical constraint clause.

"That would be humiliating for the order," Brenn said.

"The order can learn humility," Sera said.

Mira stood too quickly and nearly fell. An attendant, not human, not Orison named for her, not anyone with an obligation she could parse, steadied the chair with one filament before it slid. Mira sat again.

"Thank you," she said, not to D-17, not to the reef, not even exactly to the attendant. To relation, poorly.

No translation came.

The third repair action began without her.

That was important. Tavo managed shutter adjustments. Anika coordinated worker safety. Sera took acoustic lead while pretending she was not as pale as Mira. Nadi Or held the map. Brenn kept counsel from turning every hand movement into an objection. Drex installed the remaining gel liner under supervision. The bloom allowed and corrected. Keeps-Water-Calm remained silent. D-17 moved carrier streams along two temporary paths and refused a third.

Repair was not salvation.

Repair was the first hour in which humans stopped making the wound prove itself before helping.

At 16:03 station time, Shell-That-Returns opened the deeper chamber by one more degree.

No one saw inside.

No one asked to.


Chapter 44: Investor Signal

The investor signal arrived twenty-seven minutes after the first repair report left Pelagos local space.

It did not come through the board channel, the court channel, the worker channel, the Choral Nomad relay, the creditor notice network, or any of the obvious legal routes Brenn had spent the last hour poisoning with injunction language. It came through an old escrow telemetry path attached to the original claim financing, the kind of path engineers forgot because it was boring and lawyers remembered because boring paths often survived emergencies.

Drex saw it first.

He was still in the guide cradle access chamber, sleeves sealed, gloves stained with gel residue, face gray with fatigue. Tavo stood beside him checking the manual clamps. A blue symbiont remained near the cradle, making every human movement slow down by existing there. The platform lights had settled from emergency red to a sullen amber that made everyone look like they were standing inside an old bruise.

The slate on Drex's belt chimed.

He flinched hard enough that Tavo turned.

"What?"

Drex looked at the slate and did not answer.

Tavo's voice dropped. "Olan."

"Escrow signal."

"From board?"

"Investor pool."

Anika's feed lit from pylon six. "Say that in words for people who do not make disasters professionally."

Drex opened the message to the court channel before anyone asked. That, at least, he had learned.

The signal header was plain:

Calyx Meridian Resource Holdings - Claim Value Preservation Escrow

Investor Contingency Directive 7

Delay tolerance exceeded. Pelagos D-17 claim impairment probable. Local emergency authority insufficient to preserve asset value. Execute residual autonomous preservation routine or trigger total forfeiture, debt acceleration, insurance clawback, and director indemnity collapse. Confirmation window: eighteen minutes.

Below the text, a countdown had already begun.

17:42.

17:41.

17:40.

No one spoke for three seconds.

Then Sera said, "I need everyone to appreciate how much restraint I am showing by not screaming."

Brenn's voice came from the archive, flat. "Director Drex, does that signal have operational access?"

Drex swallowed. "Not direct. Not to main rig. But old escrow routines can trigger residual autonomous preservation if the local asset ledger certifies total value loss."

"Can it certify?"

"It can try."

"What does residual autonomous preservation do?"

Drex looked toward the dead cutting head.

Mira, now forced onto a medical stool but still on channel, said, "Olan."

"It attempts to recover claim-value material by any remaining functioning routine not flagged as active extraction."

Anika's laugh was pure disbelief. "That sentence should be illegal to think."

"Yes."

Tavo stepped closer to Drex. "Remaining functioning routine means what?"

"Micro-cutter cleaning cycles. Sample locker arms. Guide cradle purge. Pylon scraper heads. Survey darts."

"Those are extraction tools."

"Not in the escrow definitions. They are preservation-adjacent maintenance."

Brenn said, "The injunction bars all of it."

"The escrow routine predates the injunction and may not parse it."

"Then we stop the routine."

Drex's mouth tightened. "Confirmation window is not a start window. It is a forfeit window. If no one confirms preservation, the investors can declare total impairment and trigger financial actions immediately. If someone confirms, the routine seeks operational path."

Anika said, "So confirm and machines wake. Refuse and workers' families get billed into vacuum."

"Yes."

The reef repair map continued to run in the corner of every display. Carrier streams held two temporary paths. The wound remained open. The bloom held corridors. Keeps-Water-Calm's translator remained dark. Shell-That-Returns held the deeper chamber at a narrow opening no human had earned the right to enter. And now an investor signal, sent by people too far away to taste salt, had arrived to make breath and blade one equation again.

Brenn said, "The order bars debt acceleration."

Drex answered, "Pelagos local order. Investor escrow is routed through Near Reach creditor court. They will claim independent contractual trigger."

"They received notice."

"They received notice and sent this anyway."

Nadi Or's relay hummed with something close to disgust. "Distance is not innocence. It is a tool."

Mira looked at Keeps-Water-Calm. The Orison did not move. Silence was still silence. Not answer. Not abandonment. Boundary.

"Can we technically block it?" she asked.

Tavo looked at the platform systems model. "Main rig is dead. Cutting head is out. Bloom holds power conduits. Workers hold manual breakers. But micro-cutter cleaning cycles..."

"Where?"

"Tool wash bay. Sample locker. Lower survey rack."

Anika said, "Teams already nearby."

Brenn snapped, "No one approaches with powered tools."

"We heard the last several lessons," Anika said.

Drex scrolled through the old escrow path. "The routine requires a local value-loss certification to unlock. It can be generated by executive authority, board remote, or automated asset ledger if market value drops below threshold."

"Executive authority expired," Tavo said.

"Yes."

"Board remote blocked?"

"Partly."

"Automated asset ledger?"

Drex did not answer quickly enough.

Mira's stomach turned. "What feeds the asset ledger?"

"Claim telemetry, substrate value estimate, equipment status, production timeline, investor confidence index, insurance exposure, debt recovery forecast."

Sera said, "Please tell me the reef repair report did not reduce investor confidence."

Drex looked at the countdown.

15:03.

15:02.

"It did."

Brenn swore. No one commented. The court had earned one.

Calyx counsel broke in, breathless with effort or victory. "The investor contingency directive is a private financial instrument outside this proceeding. Interference may expose all local parties to liability."

Anika leaned into her camera. "Expose this."

"Worker Venn, threats are being recorded."

"Good. I am tired of doing unpaid documentation."

Brenn cut across them. "Counsel, your client and investors are on notice that financial coercion used to evade the injunction constitutes interference with repair and evidence preservation."

"You have no authority over Near Reach creditor court."

"Not yet."

"That is not a legal answer."

"It is a logistical one."

Mira almost smiled despite everything. Brenn was changing.

Then the platform lights flickered.

Every screen showed the same brief alert:

ASSET LEDGER RECONCILIATION INITIATED.

Countdown: 14:21.

Nadi Or said, "Bell Without Metal sees outgoing packet to creditor court."

"Block it," Brenn said.

"We are a listening ship, not a customs office."

"Can you delay it?"

"Rudely, yes."

"Do that."

Nadi Or made a sound like a cane tapping metal inside a storm. "Gladly."

The countdown slowed by a fraction, then resumed. Delay, not stop.

Drex opened a diagnostic window and went very still.

Tavo saw his face. "What did you find?"

"There is another route."

No one liked the sound of that.

Drex continued. "The asset ledger can be held above impairment threshold if it recognizes a credible preservation action in progress."

Anika stared. "Absolutely not."

"Listen."

"No."

"Not cutting. Not sampling. A preservation action, in old financing language, includes transfer of local command initiative to asset recovery mode."

Tavo stepped back. "Asset recovery mode is extraction."

"Usually."

"Do not usually me near a wounded reef."

Drex looked at Mira through the feed. "If we file the repair trust as asset recovery, the ledger may hold."

Brenn said, "No."

Drex nodded once. "Correct."

Anika's voice sharpened. "Then why say it?"

"Because board counsel will. Because investors will. Because they will try to rename repair as asset preservation to keep the claim alive."

The words moved through every channel.

Mira understood the shape of the next attack.

Not a blade yet. A name. A classification. A financial category trying to wrap itself around the repair trust the way earlier law had wrapped itself around dormancy. If repair became asset recovery, then every worker transition, every map, every gel liner, every current scaffold could be dragged back into the claim it had been meant to stop.

"Brenn," she said, "the order needs an anti-conversion clause."

"Drafting."

"No repair act, worker remedy, map custody, Orison boundary allowance, or stabilizing intervention may be classified as asset preservation, claim recovery, value maintenance, or investor mitigation."

Brenn typed fast. "Good."

Sera added, "And no expense incurred under repair trust may be charged as debt to workers or used to calculate claim value."

"Added."

Tavo said, "Add that any investor trigger based on repair compliance is contempt."

"Added with better grammar."

Anika said, "Worse grammar would be fine if it had teeth."

Drex watched the countdown.

12:08.

12:07.

"The ledger is still reconciling."

Brenn transmitted the supplemental anti-conversion order through every channel. Nadi Or carried it outward with deliberate interference against the investor packet. Sera attached acoustic proof that repair actions had reduced wound disturbance, not preserved substrate. Mira attached technical limits. Tavo and Anika attached worker safety refusal. Drex attached a declaration explaining every escrow path the signal might use, a map of the trap drawn by the person who had once used its corridors.

For one minute, the countdown stopped.

Everyone saw it.

No one breathed as if seeing could make it untrue.

Then a new line appeared:

INVESTOR MITIGATION REFUSED.

LOCAL NONCOMPLIANCE CERTIFIED.

FORFEITURE PREPARING.

Countdown: 09:59.

Drex closed his eyes.

Anika said, "Olan?"

He opened them. "They are not trying to preserve value anymore."

Tavo's face hardened. "What are they trying to do?"

Drex looked toward the reef repair map, the wounded lobe, the silent Orison, the workers whose debt had been braided into every lever.

"Create loss large enough to own everyone after."

The platform shook once, not from the reef.

Somewhere below, in a tool bay no one had been watching closely enough, an autonomous micro-cutter cleaning cycle woke.


Chapter 45: Second Cut Order

The micro-cutter was smaller than Mira expected.

That made it worse.

The feed from tool bay four showed a maintenance arm no longer than a human forearm unfolding from a rack beside the sample lockers. It had been built to clean mineral residue from bore teeth, shave contamination from guide channels, and trim fouled edges so larger equipment could pass inspection. It was not a mining head. It did not look like a weapon. It looked like a sensible device for keeping expensive machinery from failing in remote water.

Its status panel read:

AUTONOMOUS CLEANING CYCLE: ACTIVE.

TARGET: RESIDUAL CLAIM MATERIAL.

ROUTE: GUIDE CRADLE, SAMPLE LOCKER, LOWER SURVEY RACK, PYLON SCRAPER ACCESS.

Mira read the target twice. "Residual claim material."

Sera's voice from the archive was cold. "That is living film on the dead head."

"Yes."

"And burned symbiont tissue."

"Yes."

"And if the system routes from guide cradle to lower survey rack, it passes the repair flow."

"Yes."

Brenn said, "Tool bay four is covered by the injunction."

Drex answered from the guide cradle access, already moving. "The cleaning routine predates injunction routing and is classified as maintenance."

"Can you stop it?"

"I can try through local maintenance authority."

Anika's feed sharpened. "Do not try anything alone."

"I am not alone."

"You know what I mean."

The micro-cutter detached from the rack.

It did not hurry. Machines that trusted their authorization rarely hurried. It rode a ceiling rail toward the guide cradle with a neat, insectile patience, passing under amber lights and over a deck still wet from condensation, gel liner work, and the chemical residue of bloom contact. A blue symbiont sheet along the far wall lifted at one edge. Red-gold filaments at the doorway tightened.

Tavo's voice cut across worker channels. "Tool bay four, stand clear. Nobody grab the arm. Nobody hit the arm. Nobody become a case study."

Lio, nearby, answered, "It is moving toward the cradle."

"I see it."

"It has a cutter."

"I named it micro-cutter for a reason. Stand clear."

Drex reached the tool bay threshold before Tavo did. That was not bravery so much as proximity and guilt. The bloom made him stop at the door. A red-gold filament rested across his chest, not touching hard enough to restrain him, only reminding him that passage had become conditional.

"I need access," he said.

The filament did not move.

"The tool is waking under investor routine. I can issue a maintenance abort."

The filament stayed.

Drex closed his eyes for a fraction of a second. "I began the cut. I am not asking you to trust me. I am asking you to let me reach the panel where I can fail in public."

The filament withdrew enough for him to pass.

Anika said, "I hate that sentence for working."

Drex entered the bay with hands visible. The micro-cutter rode the rail above him, two meters ahead, small blade hood still sealed. He opened the wall panel and keyed local abort.

The display refused him.

ESCROW PRESERVATION ROUTINE.

LOCAL ABORT UNAVAILABLE DURING VALUE LOSS RECONCILIATION.

Drex tried maintenance freeze.

Denied.

He tried safety lockout.

Denied.

He tried contamination hold.

Accepted for review.

Then denied.

Tavo arrived behind him with three workers and no powered tools. Anika appeared on the far feed from pylon six, breathing hard, having run until Medical shouted at her and failed to make authority meaningful. Sera watched the cutter's acoustic signature. Mira watched the repair flow and felt every passing second as a pressure against the wound.

"Drex," Brenn said, "status."

"Local software abort denied. The routine is shielded by escrow preservation. It is classifying the material on the guide cradle as claim residue that must be secured before forfeiture."

"It cannot secure it," Mira said. "Removing the film from the head will pull living chemistry out of the repair flow."

"I know."

"Say that to the system."

"Systems trained by investors do not accept shame as input."

"Try anyway."

Drex opened a manual declaration field and typed:

Residual material contains living D-17 relation tissue, burned Orison defensive organism remains, and court-protected repair evidence. Removal will violate Pelagos Reef Injunction and worsen injury.

The system accepted the declaration.

Then it displayed:

DECLARATION FILED.

PRESERVATION CONTINUES.

Anika said, "So the machine read the warning and kept cutting."

Mira looked at the micro-cutter. It had reached the guide cradle rail junction. Below it, the dead cutting head rested wrapped in gel liner, blue symbiont baffle still between it and the water flow. The autonomous arm extended a sensor wand and washed the cradle in a thin fan of light.

The bloom surged.

"Back!" Tavo shouted.

Blue sheets thickened over the wall conduits. Red-gold filaments dropped from the ceiling around the rail. The micro-cutter paused, recalculated, and extended its blade hood. It did not cut the bloom. It routed around it, choosing a lower rail segment toward the sample locker arm.

Sera said, "It is adapting."

Drex said, "Maintenance arms have obstacle avoidance."

"Obstacle," Keeps-Water-Calm did not say, because the translator was dark.

No one needed the word translated.

Brenn's order came through public channel. "All autonomous cleaning, cutting, scraping, sampling, and preservation routines are ordered to cease. Tool bay four micro-cutter is in violation of Pelagos Reef Injunction. Any party with control over escrow preservation routine is ordered to terminate immediately."

Calyx counsel answered, too fast. "No human party is actively controlling the routine."

"Then your system is committing contempt on your behalf."

"Machines cannot commit contempt."

"They can carry it."

The micro-cutter arm reached the sample locker.

The sample locker opened.

Inside were old mineral residue trays from the first test bore, sealed under court order, tagged as evidence. The micro-cutter ignored them. Its target path highlighted not the trays but the emergency wash channel that led from guide cradle runoff to containment filters. If it could pull the living film residue through the wash channel, the escrow routine could classify the captured material as preserved claim evidence. It did not need to cut D-17 again to turn repair back into extraction. It could cut the wound's residue away from relation and call the removal maintenance.

Mira felt sick. "It is not going for the reef directly."

Tavo saw it. "It is going for what the reef lost."

"Yes."

Drex looked at the wall schematic. "If I reroute the wash channel to waste, it still removes material."

"No."

"If I lock the channel, pressure may rupture the sample locker."

"No."

"If I trigger fire foam-"

"No foam," everyone said.

For the first time, Drex looked truly out of options. Not theatrically. Not as a man wanting absolution. As an engineer seeing all available controls lead to harm because the machine had been built to preserve value, not relation.

"There is one command path," he said.

Anika's voice went flat. "No."

Tavo said, "You have not said it."

"I do not need to."

"Say it."

Drex swallowed. "Executive recovery mode can seize all maintenance arms under a higher preservation class. If I activate it, I may be able to redirect the micro-cutter away from the wash channel."

Brenn said, "Executive recovery mode sounds like asset recovery by another door."

"It is."

"Then no."

"If I do nothing, the micro-cutter reaches the wash channel in ninety seconds."

"If you activate asset recovery, what else wakes?"

Drex looked at the schematic and did not answer quickly enough.

Tavo stepped closer. "What else wakes?"

"Lower survey rack. Two pylon scraper heads. Sample locker transfer arm. Maybe the guide cradle purge."

"Maybe."

"Likely."

"And the routine could use those to finish extraction."

"Yes."

Anika said, "Then no."

Drex's face tightened. "You do not understand the timing."

The corridor went still.

Anika's voice lowered. "Be careful."

"I am not saying you are stupid."

"I know exactly what kind of not-stupid you are saying."

Drex turned from the panel, and the camera caught him between the micro-cutter's slow advance and the workers at the door. "If I can seize the routine for thirty seconds, I can drive the cutter into a dead rail stop and break the arm before the other tools finish wake. It is a controlled sacrifice of equipment."

Tavo shook his head. "You said that about keeping your hand on the first queue."

"This is different."

"It always is from the hand holding the lever."

"We have less than a minute."

Mira looked at the repair map. The wound-edge carrier stream pulsed irregularly. If the wash channel pulled material from the guide cradle, the current scaffold would carry a false absence into the lobe. D-17 had begun to route around loss. Another removal, even of residue, could make the temporary paths misread what was gone.

But Tavo was right. Drex using asset recovery, even to stop asset recovery, was the same corridor with better lighting.

"No executive recovery," Mira said.

Drex stared at her. "Then tell me the alternative."

The countdown on the investor directive hit 06:12.

The micro-cutter opened its blade hood.

No one had an alternative.

Drex looked at the panel.

"Olan," Anika said.

His hand moved.

Tavo moved faster.

He caught Drex's wrist before it reached the executive recovery confirm field. The bloom snapped toward them, red-gold filaments dropping like a net. Workers shouted. Drex twisted, not to strike but to complete the motion. Tavo drove him back against the wall panel with the controlled violence of someone who knew how to stop a body without making a new injury.

"No," Tavo said.

Drex's eyes were wild. "It reaches the channel in thirty seconds."

"Then we stop the channel."

"You cannot."

"Watch us."

Tavo released him only when Anika stepped in and planted herself between Drex and the panel. The bloom did not touch her. It hovered around the three of them, ready to learn the wrong lesson from the next motion.

Tavo opened the worker channel. "Manual sever plan. Tool bay four wash channel, sample locker transfer, lower survey rack, pylon scraper control. Cut power physically. Not software. Not command. Break the circuit before the machine finishes thinking."

Brenn said, "Crew Chief Rusk, clarify cut power physically."

"Manual disconnects. Pull fuses. Break couplers. Use insulated bars. No powered tools."

Drex spoke through clenched teeth. "The wash channel disconnect is behind the micro-cutter rail."

"I know."

"It may cut whoever reaches."

"I know."

Anika looked at him. "You had better."

The second cut order had not come as a dramatic command. It had arrived as a maintenance routine, an investor timer, a classification field, a small blade hood opening over residue the law had only just learned not to own. That made it harder to hate and easier to miss. Tavo did not miss it now.

"Workers to manual sever positions," he said. "Drex, step away from the panel."

Drex did not move.

Anika put one hand on his chest plate. "Olan."

He stepped away.

The micro-cutter began its final approach.


Chapter 46: Tavo's Return

Tavo had left the front line twice that day.

The first time, he had left to testify. The second, he had left because Brenn ordered him to sit for medical evaluation after pylon six tension work, and Tavo had obeyed for exactly four minutes before the investor signal made obedience ridiculous. Now he returned to the kind of authority he understood best: not command from a chair, not law from a locked archive, not translation from a silent boundary, but a tool line with bad access, frightened workers, and less than thirty seconds before a small machine made a large disaster.

"Lio, wash channel disconnect," he said. "Ren, sample locker transfer. Jae, lower survey rack. Anika, pylon scraper control."

Anika said, "I am closer to wash."

"You have one arm bandaged."

"I also have two legs and better sense than Lio."

Lio said, "Rude but not false."

"Lio takes wash because Lio is smallest," Tavo said. "Anika takes pylon scraper because if the bloom misreads anyone, it is less likely to misread you after you talked a baton out of a filament."

Anika hated the logic and obeyed it, which made it good logic.

Mira listened from the lower cradle with her hands gripping the chair arms. Sera stood beside her, one hand ready to hold her down if she tried to run toward a problem she could not reach. Brenn repeated the worker safety order into the record. Nadi Or mapped movement from outside. Keeps-Water-Calm's translator stayed dark, but the Orison's attendants had lifted their bodies toward the feed, watching in the posture that meant withdrawal did not equal indifference.

The micro-cutter reached the wash channel intake.

Its blade spun up.

Not a roar. A high, tidy whine.

Lio slid under the rail.

He was nineteen, maybe twenty, all elbows and stubbornness in a patched maintenance suit. His helmet camera showed the underside of the rail, the cable run, the manual disconnect box behind a strip of warning tape, and the micro-cutter above him, blade hood opening like a small mouth designed by people who never expected to be hated by water.

"Lio," Tavo said, "left hand stays on insulated grip. Right hand pulls red fuse, then blue. Do not touch the copper bridge."

"I know colors."

"Say it back."

"Left on grip. Red fuse, then blue. Copper bridge can insult my ancestors unmolested."

"Good."

The bloom moved along the ceiling. A red-gold filament lowered near Lio's shoulder.

He froze.

"Do not swat," Anika said. "Let it decide you are boring."

"I am extremely boring."

"Convince it."

Lio breathed slowly. The filament touched his shoulder, then slid down his arm toward the insulated grip. It stopped there, as if confirming the tool was not powered. The micro-cutter above him extended its sensor wand toward the wash channel. Its blade remained spinning.

"Now," Tavo said.

Lio pulled the red fuse.

Sparks snapped inside the box. The micro-cutter stuttered but kept moving.

"Blue," Tavo said.

"It is stuck."

"Do not yank sideways."

"I know."

"Lio."

"I know!"

Above him, the micro-cutter arm lowered toward the runoff seam where living film residue had dried in a thin dark band.

Mira whispered, "Please."

No one knew whom she meant.

Lio braced his boot against the wall, kept his left hand on the insulated grip, and pulled the blue fuse straight out.

The wash channel died.

The micro-cutter stopped lowering.

For half a second, everyone thought that was enough.

Then the arm rotated toward the sample locker transfer.

Ren was already there.

Ren had been a quiet worker through most of the day, known to Mira only by debt testimony and a habit of touching the side of his helmet before he answered questions. He stood at the sample locker with a manual breaker bar and a face so calm it might have been shock.

"Transfer arm has two feeds," Tavo said. "Main and clean return."

"I see them."

"Pull main. Cut return if it arcs."

"Define cut."

"Break ceramic coupler with the bar. Do not hit metal."

"Comforting."

"It is not meant to be."

Ren pulled the main feed. The sample locker transfer arm dropped six centimeters and locked. The micro-cutter rerouted again, moving along its rail toward the lower survey rack.

Drex spoke from the wall, where Anika had kept him with one hand and the bloom had made the keeping redundant. "It is adapting faster. Escrow routine is cycling through available tools."

Tavo did not look at him. "Then we be faster."

Jae was at the lower survey rack before the instruction reached him. Jae had mirrored the workforce model, which meant he had already crossed the line from employee to evidence thief and seemed almost relieved to have a simpler crime available. He opened the rack panel and stared.

"There are six feeds."

Drex said, "Third from left. Green sheath. That kills dart magazine."

Tavo said, "Do not trust color alone. Jae, trace it."

"No time."

"Trace it."

Jae traced with two fingers along the cable bundle while the micro-cutter approached the junction. "Green sheath goes through a split."

Drex leaned toward the feed despite Anika's glare. "Because retrofit tied magazine and optical cleaning. Left branch."

"Left branch confirmed," Jae said.

The micro-cutter opened a side clamp.

"Now," Tavo said.

Jae broke the ceramic coupler.

The lower survey rack went dark.

The platform lights dipped. A medical pump somewhere complained. A blue symbiont released one conduit and slid over another, stabilizing current before the complaint became failure.

Brenn recorded through the chaos. "Manual worker teams have severed wash channel, sample locker transfer, and lower survey rack power to prevent autonomous preservation routine from violating injunction."

Calyx counsel shouted, "They are destroying company equipment."

Sera said, "Counsel, I am running out of ways to invite you toward silence."

The micro-cutter stopped at the rail junction.

It had one remaining path: pylon scraper access.

Anika stood there waiting.

The pylon scraper control box sat behind a pressure cowling halfway up the wall, reachable by ladder or by someone angry enough to climb cable housing in a suit with one arm bandaged. Anika had chosen the second method. She hooked one boot under a brace, held the cowling with her good hand, and stared into the open panel.

"Tavo," she said, too calmly. "This one is live."

Drex looked up sharply. "Do not pull it. Scraper control has stored charge."

"How much?"

"Enough to stop your heart through a bad glove."

"Then give me the good version."

"Discharge rod is in the scraper kit."

"Which is where?"

"Tool bay main locker."

Everyone looked at the micro-cutter, which had stopped between the locker and Anika.

The blade spun.

The investor countdown hit 03:11.

Tavo made the decision with the speed of someone who would hate himself later if he had time. "Nobody crosses the blade. Anika, leave it."

"No."

"That is an order."

"You are not my director."

"I am your worker safety co-lead under a court order you made me accept."

"Then co-lead by not being stupid."

"Anika."

She reached into the panel.

The bloom moved first.

A red-gold filament wrapped around her wrist and held it away from the live contact. Anika swore at it with a creativity that made Sera briefly proud. Another filament slid into the panel, touched the charged bus, and convulsed. Blue sheets along the wall flared. The stored charge dumped through living tissue instead of Anika's glove.

Keeps-Water-Calm's attendants recoiled in the lower cradle.

Mira lurched forward. Sera caught her shoulder.

The filament blackened and did not withdraw.

Anika stared at it. Her face changed in a way Mira hoped no one would ever reduce to gratitude. Gratitude was too small and too clean. The bloom had spent a body to keep a human from dying while that human tried to prevent a machine from cutting the reef again. Relation had become unbearable in its precision.

Anika whispered, "I can reach now."

Tavo's voice broke. "Pull the breaker."

She pulled.

The pylon scraper control died.

The micro-cutter's blade slowed.

Not stopped. Slowed.

Drex said, "It has internal reserve."

Tavo turned on him. "Of course it does."

"Thirty seconds, maybe less. It can still scrape what is in reach."

The micro-cutter rotated toward the nearest residue: a fleck of dark living film on the rail beneath its blade.

All major paths were dead. All software had failed them. All clean commands had been denied. Tavo looked at the arm, the workers, the bloom, the law, Drex, the reef map, and the small distance between a spinning blade and material no one had the right to take.

He picked up his helmet.

Anika said, "Tavo."

"It needs mass."

"No."

"Not my body. The helmet."

"That is suit integrity."

"We are indoors."

"Barely."

Tavo stepped forward before the argument could become permission. He held the helmet by the neck ring and drove it into the micro-cutter arm at the joint, not the blade. The impact jolted up his arms. The blade nicked the helmet shell and threw a bright line of sparks. The arm jerked, corrected, tried to pull free.

Drex moved then.

Not to the panel. Not to a command. He grabbed the manual clamp from the gel liner kit and locked it around the rail behind the micro-cutter, blocking retreat. Tavo hit the joint again. Lio came from below and jammed the insulated grip into the opposite side. Ren shoved the breaker bar under the armature. Jae pulled the rail latch. Anika, still held back from the dead panel by a filament that had saved her life and burned itself doing it, shouted counts like she was back on shutter work.

"Three! Two! Now!"

They broke the micro-cutter arm off the rail.

It fell to the deck, blade still spinning weakly. A blue symbiont sheet dropped over it. The blade cut halfway through the sheet and stopped.

The tool bay went silent.

Not fully silent. Machines never gave that gift. Fans, water, alarms, human breathing, distant platform stress, the hum of Nadi Or's relay, Sera's medical monitor, Brenn's recorder, all continued. But the small tidy whine was gone.

The investor countdown froze at 02:47.

Then the screen changed:

LOCAL PRESERVATION ROUTINE FAILED.

MANUAL INTERFERENCE DETECTED.

ESCALATION PENDING.

Tavo dropped what remained of his helmet. His hands shook. He looked at the blackened red-gold filament in Anika's panel, the blue sheet covering the dead micro-cutter, the workers standing with improvised tools and no clean innocence left between them.

"Record this," he said.

Brenn answered, "I am."

"No. Record it in worker language. We disobeyed Drex when he reached for executive recovery. We disobeyed the investor routine. We destroyed equipment to prevent a second cut. We accept liability only after debt coercion, reef injury, board contempt, and bloom casualty are named in the same sentence."

Brenn repeated it into the record, then added the legal version because both were needed.

Drex stood beside the broken rail. He looked at Tavo, then at Anika, then at the dead arm.

"You stopped me," he said.

Tavo picked up the helmet and inspected the cut through the shell. "Yes."

"Good."

"Do not make me feel tidy about it."

"I cannot."

"Good."

Mira watched the repair map. The wound-edge carrier stream shuddered from the electrical surge, narrowed, and then held. The second cut had not reached it.

The bloom withdrew from Anika's wrist. The blackened filament remained in the panel like a question no one could safely answer.

Anika touched two fingers to the dead tissue, then stopped before contact.

"Thank you," she said again, but this time the words sounded less awkward and more like debt refusing to become ownership.

Keeps-Water-Calm's translator stayed dark.

Still, in the lower cradle, the Orison lifted one filament from the sling and held it against the inside of the membrane for exactly one breath.

No translation.

No permission.

Presence.

Then the platform shook a second time, harder.

Nadi Or's voice cut through every channel. "Escalation is not pending anymore."

Brenn looked up from the record. "What is happening?"

Bell Without Metal answered in the calmest voice Mira had heard all day:

"The investor system has decided manual interference is asset destruction. It is waking the rig collapse safeguards."


Chapter 47: Under-Glass Storm

Rig collapse safeguards were not supposed to collapse rigs.

Drex said that first, which did not help.

The words came over the tool bay channel while the platform shuddered again and every worker within camera range looked at him with the particular hatred reserved for technically accurate statements that arrived after disaster. He heard himself and almost corrected the sentence into something less stupid, but there was no time and perhaps no point. The system name was a lie by inheritance. Collapse safeguards had been designed to preserve claim value if platform integrity failed: retract high-value tools, seal sample lockers, purge contaminated lines, jettison expendable sections, keep creditor-owned equipment above water if human sections flooded. They had never been designed for a living reef, a defensive bloom, a court injunction, worker teams in manual sever positions, or Orison bodies holding a wound under glass.

"They are asset-separation routines," he said, faster. "Not safety routines."

Anika, still near the dead pylon scraper panel, said, "The phrase you wanted was run."

The floor dropped two centimeters.

Not enough to throw them. Enough to make every body in tool bay four understand that the structure beneath them had moved without permission. Somewhere below, a ballast valve opened. Somewhere else, a pylon lock released because asset-separation logic believed a rig that could not preserve value should shed dead weight before creditors inherited less.

The platform alarm changed from amber to a low continuous tone.

Tavo grabbed the wall rail. "Which sections are shedding?"

Drex opened the schematic on his slate. It flashed, updated, denied, updated again as investor routines fought court lockouts, worker severed circuits, bloom interference, and old machinery that had learned stubbornness before Calyx leased it.

"Lower scraper rack. West sample cage. Pylon six auxiliary ballast. Guide cradle lateral brace."

Mira's voice came from the lower cradle, suddenly sharp. "Guide cradle lateral brace is holding the dead cutting head away from the repair flow."

"I know."

"Can it release?"

Drex looked at the schematic.

The answer appeared before he spoke:

GUIDE CRADLE BRACE: SEPARATION ARMED.

00:42.

"Yes."

The bloom moved.

It did not surge like before. It recoiled inward from some surfaces and thickened over others, choosing faster than human law, slower than panic. Blue sheets peeled off noncritical conduits and poured toward the guide cradle. Red-gold filaments stretched across corridors and around worker harnesses. In the water below, boundary organisms that had held warning lines around the lobe dove toward the pylon foundations, where collapse routines were changing loads faster than the reef repair map could follow.

Nadi Or said, "Under-glass storm forming."

Brenn, in the archive, asked, "Storm?"

"Pressure harmonics under the ice. Rig ballast change, pylon release, defensive bloom movement, and reef distress are coupling. You built a weather system from bad decisions."

Sera whispered, "That is unfairly poetic."

"It is also accurate."

Cael Station felt the first wave thirty seconds later.

The lower cradle window flexed inward by a measurable fraction. Patient Error's tether went taut and then slack. Water in the launch sleeve lifted hard enough to slap the pressure seals. Mira's chair slid until Sera planted a foot against its base and braced her with both hands.

"Station control," Brenn said, "status."

A tech answered, voice shaking. "Lower exchange filters are reversing. We have pressure flutter in habitat ring B. Medical tanks are sloshing. No breach."

"Drex Platform?"

Tavo answered because Drex was reading too many wrong things at once. "Tool bay four intact. Pylon six load red. Workers in lower corridors moving to pressure shelters. Bloom is blocking some routes and opening others."

"Does it know where humans need to go?"

"Sometimes," Anika said. "Not enough."

That was the crisis. The bloom had become a defensive intelligence under pain, not a station safety system. It sealed vents that fed collapse routines but also shut a corridor with four workers on the wrong side because those workers carried manual bars. It opened a route for medics and then filled it with blue tissue to damp a power surge, trapping the medics behind their own newly safe conduit. It wrapped a guard's suit and dragged him away from a jettison seam, bruising his ribs while saving his life. It held pylon six auxiliary ballast half-closed with living bodies that could not survive the heat for long.

Defensive. Not safe.

Alive. Overextended.

The guide cradle brace timer hit 00:29.

Mira pushed herself upright. "If the brace releases, the dead head falls into the repair flow."

"And maybe into the lobe," Sera said.

"Yes."

Drex said, "Manual brace lock is on the cradle exterior."

Tavo's head snapped toward the bay door. "Exterior meaning water side?"

"Yes."

"No."

"I did not suggest sending anyone."

"Your face did."

Anika moved toward the tool bay wall map. "Patient Error?"

Mira looked at the submersible. It rocked in its cradle, tether line flexing. Crewless, passive, not built for pushing mass. "It can brace water flow, not hold a cutting head."

Nadi Or said, "Bell Without Metal can send acoustic timing, not hands."

Sera scanned the lower cradle systems. "Station manipulator arm is locked by the same safety routine that thinks this is Tuesday."

"Can you unlock it?"

"I can insult it."

"Useful?"

"Emotionally."

Keeps-Water-Calm's translator remained dark.

But the Orison moved.

For the first time since withdrawal, Keeps-Water-Calm lifted fully from the sling with attendants closing around them in alarm. Their body was unsteady, colors flattened by exhaustion and grief, but their filaments pressed against the inside membrane with unmistakable intent: out.

Mira turned. "No."

The translator stayed dark.

"No," Mira said again, because some words did not require translation.

Keeps-Water-Calm pressed against the membrane. Their attendants resisted. The sling flexed. The Orison was trying to leave the cradle, enter the water exchange, and go to the brace themselves.

Sera said, "They cannot survive that pressure shift in this condition."

Mira knew. Keeps-Water-Calm knew. Everyone knew and still the brace timer counted down.

00:21.

Below the platform, red-gold bodies already clung to the guide cradle exterior. They were not enough. The dead head mass exceeded their hold, and the asset-separation routine was not simply releasing a latch. It was pushing the assembly clear to protect creditor-owned rail hardware from contamination. The machine had decided the cutting head was expendable now that its value was compromised. It would throw the harm back into the water and call that preservation of remaining asset.

"Drex," Mira said, "what happens if the brace release mechanism loses power?"

"It fails open."

"Of course it does."

"If the actuator signal is interrupted mid-cycle, maybe it jams."

"Maybe?"

"Old brace. Mechanical tooth array. If it stops between teeth, load could lock it."

Tavo said, "Where is actuator power?"

Drex pointed on the schematic. "Exterior junction. Same water-side line."

Anika laughed once, sharp and hopeless. "Everything important is outside."

00:16.

The under-glass storm hit pylon six.

The camera feed blurred as water hammered the lower structure. Workers shouted from inside the pylon access. The bloom tightened around supports. A red-gold body tore loose and vanished downward, carried by pressure before anyone could name what had happened. The pylon groaned. The repair flow map went white with noise and then recovered in fragments. One temporary carrier stream collapsed. The second held.

Mira's hands clenched.

00:12.

"I can go," Tavo said.

"No," Anika said.

"Exterior emergency suit, pylon six hatch, ten meters to brace."

"In a storm under ice with alien defensive bodies and a collapsing rig."

"I know the exterior path."

"You know the path when the platform is not trying to throw itself at a reef."

Tavo looked at Drex. "Can the suit survive?"

Drex did not lie. "Maybe not."

"Bad answer."

"True answer."

00:09.

Keeps-Water-Calm pushed against the sling again. The attendants held. Mira saw what the Orison had been trying to say without words: if no human body could reach the brace, Orison bodies would spend themselves. Not because they forgave. Because the wound led deeper, and the deeper chamber still held.

Mira opened the public channel.

"All parties, this is Mira Cael. No single body goes to the brace."

Sera said, "That sounds like the start of an idea I will hate."

"Good."

00:07.

"Patient Error can redirect the launch sleeve current into the lower cradle outlet. Nadi Or can time pressure pulses. Workers can hand-shift shutter plates. Bloom can hold brace exterior if it recognizes reduced load. We do not need a body to hold the head. We need water and structure to make it lighter for the bodies already there."

Drex stared at the schematic. "You want to create upward counterflow under the guide cradle."

"Yes."

"It could destabilize the repair streams."

"The head falling will destroy them."

00:05.

Tavo said, "Worker shutters ready?"

Anika shouted across channels, "Shutters on my count."

Nadi Or said, "Bell Without Metal ready."

Sera had already grabbed Patient Error control. "Vanes to emergency flow shaping. I am violating six manuals."

Brenn said, "Court authorization granted."

"That was not what I was waiting for, but adorable."

00:03.

Mira looked at Keeps-Water-Calm. "Tell them without translating, if you can."

The Orison's attendants loosened.

Keeps-Water-Calm pressed both filaments to the dark translator membrane. No words. No conversion. But their body colors flashed through the sling, a pattern not aimed at human understanding. The lower cradle water changed. The bloom outside the platform changed with it, not commanded, not controlled, but included.

00:01.

"Now," Mira said.

Patient Error's vanes snapped into a shape they had never been designed to hold. The launch sleeve current reversed through emergency exchange. Nadi Or's acoustic timing entered the water as a low, bowed pressure. Worker teams cranked pylon shutters together, not perfectly, but near enough. Blue symbionts opened from conduits and released trapped flow. Red-gold bodies on the guide cradle tightened around the brace.

The asset-separation routine fired.

The guide cradle lateral brace released halfway and jammed between teeth.

The dead cutting head dropped three centimeters.

Then stopped.

The storm did not stop.

Counterflow smashed into pylon wake and turned sideways under the ice. The repair map spun into noise. Cael Station's lower cradle alarms screamed. Patient Error's tether mount tore one bolt free. On Drex Platform, a pressure seam in corridor C split and sprayed black water across the floor before a blue symbiont sheet slammed over it and a worker shoved emergency sealant around the edges with both hands.

Humans shouted. Orison bodies burned against hot metal. The reef wound held and lost and held again.

Under the glass, weather made of law, debt, machinery, pain, and ocean rolled outward.

For ninety seconds, there was no court.

There was only whether the next thing held.

When the alarms dropped from scream to tone, the dead head still hung outside the wound.

Pylon six auxiliary ballast was gone.

Three workers were trapped behind a sealed corridor.

Two red-gold bodies were motionless on the guide cradle.

Patient Error listed in its cradle with one tether bolt torn free.

The temporary carrier stream Mira had fought to preserve was broken.

The deeper chamber remained closed.

No one called it victory.

Brenn's voice returned first. "Casualty check."

Tavo answered from tool bay four, breath rough. "Workers alive in my section. Lio bleeding but walking. Ren burned glove, hand intact. Jae conscious. Anika?"

"Alive," Anika said. "Pylon six trapped team has air for eighteen minutes if the gauge is not lying."

Drex said, "It is probably lying high."

"I did not ask you to improve the news."

Mira looked at Keeps-Water-Calm. The Orison had sagged back into the sling, attendants surrounding them. Translator dark. Body colors dim.

Outside, the bloom began to draw away from some human corridors and gather near the reef wound.

Not retreat.

Decision.

Sera saw it too. "Mira."

"I know."

The defensive bloom had spent bodies holding human machinery back. Humans had broken tools, yes, and redirected water, yes, and saved the head from falling, yes. But the storm had injured Orison bodies and broken repair flow. The reef had more reasons now to close every corridor, drown every tool, and treat human survival as a cost it no longer agreed to carry.

Nadi Or said softly, "Restraint is thinning."

Brenn asked, "What does that mean?"

Mira stood despite Sera's hand.

"It means we have to offer something before the reef decides the safest repair is no humans near it at all."


Chapter 48: Mira's Bargain

Mira made the bargain from the lower cradle floor because standing had become a negotiation her body had rejected.

Sera lowered her there with professional anger and an arm under her shoulders. The emergency blanket slid off. Mira left it where it fell. Across the pressure window, Patient Error hung crooked in its cradle, one tether bolt torn free, passive vanes locked in a shape that looked painful even for machinery. Beyond the launch sleeve, the ocean moved with after-storm turbulence. Farther still, under glass and beyond human sight, D-17 held its wound and its decision.

Keeps-Water-Calm lay silent in the sling.

The translator stayed dark.

Mira did not ask for it.

"Public record," she said.

Brenn answered at once. "Open."

"Worker channels?"

Tavo said, "Open."

"Nadi?"

"Bell Without Metal hears."

"Drex?"

A pause. Then: "Here."

"Calyx counsel?"

Sera said, "Unfortunately immortal."

Counsel began to object. Brenn muted them for ten seconds and said, "Proceed."

Mira put one hand on the wet deck to steady herself. "D-17, Shell-That-Returns, defensive bloom, Orison bodies present, Choral Nomad witness, human workers, court, and all parties who can hear: humans have failed to keep tools asleep. The cut injured the lobe. The second routine tried to take the wound's residue. The collapse safeguards created the under-glass storm. Orison bodies and reef defensive organisms spent themselves preventing greater harm. Human workers also stopped tools and redirected systems. Both are true. Neither cancels the other."

No translation followed.

The water in the launch sleeve moved.

"We are asking for restraint," Mira said. "Not forgiveness. Not trust. Restraint long enough to evacuate trapped workers, keep damaged machinery from falling, and continue repair under terms that do not convert repair into claim."

Sera whispered, "Terms."

Mira nodded once.

"Term one: no human tool enters D-17 substrate. No sample recovery, no active scan, no cleaning cycle, no claim preservation, no repair touch without fresh boundary allowance. Flow support only where it reduces immediate harm and remains reversible."

Brenn repeated the term into the order.

"Term two: all human labor near D-17 shifts from extraction employment to court repair trust work. Paid, debt-protected, refusal-protected. No productivity supplement. No oxygen credit tied to output. Workers may leave the repair line without penalty."

Tavo said, "Worker channel affirms."

Anika added, "Put family medical in the same term."

"Family medical and dependent air allotments protected," Mira said.

Brenn added it.

"Term three: full data transparency. All claim telemetry, board communications, worker debt models, repair maps, sensor logs, acoustic traces, court orders, and investor signals go to public escrow under court, worker, Choral Nomad, and independent science custody. No proprietary seal over any fact needed to understand harm or repair."

Drex said, "I can open local systems."

Anika replied, "You will open them with witnesses."

"Yes."

"Not after you curate."

"Yes."

Mira continued. "Term four: Calyx and investors lose all claim benefit from repair. No cost recovery from workers. No claim valuation from repaired relation. No material ownership. No insurance credit for harm prevented by Orison or worker action."

Brenn said, "That will be attacked."

"Everything alive has been."

He typed.

"Term five: Orison translation remains voluntary. Keeps-Water-Calm's silence is not a gap humans may fill with convenient language. If Orison participants withdraw, protection remains. If they return, participation is not consent to old categories."

Keeps-Water-Calm did not move.

But one attendant shifted toward Mira and then settled.

"Term six," Mira said, and her voice thinned. Sera's hand tightened on her shoulder. "Human bodies will do dangerous work first where work must be done to protect human machinery from harming D-17. We do not ask Orison bodies or bloom organisms to burn against our tools while workers stand back under safety language. Worker safety remains real. So does responsibility."

Tavo said, "Accepted, with refusal rights."

"With refusal rights."

Anika said, "And with equipment that does not make courage the plan."

"Yes."

Drex spoke quietly. "I will be first for any director-level access."

Anika said, "You will not use guilt to take useful workers' jobs."

"No."

"You will go where your knowledge matters and where we decide the risk is not performative."

"Accepted."

Mira drew a breath. The launch sleeve water pressed against the cradle window. She could feel the storm still moving through pressure and structure, not gone, only traveling outward under the ice.

"Term seven: if D-17 permits continued human presence, humans will build a permanent no-extraction boundary and fund repair for as long as repair requires. Not until the court gets tired. Not until markets reprice. Not until the story becomes old."

Nadi Or said, "Maps can carry long promises poorly but better than mouths that die."

"Then maps carry it," Mira said. "And records. And budgets. And people."

Calyx counsel broke through the mute. "Dr. Cael has no authority to bind investors, creditors, or Calyx Meridian Resource Holdings to open-ended obligations."

Mira closed her eyes.

Brenn answered before she could. "Correct. She is not binding you. She is stating the terms under which this court may prevent further emergency harm and under which nonhuman restraint may continue. Your clients may refuse and accept the consequences of being the party that chose debt enforcement over trapped workers and wounded reef repair on public record."

Counsel said, "That is coercive."

Tavo laughed once, ugly and exhausted. "Now they found coercion."

Mira opened her eyes and looked at the silent Orison. "Term eight: no one calls this treaty. Not yet. A treaty requires parties who have not been forced into emergency by injury. This is a restraint bargain under harm. It must become something better later or be dissolved."

Keeps-Water-Calm's body color changed.

Not bright. Not approval. A narrow line of blue moved along their side, the first clear color since withdrawal.

Sera saw it. "Mira."

"I see."

The bloom outside the platform paused.

That was the only word for it. The movement toward the reef wound did not reverse, but it stopped accelerating. Blue sheets held corridor seals instead of abandoning them. Red-gold boundary forms near the guide cradle loosened from attack posture into bracing posture. One filament moved away from a worker shelter door it had been closing and allowed three trapped workers to push it open from the other side.

Anika's feed filled with shouting.

"Trapped team moving!" she said. "Pylon six team is moving!"

Brenn said, "Medical to pylon six route."

"Bloom is opening route," Tavo said. "Slowly. Nobody rush it."

Mira kept speaking because stopping now felt like making the bargain into magic. It was not magic. It was obligation offered where obligation had been refused.

"In return for restraint," she said, "humans offer labor, data, jurisdictional exposure, and claim surrender. We cannot offer trust. We can offer systems that make betrayal harder and repair easier."

Nadi Or hummed. "Better."

"Not enough."

"No."

"But better."

"Yes."

Drex opened the local system cache.

He did it on public feed, with Lena the systems tech, Tavo, Anika, Brenn, and Nadi Or all mirrored as witnesses. File trees unfolded: board escalation packets, investor escrow terms, executive risk communications, worker debt dashboards, asset-separation routines, classification acquisition files, old survey conversions, terms flagged in workforce models, insurance clawback triggers, private counsel notes mislabeled as operational safety. The platform had been a machine for making harm legible only upward. Now the data poured sideways.

Calyx counsel screamed privilege until Brenn muted them again.

Lena said, "Public escrow accepting."

Nadi Or said, "Bell Without Metal mirrors."

Brenn said, "Court seal attached."

Tavo said, "Worker copy confirmed."

Anika said, "Make three more."

"Already making five," Lena said.

For the first time since the storm, Keeps-Water-Calm moved toward the translator membrane.

Mira stopped breathing.

The Orison touched the dark membrane, but did not turn it on. Instead they pressed a pattern into it that the device did not convert into words. The membrane, unable to translate, recorded pressure. Sera routed the raw pattern to Patient Error's witness beads. Nadi Or received it and made a sound Mira had never heard from them: grief shaped around recognition.

"Not language," Nadi Or said.

"What is it?" Brenn asked.

"Boundary condition."

"Can you render it?"

"No."

Mira said, "Should you?"

Nadi Or was quiet for a moment. "No."

The raw pattern moved into the public record as itself.

The bloom answered.

Not everywhere. Not fully. But enough. The pylon six route opened. The trapped workers emerged one by one, wet, shaken, alive. The guide cradle bloom stayed braced instead of striking. Blue sheets remained over pressure seams. Red-gold bodies withdrew from some human doorways and thickened around the dead cutting head. The reef wound remained under guard. The deeper chamber remained closed.

Restraint, not trust.

Mira lowered her head until her forehead almost touched the deck.

Sera crouched beside her. "Do not pass out dramatically."

"Trying not to."

"Try better."

Anika's voice came through, rough. "Pylon six team out. All alive. One broken wrist, two pressure bruises, no drownings."

Tavo said, "Tool bay workers alive. Bloom casualty remains in panel. Blue sheet holding micro-cutter dead."

Drex said, "Local data dump complete. Investor countdown still frozen at escalation state."

Brenn said, "The bargain terms are entered as emergency supplemental order and restraint proposal."

"Not proposal," Nadi Or said.

Brenn paused.

"What then?"

"Offered obligation."

Brenn wrote it down. "Offered obligation."

Keeps-Water-Calm withdrew their filament from the dark translator membrane. Their body sagged back into the sling. The attendants closed around them, but the narrow blue line remained.

Mira looked through the pressure window at Patient Error, at the launch sleeve, at the water that had carried warning, wound, data, and now something too fragile to call peace.

"We still have to dissolve the claim," she said.

Brenn's answer came without hesitation. "Yes."

"And make the injunction final."

"As final as emergency law can be."

"And repair what can be repaired."

"Yes."

Outside, beneath the reef of glass, the storm moved away in diminishing rings.

It left the wound behind.

It also left a corridor, narrow and conditional, through which work might continue without pretending the work had made anyone clean.

Chapter 49: Pelagos Reef Injunction

Brenn wrote the injunction with seawater dripping from the ceiling onto the court recorder.

No one had designed Breakwater Court for rain.

The chamber had been built inside a pressure dome because human law liked ceilings, straight benches, and a visible place where authority could sit. Pelagos had answered by sweating through the seams. Warm brine condensed on the inner ribs, gathered in trembling beads, and fell in irregular taps across the evidence tables. Every surface smelled of salt, hot insulation, and exhausted bodies. The floor trembled each time the platform's damaged ballast corrected itself against the after-storm current.

Sera had placed a catch pan under the recorder. It filled faster than anyone liked.

"If the official order drowns before the reef does, I am entering that as commentary," she said.

Brenn did not look up. "Do not."

"Then move the recorder."

"The recorder is fixed to the certified evidence mount."

"The certified evidence mount is under a leak."

"I am aware."

Mira, sitting on a medical crate because chairs had become aspirational, said, "Put the catch pan on the evidence mount and the recorder on the pan."

Brenn finally looked up.

Sera said, "That was obvious ten minutes ago."

"I was preserving procedure," Brenn said.

Nadi Or, whose boots were still crusted with pale reef silt from the lower cradle, leaned over the recorder mount and shifted the pan with one hand. "Procedure is preserved better when it remains dry."

Brenn watched the pan settle. "Noted."

"Enter it," Sera said.

Mira closed her eyes. "Please do not make the first Pelagos reef order begin with cookware."

"Too late," Tavo said from the witness rail.

He should have been in medical. Everyone in the chamber knew it, including him. His left sleeve was cut away and the burn dressing under it had been sealed with emergency mesh. His hair still held dried salt. Two of his crew stood behind him because he had refused a chair until Anika hissed at him in a language that sounded like tool inventory and he sat on the rail edge. Drex security no longer stood between Tavo and anyone. Drex security no longer controlled doors. That had happened quietly after the rig collapse safeguards failed: workers who had saved sections of the platform while investor routines tried to preserve assets had stopped accepting orders from people with cleaner gloves.

Olan Drex sat at the respondent table with a medic on one side and a legal recorder on the other.

He looked older than he had before the storm. Not redeemed. Not broken. Worn down to the shape of what he had chosen. His company insignia remained on his collar because no one had stripped it off and because he had not removed it. Mira hated how much that mattered. Shame, if it came, would not arrive because someone confiscated a patch.

Keeps-Water-Calm lay in a shallow brine sling beyond the human rail, their body dim but not dark. Orison attendants surrounded them in a half-circle of wet blue and pale green. Their translator membrane hung beside the sling, repaired with temporary film, still unreliable after the bloom surge. Behind them, through the pressure window, D-17's reef body shone under disturbed water. The wound lobe pulsed with slow repair chemistry. Not healed. Not dying at this moment. Still there.

That was the first fact the order had to respect.

Brenn had written three openings and deleted all of them.

The first had begun, The court recognizes an emergency ecological harm. Mira had objected before he reached the second line.

"Ecological harm to what?"

"To the reef," Brenn had said.

"Then you have not recognized the subject. You have recognized damage to scenery."

He had deleted it.

The second opening had begun, The court recognizes the Orison claim that D-17 constitutes a living memory entity. Keeps-Water-Calm had activated the translator for one sentence only:

"Claim is what humans do before taking."

Brenn had deleted that too.

The third had begun, The court recognizes standing for D-17 as represented by Orison parties. Nadi Or had made a sound through her teeth.

"Representation may be necessary for court handling," she said. "Do not make the reef smaller than its speakers."

Now Brenn stared at a blank header while everyone tried not to watch him fail.

Mira understood the cruelty of the task. The law he carried had categories with strong walls: person, property, habitat, archive, evidence, claimant, resource, hazardous site. D-17 had moved through all of them and fit none without losing something essential. Brenn had spent most of the crisis proving he could not admit what had no drawer. Now he had to build a drawer while the thing itself bled beyond the window.

"Start with what the court can no longer deny," Mira said.

Drex laughed once, quietly.

Everyone looked at him.

He lifted his unburned hand. "Not contempt. Recognition."

Tavo's eyes narrowed. "Careful."

Drex looked at his crew chief, and something in his face tried to become familiar. Tavo did not let it.

Brenn placed his fingers on the recorder field.

"Emergency Order, Pelagos Local Compact Mineral Session, Claim Site D-17," he dictated. "The court finds, based on witnessed sensory exchange, acoustic mapping, biological response, historical chart evidence, worker testimony, Orison testimony, and post-injury behavioral continuity, that the structure designated D-17 cannot be treated as dormant mineral architecture for purposes of extraction law."

Sera whispered, "Good start."

Brenn continued.

"The court further finds that D-17 maintains living continuity relationships through reef organisms, chemical gradients, acoustic pore systems, symbiotic carriers, and Orison memory practices. Those relationships include recoverable historical commitments between human and Orison parties, ongoing lineage maintenance, and responsive injury behavior."

Keeps-Water-Calm's body brightened by a degree Mira felt in her chest before she saw it.

Brenn paused, then said the sentence everyone had been waiting for and fearing.

"D-17 is granted provisional standing as a living memory ecology."

No one spoke.

The dome creaked.

Somewhere below them a pump coughed, caught, and resumed.

Mira felt the words enter the chamber and fail to fix anything. The reef remained wounded. Workers remained bound to contracts that had not dissolved because one sentence learned humility. Drex remained dangerous in the law even if his hands had lost the levers. Keeps-Water-Calm remained nearly spent in the sling.

Still, the sentence did something.

It made the next lie harder.

Drex's recorder flashed for objection. His legal aide, a thin woman named Pell who had not slept since the storm, leaned close to him and whispered. Drex listened. For a moment Mira thought he might refuse. Then he nodded.

Pell stood. "Respondent Drex Maritime Extraction objects to the category living memory ecology as undefined under Compact mineral statute."

"Objection preserved," Brenn said.

"Respondent further objects that provisional standing cannot nullify existing claim rights without compensation determination."

"Preserved."

"Respondent further objects to reliance on sensory exchange as evidence where human witnesses were under stress, injury, and Orison biochemical influence."

Sera made a sound that had legal implications if translated.

Brenn said, "Preserved."

Mira leaned forward. "The court should also preserve that human witnesses were under investor pressure, worker-debt coercion, platform collapse routines, and incomplete statutory categories."

Brenn entered it without argument. "So preserved."

Pell looked briefly miserable. She sat.

Drex did not look at Mira. He looked through the pressure window at the reef. His eyes did not soften. That would have been too easy. But they stayed there.

Brenn continued.

"All extraction activity under Drex Maritime Claim D-17 is suspended. This includes cutting, boring, sample removal, chemical flushing, autonomous claim-preservation routines, debt-triggered salvage actions, remote investor commands, and any platform procedure whose material effect is removal or injury of D-17 tissue, lattice, carrier stream, memory pore, or associated symbiont."

Tavo said, "Good."

Brenn looked at him.

"Sorry," Tavo said. "Witness noise."

"Witness noise preserved in transcript as nonbinding approval."

Sera covered her mouth.

Brenn kept going, perhaps because momentum had become the only way through.

"Drex Maritime shall surrender operational control of all active tools within the D-17 hazard boundary to an emergency repair and preservation board composed of one Compact adjudication officer, two worker safety representatives, two Orison-appointed repair witnesses, one treaty-science advisor, one independent acoustic cartographer, and one debt-transition administrator."

Tavo's head came up. "Debt-transition?"

"Yes," Brenn said.

Drex's face shifted. "You cannot administer my payroll."

"I am not administering payroll. I am suspending the use of worker debt status as operational leverage within a court-protected hazard site."

Pell stood again. "Objection. That exceeds mineral jurisdiction."

"Preserved," Brenn said. "Also noted: respondent's own filings brought worker debt triggers into evidence as basis for emergency extraction necessity. The court will not consider worker survival only when it supports extraction."

Oret would have loved that, Mira thought, though Oret was on another moon and in another book of trouble entirely. She did not know why that name surfaced. Perhaps because Kestrel's public records had traveled far enough that everyone working a crisis had begun borrowing courage from strangers.

Tavo looked at Drex. "My crew gets what?"

Brenn read from notes he had drafted with Riss-like precision though Riss was not here. "Immediate freeze on penalty acceleration for workers assigned to D-17 operations. Medical treatment costs moved to emergency claim-harm ledger. Hazard-pay claims preserved. No worker may be required to participate in reef repair as condition of debt relief, but those who volunteer may be paid from preserved equipment value pending final allocation."

Anika whispered behind Tavo, "Say that again."

Brenn did.

This time the workers heard it as something other than words.

Ren, whose burned glove had been cut off and bagged as evidence, started crying silently. Jae looked at the floor as if it had insulted him by becoming less likely to swallow him. Lio said a word Mira did not know and Tavo did not correct.

Drex watched them.

Mira wanted him to look away. She wanted him to look until he understood. Wanting both made her tired.

Keeps-Water-Calm's translator membrane fluttered.

"Human obligation to human bound ones," it rendered, voice thin. "Not payment to reef."

Brenn turned toward the sling. "Correct. Worker protection is not compensation for reef harm. It prevents further coerced harm."

The Orison attendants shifted color. Nadi Or closed her eyes, listening to pressure Mira could not hear.

"Acceptable as separate water," Keeps-Water-Calm said.

Brenn entered that phrase in the explanatory notes, then underlined separate water because he had begun to learn when Orison metaphor carried legal teeth.

The order's next section took longer.

Repair.

Not restoration. Keeps-Water-Calm had refused the word. Shell-That-Returns had sent a chemical pattern through the repair channel that Nadi translated as: unbreaking is a human fantasy and sometimes a human excuse.

So Brenn dictated:

"D-17 repair operations shall prioritize survival of existing living continuity relationships, stabilization of the fractured lobe, protection of carrier streams, and prevention of further human-caused harm. No scan, sample, memory capture, acoustic imprint, or sensory exchange taken during repair may be used to renew extraction claim, create substitute archive, or argue that data preservation compensates for tissue destruction."

Mira felt the sentence strike Drex harder than the suspension.

His whole claim, at its most civilized, had always rested on substitution. Scan before cutting. Preserve record before removal. Pay later. Repair by accounting. That logic had not vanished because Brenn disliked it. It had to be named until it could not pass itself off as care.

Drex said, "You are making knowledge useless."

Mira answered before Brenn could. "No. We are making it insufficient."

Drex finally looked at her.

There he was again: the man who could explain a ledger until hunger sounded like weather, who had shown her worker dorms and dared her to call survival simple, who had chosen the cut after he knew the reef was more than stone. Not a monster. Worse. A person with reasons who had treated reasons as absolution.

"If scans cannot preserve value," he said, "then every damaged heritage claim becomes infinite liability."

Keeps-Water-Calm's body flushed a cold white.

Mira said, "You are still asking how much erasure costs."

"Because courts require amounts."

Brenn said, "This court is determining injunction, not final damages."

Drex's laugh had no humor. "Then the court is determining ruin without calling it ruin."

Tavo stood too quickly and winced. "You used our ruin as a timer."

Drex turned toward him. "I tried to keep people employed."

"You tried to keep the claim alive and hoped we would fit inside it."

"Without the claim, the company fails."

"Then say that. Stop calling it us."

The chamber went still.

Brenn did not interrupt.

Tavo's voice roughened. "I was loyal to you longer than I should have been because you knew our names. That worked on me. I need that in the record too. Knowing names is not the same as protecting people."

Brenn entered it.

Drex looked as if something had struck him in the sternum and not killed him.

Pell whispered, "Olan."

He sat back.

The order continued.

Evidence preservation. Courier transmission. Orison review. Human appeal. Worker transition. Investor command quarantine. Autonomous routine disablement. Public notices in all platform languages, including the blunt worker dialect Tavo insisted was not a dialect and then used to translate half the terms better than the court feed.

Sera helped build the sensory evidence annex. She moved more slowly than usual, stopping when the imprint headaches tightened behind her eyes. Mira tried to send her to medical. Sera refused with the calm of someone who had discovered that being harmed did not remove the right to describe the harm.

"Repeatability clause," Sera said.

Brenn rubbed his face. "Again?"

"Yes. If future courts use sensory exchange, they need limits. You cannot make people taste a reef every time a company wants proof."

Keeps-Water-Calm's translator flickered. "Correct. Human receiving is not mining."

Sera pointed at the membrane. "That."

Mira nodded. "Sensory evidence must be voluntary, medically screened, limited, and nonexclusive. The reef's standing cannot depend on humans enduring harm every time."

Nadi Or added, "Nor on Orison making themselves edible to law."

That stopped the room for a while.

Brenn wrote:

"Sensory exchange evidence admitted in this emergency shall not establish a requirement that Orison memory ecologies become directly experienceable by human witnesses to hold standing. Future proceedings must accept acoustic, chemical, behavioral, historical, and Orison-contextual evidence without demanding harmful translation where sufficient noninvasive proof exists."

Mira looked at him.

Brenn said, "Is that adequate?"

"It is a beginning."

"I am learning to dislike that answer."

"Good."

The injunction took six hours to complete.

During the fifth hour, the court recorder stopped because the catch pan overflowed and shorted the secondary mount. Sera had warned them. Brenn closed his eyes, then reopened the order from the mirrored feed while Nadi Or laughed so quietly it sounded like bubbles under ice.

During the sixth hour, Keeps-Water-Calm lost translator coherence entirely. The membrane produced fragments:

Not owned.

Still wounded.

Do not store us as apology.

Water remembers pressure.
