The brine channel answered in small disturbed pulses.

Cho's delayed voice came thinner. "Field Director Drex, why was this memo not included in your initial claim filing?"

"Because it was finance-side operational advice, not mineral evidence."

"That is the formal answer. Provide the useful one."

Drex almost smiled. Compact delegates sometimes remembered why law mattered.

"Because if the memo sat beside the claim, everyone would see the categories were being used as a weapon."

"By you?"

"Through me."

Pell said, "My client is not qualified to assign legal culpability to third parties."

"No," Drex said. "But I am qualified to describe the lever I pulled."

Tavo spoke from medical for the first time. "And the lever pulled us."

Vennel looked at the panel. "Crew Chief Rusk, you are not called."

"Then call me."

Qadir glanced through documents. "Crew Chief Rusk may give limited statement regarding operational pressure."

Tavo shifted, winced, ignored it. "The crew knew the money was bad. We did not know the debt had been written to make review look like theft from our own families. Housing remittance, medical coverage, hazard rotation penalties. Every hour of waiting turned into a thing a worker could blame on the reef, on Cael, on Orison, on anyone except the contract. That matters. Not because it excuses the cut. Because it tells you how to build the next disaster if you leave the debt alone."

No one spoke over him.

Anika's voice came from somewhere offscreen. "Tell them about contamination."

Tavo sighed. "My medic says tell you about contamination."

Vennel said, "Proceed."

"After blue contact on Pylon Two, we used the word contamination. I stopped it once. Not always. The platform's maintenance language treats foreign living matter on equipment as contamination by default. Hale's memo says contamination event may justify emergency clearing if it threatens claim integrity. Not worker life. Claim integrity. Those words were waiting for us before the Orison touched the pylon."

Sera found the clause. Put it up.

Mira did not look at Drex. She looked at the sentence.

If nonhuman biological contact compromises equipment surfaces essential to claim preservation, field direction may initiate emergency clearing protocols to preserve recoverable asset integrity.

Keeps-Water-Calm's translator spoke low.

"Skin becomes dirt when the tool wants innocence."

Cho said, "This hearing enters provisional finding that the finance instrument, advisory memo, and operational language created foreseeable pressure toward unauthorized acceleration and evidentiary narrowing. Field Director Drex's admissions preserve rather than resolve questions of personal culpability."

Pell murmured, "That means they still get to punish you."

Drex said, "I know."

Qadir said, "Immediate sanctions proposed: continued full operational hold on extraction at D-17; creditor intervention rights suspended pending Compact review; all DME sensor, finance, maintenance, and communications records preserved; worker remittance and medical coverage placed under emergency labor protection if Compact labor confirms authority; Field Director Drex restricted from unilateral operational restart; Sivek Hale Consulting and named creditor committee notified as interested parties subject to evidence demand."

Pell stood again. "DME cannot accept unilateral suspension of creditor rights without guaranteed labor protection."

"DME is not being asked to accept," Qadir said.

"Then your order must also prevent the creditors from punishing workers for compliance."

The room noticed her then, not as shield for Drex, but as someone who had finally chosen a target beyond the chair.

Cho nodded after the delay. "Add provisional labor shield. Station Director Vennel, can Cael Station administer escrowed emergency support for seventy-two hours?"

Vennel made the face of a man being handed a burning machine part and asked whether he had gloves.

"Administer, yes. Fund, no."

Mira said, "Use the claim bond."

Pell looked at her. "That bond secures environmental damage."

"This is environmental damage."

"Worker housing is not reef tissue."

Keeps-Water-Calm's translator answered before Mira could.

"The hand that must feed children cuts faster. Feed the hand if you want the knife still."

Pell stared at the brine channel.

Then she said, "I withdraw the objection."

Drex looked at her.

She did not look back.

Cho entered the order.

The public board updated line by line. Operational hold. Evidence preservation. Labor shield. Claim bond emergency use. Creditor rights suspended. Advisory network evidence demand. Drex restricted from unilateral restart.

It should have felt like defeat.

Instead it felt like one small piece of the room becoming visible.

Qadir said, "Field Director Drex, do you accept the restrictions?"

Pell began, "For the record, my client-"

"Yes," Drex said.

Pell sat down slowly.

Qadir looked almost annoyed at the lack of ceremony. "Do you have any final statement before provisional order closes?"

Drex had thought about apology. He had even written one. It had contained all the correct nouns: harm, accountability, listening, regret, repair. Reading it now would be another kind of extraction. Taking feeling from the room to polish his own shape.

He looked at Keeps-Water-Calm.

"No final statement."

The Orison sling brightened.

Cho said, "Hearing closed pending full review."

The magnetic click released the chair.

No one moved quickly.

That was becoming a local habit.

Then the room broke into tasks. Pell demanded copies of the exact order language. Sera and Nadi began tagging the Hale memo for translation hazards. Vennel went to find money that had become morally available faster than it had become administratively real. Keeps-Water-Calm's attendants adjusted flow through the sling. Low-Current-Under-Stone pulsed toward the evidence board where the creditor names remained displayed.

Drex stood.

His legs felt less reliable than he preferred.

Mira approached him, not close enough for comfort.

"You understand that naming Hale does not clean your part," she said.

"Yes."

"Good."

"Did you know he was connected?"

"No."

"Does it change the review?"

"It widens it."

He nodded. "That is worse."

"Usually."

Tavo's remote panel was still active. "Olan."

Drex turned.

The crew chief's face had settled into something more tired than anger.

"Workers are asking whether the labor shield is real."

"Tell them I do not know yet."

Tavo's mouth twitched. "Bad answer."

"Tell them it is ordered and not funded, which means it is half real and needs witnesses."

"Better."

"Tell them the claim bond may cover seventy-two hours if Vennel can make the money move before the creditors teach it to freeze."

"That is too many words."

"Then tell them the room is fighting for it."

Tavo held his gaze through the screen. "Are you?"

Drex felt the old director reflex reach for certainty.

He put it down.

"I am in the room," he said. "I am not leading it."

Tavo considered that.

"Might be your first useful order."

The panel went dark.

Drex looked back at the evidence board. Sivek Hale's memo remained open under the creditor list. Aster Vale. Harrow Meridian. Leontes. Pelagic Infrastructure. Names that would claim distance, process, fiduciary obligation, no direct order. Names that had made a room where cutting felt like responsibility.

Keeps-Water-Calm's translator clicked beside him.

"The chair releases the body," the Orison said. "Not the pattern."

Drex did not know whether that was accusation, mercy, or an instruction.

For once, he did not ask for a cleaner category.

Chapter 54: The Reef Refuses a Monument

The first proposal for a memorial arrived before the repair medium held for a full minute.

It came from Compact Public Continuity, which meant it came from people far enough away to believe grief could be scheduled after liability and before policy reform. The packet entered Cael Station's queue under a tasteful seal, blue-gray, no corporate mark, with language that had clearly survived several committees and lost every living edge in the process.

Vennel read the opening line aloud in the operations galley because he had made the mistake of opening the packet while holding coffee and now required witnesses.

"In recognition of the regrettable D-17 incident and in commitment to interspecies healing, the Compact proposes a joint commemorative marker at Cael Station, incorporating human, Orison, and Meridian civic forms-"

Sera put both hands over her face.

Anika said, "No."

Lio, who had been asleep with his forehead on a ration warmer, woke enough to say, "No what?"

"All of it," Anika said.

Mira took the packet from Vennel before he could read more damage into the room.

The proposal was worse in detail. A transparent plaque in the treaty corridor. A low acoustic tone derived from the first recorded reef harmonic. A public apology text to be drafted by Compact cultural staff with Orison consultation. A small living-water feature, if Orison representatives approved, symbolizing resilience. Optional inclusion of worker names under a heading called Shared Risk.

At the bottom, under Implementation Considerations, someone had written:

Memorial may assist public transition from emergency posture to long-term reconciliation framework.

"They want a period," Sera said.

Mira looked at her.

"At the end of the sentence," Sera said. "They want punctuation they can put under glass."

Ren, still wrapped in the same gray blanket she had carried since the archive audit, took the packet and read with the expression of someone checking a wound for infection.

"They are not only wrong," she said. "They are early."

Vennel rubbed his eyes. "Public Continuity always arrives early. It is how they avoid being useful."

The galley had become the station's unofficial grief room because it contained heat, bad coffee, and no formal recorders unless Sera brought one by accident. Workers came through in ones and threes between repair shifts. Scientists slept there by mistake. Orison attendants did not enter the dry galley, but the brine channel in the adjacent corridor carried enough chemical exchange that no conversation felt entirely human.

Tavo appeared on the wall panel, released from medical confinement into remote irritation.

"What did they propose?" he asked.

Vennel said, "A marker."

"For what?"

No one answered quickly.

That was the problem.

For the fracture? For the unauthorized cut? For the Orison repair body startled and not torn? For the workers who had nearly become instruments of a contract? For the human habit of discovering harm only when it became visible? For D-17, which was not dead and therefore could not be mourned cleanly?

Mira said, "They think a marker can help the public move from emergency to reconciliation."

Tavo stared through the panel.

"The public can come move medium through a wound for fifteen seconds and then talk to me about transition."

Anika pointed at the panel. "That one goes on the wall."

"Do not put me on more walls."

Mira folded the packet closed. "Keeps-Water-Calm has to see it."

Sera groaned. "Do they?"

"Yes."

"Can we place bets on the first translated insult?"

Vennel said, "No betting on diplomatic harm in my galley."

Lio raised his head. "Can we place nonmonetary predictions?"

"Also no."

Mira carried the proposal to the treaty room with the uneasy feeling of a person delivering a beautifully wrapped object that might be either useless or flammable.

Keeps-Water-Calm received it in the shallow sling, attended by Low-Current-Under-Stone and three smaller Orison witnesses who had arrived after the repair cycles began. They had no translated names yet. Nadi Or called them, temporarily and with their permission, Witness-At-Edge, Witness-With-Old-Salt, and Witness-Who-Waits-Aside. Brenn objected to provisional descriptive names in official logs until Nadi asked whether he preferred "unidentified alien organism one." He had withdrawn the objection and looked damaged by his own categories.

Brenn was present now, as was Pell, Vennel, Sera, and a half-dozen remote Compact observers. Drex was not. He had asked whether his presence would help and accepted the answer when Mira said no.

Mira sent the memorial proposal through the shared display.

Keeps-Water-Calm did not respond to the first paragraph.

Low-Current-Under-Stone did not respond to the second.

Witness-At-Edge drifted closer to the membrane during the section on acoustic tone.

The translator clicked, failed, clicked again, and rendered:

"They have taken the wound's voice and made it a door chime."

Sera whispered, "There it is."

Brenn gave her a warning look. It had little force. He looked like a man who had spent the last six hours discovering that every framework he brought to Pelagos had teeth marks in it.

Keeps-Water-Calm pulsed once, sharply. The room quieted.

"Continue," they said.

Mira did, though she hated every line more when it had to pass through Orison attention. Human apology text. Joint healing language. Living-water feature. Public transition. Reconciliation framework.

When the proposal finished, the brine channel remained still.

Too still.

Mira had learned that Orison anger was rarely loud. Loudness was what humans added when they needed emotion to cross air. In water, anger could be a reduction. A withdrawal of motion. A refusal to feed the translator with anything smooth enough to become a sentence.

Finally Witness-With-Old-Salt moved.

Their body was broader than Keeps-Water-Calm's, dark at the edges, with pale rings that seemed less like markings than records of old pressure. They touched the membrane once.

The translator gave a voice after a long search.

"When human bone breaks, do you build a small bone beside it and call the walking complete?"

Brenn leaned forward despite himself.

Mira answered because no one else did. "No."

"When a child is hungry, do you engrave bread?"

"No."

"When a promise is cut, why do humans raise a sign and ask the cut place to become memory instead of demand?"

The remote observers began adding notes. Mira wanted to shut them all off.

Pell spoke carefully. "Some human memorials preserve obligation. They can hold public attention."

Witness-Who-Waits-Aside shifted near the back of the sling. The translator took their chemical pulse through Keeps-Water-Calm's contact.

"Attention is not the same as being fed."

Pell nodded once. "No."

Brenn said, "The proposal is not intended to replace repair or legal review."

Keeps-Water-Calm's color darkened.

"Intention is the song humans sing when consequences enter late."

Brenn absorbed that as if it had weight.

"Then help me understand the consequence," he said.

That was new. Mira looked at him.

He did not look back. His attention was on the wet side of the room, not the recorders, not the observers, not the public shape of his own humility. Perhaps exhaustion had finally done what argument could not. Perhaps he had simply run out of ways to be correct.

Keeps-Water-Calm moved closer to the membrane.

"A monument teaches distance," they said. "It says: harm has a place. Stand here. Feel here. Leave changed if you can. Then go where your hands are ordinary again."

Brenn said, "And you object to distance."

"We object to false distance. D-17 is not past. The cut is not still. The repair that failed for fifteen seconds is not over. The workers who were made sharp by debt are not unmade. The adviser whose words pressed through another human's hand is not water gone cold. If you build a marker now, human attention will gather there and call itself care. It will leave the wound quieter."

Sera said, "That is exactly what the implementation note says."

She threw the line onto the display:

Memorial may assist public transition from emergency posture to long-term reconciliation framework.

Low-Current-Under-Stone pulsed.

"Transition means the hand wants to stop shaking before the wound stops needing it."

Vennel sat back hard enough that his chair complained.

The Compact observers remained silent.

Mira thought of Kestrel's names on the vane, the lists of missing and dead and injured, the public charter that had tried, imperfectly, to make memory administrative instead of ornamental. Even there, the danger had been present. Names could become a substitute for schedule. A wall could let people visit duty instead of doing it.

"What would you accept?" Brenn asked.

Keeps-Water-Calm's attendants moved around them in a slow tightening pattern.

"No marker."

The answer was immediate.

Brenn waited. "No public acknowledgment?"

"Acknowledgment must remain attached to work."

"Define work."

Nadi Or laughed once under her breath and then looked horrified at herself.

Keeps-Water-Calm did not seem offended. "Good. Define it."

Sera opened a blank board.

Mira said, "Repair cycles."

Anika's voice came from the worker channel, where someone had clearly patched her in without asking. "Tool redesign."

Tavo added from another unauthorized patch, "Labor shield."

Pell said, "Creditor evidence demands."

Ren, from the archive station, said, "Public access to the debt clauses and advisory memo once worker privacy is redacted."

Brenn said, "A standing review category."

Everyone looked at him again.

He cleared his throat. "Provisional. I said provisional."

Sera typed anyway.

Keeps-Water-Calm watched the list assemble.

"This is not a monument," they said.

"No," Mira said. "It is a maintenance schedule with witnesses."

Witness-At-Edge touched the membrane. The translator rendered: "Better. Ugly. Better."

Vennel made a small sound that might have been agreement or administrative despair.

The proposal changed shape over the next hour.

It stopped being a plaque. Then it stopped being a corridor feature. Then it stopped being an event. Sera deleted the acoustic tone with visible satisfaction. Nadi replaced "healing language" with "unresolved obligations." Brenn moved the document out of Public Continuity template and into emergency order format. Pell insisted the labor shield appear before any public apology because workers read order in documents the way creditors did. Tavo objected to being included under Shared Risk and proposed Manufactured Risk, which Pell surprised everyone by supporting.

The Compact observers woke enough to object.

"Public documents require accessible terminology," one of them said.

Ren replied from archive, "Then make the truth accessible."

The observer said, "Manufactured risk implies fault before final adjudication."

Drex's voice entered the channel. He had not been invited. Nobody sounded surprised.

"Use it."

Pell closed her eyes. "Olan, you are still restricted from unilateral operational speech."

"I am not ordering. I am agreeing to be described accurately."

Brenn looked up. "Field Director Drex, you are not a party to this drafting session."

"Then remove me after I say one thing."

Keeps-Water-Calm's brine lightened by a fraction. "Let the chair's body speak if the pattern allows."

Drex took one audible breath.

"Do not put worker names on a marker unless every worker chooses it after the debt review, not before. Some of them will want their names nowhere near this. Some will want them everywhere. Neither choice should be made while the company still holds their housing. And do not call what happened shared risk. We did not share it evenly."

The channel stayed open for three seconds after he stopped.

Then Pell said, "That is one thing."

He disconnected.

Anika said, "Annoying when he is right."

Tavo said, "Frequent problem this week."

By the end of the hour, the document title had become:

CONTINUING OBLIGATION RECORD: D-17 FRACTURE, REPAIR, LABOR, AND EVIDENCE DUTIES

It contained no image. No tone. No symbolic water feature. No declaration of reconciliation. It required a public board, but the board had to display current work, not finished feeling: repair cycle status, harm indicators, labor shield funding, creditor compliance, outstanding evidence demands, Orison witness statements that were permitted to remain unresolved, and a blank line labeled:

WHAT HAS NOT YET BEEN LEARNED

Brenn objected to the blank line twice.

Keeps-Water-Calm refused both objections without words.

The blank line stayed.

Mira expected Public Continuity to hate it. She did not expect them to answer within twelve minutes.

Their reply was brief:

This format may be difficult for public engagement and lacks closure language.

Vennel read it aloud.

No one laughed.

That made it worse for Public Continuity somehow.

Sera wrote a draft response:

Correct.

Brenn said, "We need a complete sentence."

Sera added a period.

Mira took the response pane before it could become a second incident.

Keeps-Water-Calm said, "Send the small word."

"Correct?" Mira asked.

"Yes. It refuses the wrong hunger."

Brenn looked as if he might object on procedural grounds and then decided to preserve his remaining dignity.

Vennel sent:

Correct. Closure language is not appropriate while repair, labor protection, and evidence review remain active. Public engagement will attach to continuing obligations rather than symbolic completion.

Public Continuity did not respond again.

The new board went live three hours later in the same treaty corridor where the plaque would have hung.

Workers came first, because workers always found new boards before officials finished announcing them. Anika stood with Lio and Jae, reading the categories without speaking. Tavo appeared remotely, though medical had tried to limit his access to "nonagitating channels." Ren brought a stool and sat near the board with a portable archive key in her lap. Pell came to check the wording around creditor compliance and stayed longer than she intended. Drex did not come. Mira noticed and did not decide whether that was restraint or shame.

Orison witnesses arrived through the adjacent brine channel.

Not many. Enough.

Witness-With-Old-Salt touched the membrane beside the line WHAT HAS NOT YET BEEN LEARNED.

The board registered contact but did not translate.

That had been one of Sera's design choices. Not every touch should be forced into human text.

A worker Mira did not know, young, orange jacket still stained from platform grease, stood in front of the board for a long time. Finally he said, not to anyone in particular, "So this is not the memorial?"

Anika answered. "No."

"What is it?"

Tavo's remote panel flickered. He must have heard.

"A work order that stares back," he said.

The worker considered that.

"Good," he said.

He touched the labor shield line, saw the funding status still marked partial, and swore with quiet focus.

Then he went to find someone who could tell him why.

Mira watched him leave and felt the shape of the thing settle. Not comfort. Comfort would have been premature. But direction.

Keeps-Water-Calm hovered in the brine channel beside her.

"The reef refuses a monument," the translator said.

Mira looked through the corridor glass toward the darker water beyond the station, where D-17 lay injured and continuing.

"Does the reef know we refused it?"

"Bad question," Keeps-Water-Calm said.

Mira almost smiled.

"Incomplete?" she asked.

"Better."

The Orison's colors softened, not into approval, but into something that did not require immediate correction.

"The relation changes when the hand remains," they said.

Behind them, the board updated:

REPAIR CYCLE THREE: THIRTY-EIGHT-SECOND MEDIUM HOLD. NO NEW HARM FLAG. TOOL REDESIGN STILL INCOMPLETE.

The corridor did not cheer.

No one wanted to make a ceremony out of thirty-eight seconds.

But Anika put one hand against the wall below the board, palm flat, and held it there until the next update arrived.

Mira did not know whether the gesture meant apology, promise, exhaustion, or simply balance.

For once, the difference did not need to be resolved before the work continued.

Chapter 55: Treaty Science

Mira wrote the first draft of the protocol in a room designed for evacuation drills.

No one had chosen it for symbolism. Cael Station had run out of rooms that were not wet, occupied, recorded, legally contested, medically reserved, or being used by exhausted workers who had discovered that sleeping under a public obligation board made them feel less alone. The evacuation room sat behind the eastern pressure baffles and contained benches, emergency masks, two lockers of expired thermal foil, and a wall map of station exits that had been amended so many times the safest route looked like a legal argument.

It was perfect for treaty science, Mira decided. Uncomfortable, temporary, full of paths no one trusted completely.

Sera sat on the floor with three tablets and a ration bulb she kept forgetting to drink from. Brenn occupied a bench as if posture could preserve jurisdiction. Nadi Or stood near the door, translating nothing and watching everything. Ren had claimed the evacuation map because it was the only vertical surface large enough for her archive links. Pell came and went, carrying order language, creditor objections, worker shield clauses, and the expression of a person who had become necessary to several people she did not like.

Keeps-Water-Calm did not attend.

That absence shaped the room more than any presence.

The protocol title had already changed six times.

Mira's first version had been:

NONEXTRACTIVE EVIDENCE PROTOCOL FOR ORISON LIVING MEMORY ECOLOGIES

Brenn objected to living memory before the evidence standard established it.

Sera objected to established because the standard being drafted was the thing that might establish it.

Ren objected to Orison because D-17 had just spent a week teaching them that human naming of whose relation counted was unstable.

Nadi objected to ecology because Orison witnesses used a term that meant something closer to "continuity that metabolizes relation," which everyone agreed was too long for a document title and too important to ignore.

Pell objected to nothing at first, which meant she was about to object to everything later.

The current title read:

PROVISIONAL NONEXTRACTIVE EVIDENCE PROTOCOL FOR CONTINUITY RELATIONS IN DISPUTED BIOGENIC STRUCTURES

Sera hated it.

"It sounds like the document was raised in captivity," she said.

Brenn did not look up from his tablet. "It sounds admissible."

"Those are related problems."

Mira wrote the first principle:

1. Evidence gathering must not require the relation under examination to perform harm-legible activity in order to receive protection.

The room went quiet enough that the ventilation became political.

Brenn read the line twice. "Harm-legible is not a standard term."

"It is now a necessary one," Mira said.

"Define it."

She did.

Harm-legible: Capable of being recognized as harm by the reviewing institution without requiring the affected relation, body, community, or ecology to escalate distress into forms the institution already understands.

Pell, who had just entered with a stack of creditor response panes, stopped in the doorway.

"That will make every insurer in the Reach reach for a weapon."

"Good," Sera said.

"Not good. Predictable."

Brenn adjusted the line. "Replace weapon with objection in your private satisfaction."

"I did not say it for the record," Pell said.

Nadi said, "You entered a room containing three recorders."

Pell closed her eyes. "Of course I did."

Mira kept writing.

2. Absence of active habitation, centralized speech, human-analog cognition, or high metabolic exchange must not be treated as evidence of absence where adaptive relation, reciprocal response, or continuity markers are observed.

Brenn inhaled.

Sera pointed at him without looking up. "If you say too broad, I am putting your category sheet in the airlock."

"Too broad," Brenn said.

"There it is."

"A protocol that captures too much becomes unusable."

"A protocol that captures only what looks like us is worse."

"Both statements can be true."

That stopped Sera for half a beat.

Mira leaned back. Her shoulders had tightened sometime during the second clause, the old field posture of trying to write law without giving science a limp. "He is right."

Sera turned on her. "I hate when you do that."

"So do I."

Brenn looked briefly pleased, which was unwise.

Mira added a limiting clause:

Where at least two independent nonextractive observations indicate persistent adaptive relation across environmental events.

Ren said, "Define independent."

Mira wrote:

Independent observations may include passive acoustic persistence, chemical continuity markers, non-damaging symbiont response, tool-contact response, current-mediated pattern recurrence, and witness testimony from affected nonhuman relations, provided no observation is required to force invasive response.

Pell said, "You cannot call Orison testimony an observation."

Nadi's head turned.

Pell lifted one hand. "Legally. I am not saying it is less than observation. I am saying observation has a technical chain."

Ren said, "Testimony has a chain too."

"Different chain."

"Then write both chains."

Pell looked at Mira. "You are all making a document that will be impossible to implement quickly."

Mira said, "The quick document almost let a reef get cut apart."

"No. The quick finance instrument did. The document failed to stop it."

That distinction mattered.

Mira wrote it down in the margin:

Evidence protocol must identify when non-evidentiary systems are training action faster than review can operate.

Brenn said, "That is not evidence science."

Pell said, "It is if the non-evidentiary system affects what evidence is produced."

Everyone looked at her.

She set the creditor panes on a bench harder than necessary. "I am still a lawyer."

Sera said, "We're all grieving in our own ways."

Pell ignored her, but less efficiently than usual.

The third principle took the longest:

3. Classification failure must trigger protection rather than permission where the failure arises from the reviewing framework's inability to preserve observed relation.

Brenn objected before the line was finished. "That reverses the burden of proof."

"Yes," Mira said.

"You cannot simply write yes and move on."

"I was not planning to move on."

"Mineral law requires a claim to be restricted by recognizable grounds. If every category failure becomes protection, no one can operate under uncertainty."

Mira looked at the evacuation map behind Ren. A thick red arrow pointed toward an exit that had been sealed after a pressure seam failure eight years ago. Someone had drawn a black X over it, then someone else had added a new arrow in blue, then a note reading USE ONLY IF WEST BAFFLE HOLDS. Instructions under uncertainty. Some were false safety. Some were the only way out.

"No one should operate extractively under certain kinds of uncertainty," she said.

Brenn's jaw worked. "Define certain kinds."

"Where extraction would destroy the evidence needed to resolve the uncertainty."

Sera whispered, "There."

Ren added archive links to three prior disputes where first cuts had erased contested structures before review concluded. Two involved Orison relations. One involved a posthuman fungal archive on Vale Minor that human law still called a storage substrate despite the fact that it had negotiated for its own weather.

Brenn read the links and looked older afterward.

"Temporary protective presumption," he said.

Mira nodded. "Yes."

"Not permanent recognition."

"Not yet."

"You realize this protocol cannot give the Orison what they are asking."

There it was.

The limit they had all been circling.

Sera's tablet dimmed from inactivity. Ren stopped moving links. Nadi's face shifted into the interpreter's discipline of feeling something later. Pell sat down without meaning to.

Mira looked at the draft. It was good work. Careful work. Better than what they had. It could delay cuts, force better evidence, expose debt pressure, admit witness relations, punish category failure less often with destruction. It could make the next Drex wait. It could make the next Brenn ask a better first question.

It could not make human law become Orison relation.

It could not keep translation from turning living obligation into reviewable units.

It could not be innocent.

"I know," Mira said.

The room did not punish her for the honesty. That made it harder.

Nadi spoke after a long pause. "Keeps-Water-Calm refused to attend because if they helped draft it, humans would call the shape consent."

Mira had suspected. Hearing it still hurt.

Brenn looked at the brine access indicator on the wall, though there was no Orison channel in the evacuation room. "Then are we appropriating their terms?"

"Yes," Nadi said.

Pell made a small, involuntary sound.

Nadi continued. "Also no. Also there is no clean place from which to write after the cut. The question is whether you leave the dirt visible."

Sera set her tablet down. "That sounds like something they said."

"It is something I am saying after listening to them."

"Sorry."

"Accepted."

Mira added a preamble.

This protocol is not recognition of the full nature, sovereignty, or obligations of the relation under review. It is an emergency human scientific-legal restraint designed to prevent irreversible harm while human institutions remain unable to classify the relation without reduction. Its use must not be represented as consent by affected Orison witnesses or by any nonhuman relation observed under it.

Brenn read it.

"That is unusually hostile for a preamble."

"Good," Pell said before Sera could.

Mira looked at her.

Pell shrugged. "A polite preamble will be used in a brochure."

They kept writing.

By hour three the room smelled of stale suit liners, hot circuitry, ration paste, and the metallic nervousness of people trying to build a bridge while admitting it did not reach the other side.

The protocol gained sections:

Observation Without Provocation.

Witness Terms and Refusal Rights.

Instrument Transparency and Category Failure Logs.

Financial and Operational Pressure Disclosure.

Evidence Preservation Under Protective Uncertainty.

Worker Transition and Dependency Mapping.

Prohibited Language: Contamination, Dormancy, and Hazard in Disputed Relations.

That last section triggered the most argument.

Brenn said, "You cannot prohibit ordinary technical words."

Sera said, "Watch us."

Pell said, "You can restrict their use in operational orders when the terms trigger emergency authority."

Ren added three examples from the archive showing contamination language used to justify clearing indigenous biofilms from machinery before review. One had resulted in six deaths and a treaty footnote no one taught.

Mira wrote:

Use of contamination, dormant, inert, hazardous, abandoned, or unoccupied in disputed continuity relation cases must include explicit scope, evidence basis, and prohibited inference warnings.

Sera said, "Prohibited inference warnings is a terrible phrase."

"It is a useful terrible phrase," Brenn said.

"Do not enjoy yourself."

"I am not."

"Good."

Pell drafted one:

WARNING: The term dormant here describes currently measured metabolic exchange only. It does not establish absence of relation, witness, continuity, sovereignty, or harm.

Everyone stared at it.

Pell looked defensive. "What?"

Mira said, "That is good."

"I am capable of being useful."

Sera said, "We are all making discoveries."

Pell pointed a stylus at her. "One day there will be no emergency and I will remember every sentence you have said."

"I am counting on institutional failure."

Brenn, to everyone's surprise, laughed.

It was a small sound, and it embarrassed him immediately, but it changed the room. Not enough to make the work easy. Enough to remind them that ease was not the only evidence of a room becoming less dangerous.

The first external review came from Drex Platform.

Not Olan. Tavo.

He appeared on the evacuation room wall, still half-reclined because medical had learned to compromise by making disobedience ergonomic.

"Worker dependency mapping," he said without greeting. "Define it so no one can turn it into worker blame."

Mira opened the section. "Help us."

Tavo narrowed his eyes. "You mean that?"

"Yes."

"Fine. Start with: no worker need created by employer debt, housing control, medical coverage, hazard pay, remittance schedule, transport restriction, or immigration condition may be cited as worker consent to ecological harm unless independent worker counsel confirms informed, noncoerced choice."

Pell blinked. "You have been thinking about this."

"I have been living inside it."

Ren wrote fast.

Tavo continued. "Add that transition funding has to be arranged before operational shutdown if the shutdown is based on protected uncertainty. Otherwise every worker becomes a hostage to the old job."

Brenn said, "Funding source?"

"Claim bond first. Creditor recovery pool second. Compact emergency labor third. Company assets fourth. Director dignity never."

Sera typed the last phrase.

Pell deleted it.

Sera put it in a comment.

Mira let it stay there.

Tavo read the preamble and went quiet.

"You know workers will hate that this does not promise jobs," he said.

"Yes."

"You know creditors will hate that it might."

"Yes."

"Good. It is aimed correctly."

His panel disconnected when a medic threatened something offscreen.

The second external review came from Keeps-Water-Calm.

Not in person.

Not by translation.

The evacuation room door chimed, and a station tech delivered a sealed wet-carrier tube no longer than Mira's hand. Inside it floated a membrane strip, dark blue at the edges, with chemical marks that Nadi received like a fragile argument. She did not translate immediately. She read, stepped away, read again, and then set one hand on the evacuation bench.

"They say the protocol may proceed to Orison witness review if three limits are entered without alteration."

Brenn straightened.

Mira's throat tightened. "Read them."

Nadi did.

"First: The protocol must state that refusal to answer an instrument is not evidence against the relation."

Mira wrote it.

"Second: The protocol must state that Orison witness participation may be withdrawn if human procedure treats partial translation as full understanding."

Brenn opened his mouth, then closed it.

Mira wrote it.

"Third: The protocol must state that no human document can close the obligation created by harm."

The evacuation room became very still.

Pell whispered, "That one will be difficult."

Nadi looked at her. "Yes."

Brenn said, "Difficult to enforce."

"Difficult to accept," Nadi said. "Enforcement is your smaller problem."

Mira wrote:

No finding, order, protocol, memorial, settlement, or classification produced by human institutions may be represented as closing the obligation created by harm to the relation. Closure claims are prohibited unless accepted under terms set by affected witnesses and continuing relations, and even then must be limited to the specific duty named.

Pell sat with the sentence for a long time.

"That will break settlement language."

Mira said, "Good."

Pell did not disagree.

Brenn signed the draft as provisional receiving adjudicator, which was less than adoption and more than Mira had expected that morning. Vennel signed as station emergency authority. Pell signed as respondent counsel acknowledging review but not waiving objection. Tavo signed later with a thumbprint from medical and a note that read:

Workers are not ballast.

Ren archived the draft under restricted public release and sent sanitized notice to the continuing obligation board.

Mira was the last to sign.

Her hand hovered above the tablet.

She thought of the first time she had come to Pelagos believing patient observation could teach humans how to be less dangerous. She thought of the claim file calling the south branch non-extractive adjacency. She thought of the reef answering the crack. She thought of a protocol that could protect without understanding, and the arrogance that still lived inside the desire to protect.

Science, she had once believed, was a way to approach without taking.

Now she knew better.

Approach changed the approached. Measurement made claims. Protection drew borders. Even refusal entered the record as a human-readable event if the recorder was hungry enough.

Treaty science was not clean science.

It was science that kept its dirt in the margins and taught the margins to speak.

She signed.

The continuing obligation board updated five minutes later:

PROVISIONAL NONEXTRACTIVE EVIDENCE PROTOCOL FILED FOR WITNESS REVIEW.

Under WHAT HAS NOT YET BEEN LEARNED, Ren added:

Whether a human restraint can protect a relation it cannot fully understand without mistaking restraint for recognition.

No one objected.

That was not the same as agreement.

Mira left the evacuation room after midnight and found Keeps-Water-Calm waiting in the corridor brine channel.

Their body was dim, attendants absent, translator membrane set to low power. For once, no recorders followed except the station's unavoidable life-safety logs.

"You did not attend," Mira said.

"I attended by absence."

"I know."

"No," Keeps-Water-Calm said. "You are learning."

Mira accepted the correction.

The Orison drifted beside her as she walked along the corridor. Human on dry deck. Orison in brine. Separated by transparent membrane, pressure engineering, law, blood chemistry, and the thin mercy of not pretending those were trivial.

"The protocol is not enough," Mira said.

"Yes."

"It may still help."

"Yes."

"I hate that both are true."

Keeps-Water-Calm's cilia opened slowly. "Good. Hatred of insufficient help may keep help from becoming pride."

Mira leaned one shoulder against the wall and let herself be tired.

"Will the witnesses review it?"

"Some will refuse."

"That is allowed."

"Now it is written."

The words landed softly and still hurt.

Behind them, the obligation board changed again:

REPAIR CYCLE FOUR: FIFTY-NINE-SECOND MEDIUM HOLD. ONE TOOL REDESIGN ACCEPTED FOR LIMITED USE. NO CLOSURE.

No closure.

Mira read the line twice.

Keeps-Water-Calm's translator clicked.

"A small useful human sentence," they said.

It was the closest thing to praise Mira expected to survive the week.

Chapter 56: Keeps-Water-Calm's Terms

Keeps-Water-Calm returned to the treaty room with the translator turned off.

The act violated three station habits, two Compact procedural expectations, and one assumption so deep no one had thought to write it down: that when Orison entered a room built for human proceedings, meaning would be converted for human use as quickly as the machines could manage.

The speaker node above the brine channel remained dark.

The chemical ports stayed closed.

The acoustic couplers registered contact and displayed nothing but raw waveform.

Brenn noticed first. Of course he did. Adjudicators survived by noticing when procedure stopped pretending to be furniture.

"Is the translation membrane malfunctioning?" he asked.

Nadi Or, standing beside the wet-side rail with both hands folded behind her back, said, "No."

Pell looked at Mira.

Mira shook her head once. She had not arranged this.

Sera leaned toward her console, then stopped before touching it. That restraint cost her visible effort.

Keeps-Water-Calm waited in the sling, body dim and gathered. Witness-With-Old-Salt hovered behind them. Witness-At-Edge pressed near the lower current. Low-Current-Under-Stone rested to one side, repair discoloration fading but not gone. The room smelled of brine and disinfectant and human systems trying not to panic.

Vennel, who had learned to recognize historical inconvenience when it entered his station, said, "Nadi?"

Nadi did not look away from the brine channel. "Keeps-Water-Calm will speak after terms are acknowledged."

Brenn straightened. "Terms for testimony?"

"Terms for translation."

The distinction moved through the room like a pressure wave.

Pell set down her tablet slowly. "Translation terms are usually technical accommodations."

Nadi nodded. "These are not accommodations."

The dark speaker node remained silent.

Mira remembered the first treaty tank engineers with their good intentions and cheap membranes. They had built the room to avoid insult. They had not built it to let Orison refuse convenience without being treated as obstruction.

Brenn said, "State the terms."

Nadi lifted a wet-carrier strip from a sealed case. "I am authorized to render them once, with the warning that my rendering is not the terms themselves."

"Understood," Brenn said too quickly.

Keeps-Water-Calm's body shifted. No translator spoke, but the room saw the correction.

Brenn caught himself. "Not understood. Received."

The brine lightened by a fraction.

Nadi read.

"First term: No translated sentence may be treated as complete while the originating relation remains ongoing."

Sera whispered, "That breaks transcripts."

"It marks transcripts as broken," Nadi said.

Brenn's stylus hovered over his pad. "How is a proceeding to cite testimony?"

"With incompleteness visible."

"That is not a method."

"It is a boundary."

Mira felt the line settle. Boundary, not accommodation. Humans loved methods because methods promised control. Boundaries promised consequence.

Nadi continued.

"Second term: If a human category is used for convenience, the record must identify what the category fails to carry."

Ren, patched in from archive, made a small approving sound over the channel. "Failure fields."

Sera's eyes lit despite herself. "We can build that into the board."

Brenn said, "Do not design during terms."

"Then think faster."

Pell murmured, "I am begging everyone to remain sanctionable one at a time."

Nadi read the third term.

"Third: Orison silence is not absence, refusal is not hostility, and nonanswer is not evidence of nonpersonhood, nonrelation, dormancy, consent, or waiver."

Pell exhaled through her nose. "That one belongs in every settlement clause."

Brenn glanced at her.

"I said what I said," Pell told him.

"Fourth," Nadi said. "Translation may be withdrawn if the hearing uses Orison speech to close a question the Orison speaker left open."

Vennel leaned back. "That will terrify the observers."

"Good," Sera said.

Mira gave her a look.

Sera spread her hands. "Quietly good."

Nadi's mouth curved very slightly, then settled.

"Fifth: The reef may be referenced in human records only as D-17 for location, not identity. D-17 is a claim wound, not a name."

That struck Mira harder than she expected.

D-17 had been convenient. Necessary, even. A coordinate, a disputed site, a place the law could find. She had used it a hundred times. Claim Site D-17. D-17's wound. D-17's outer lattice. Every use tied the relation to the cut that had made humans notice it.

"What should we call it?" Brenn asked.

Nadi looked at Keeps-Water-Calm. The Orison did not move toward the translator. Their body changed color in a slow gradient, dark to blue to a pale gray that the machines did not interpret.

"No name is offered," Nadi said.

Brenn frowned. "For now?"

Nadi waited.

He corrected himself. "No name is offered."

"Sixth," Nadi read. "No human participant may ask an Orison witness to simplify for legal clarity without entering that request as pressure."

Pell closed her eyes. "I have done that three times this week."

"More," Sera said.

"Do not help."

Nadi looked at Pell, not unkindly. "The term does not prohibit request. It makes the cost visible."

Pell nodded once. "Fair."

"Seventh: Human urgency may be described, but it does not create entitlement to Orison speed."

Tavo, listening remotely from medical, gave a low laugh. "Put that on every creditor packet."

Brenn said, "These terms are broader than witness procedure."

Mira said, "So is the harm."

He did not argue immediately. That was becoming one of the week's more improbable forms of progress.

Nadi set the wet-carrier strip down.

"Those are the terms."

The treaty room held them.

Remote Compact observers began lighting request flags. Brenn muted them before the first objection arrived. Everyone looked at him.

"They may file written objections," he said. "They do not get to interrupt terms before we know whether testimony exists to interrupt."

Sera leaned toward Mira. "I am trying not to like him."

"Keep trying."

Brenn faced the brine channel. "Keeps-Water-Calm, these terms are received by the proceeding. Some require implementation mechanisms before they can be fully adopted. I can provisionally acknowledge them as conditions for voluntary witness participation."

Keeps-Water-Calm did not move.

Nadi said nothing.

Brenn looked at Mira with something close to panic hidden under professional discipline.

Mira said, "You included a human hedge."

"I am human."

"Try again with the hedge named."

He took off his spectacles, though the lenses were display film and not necessary for vision.

"Keeps-Water-Calm," he said, "I am limited by Compact procedure and will likely fail parts of these terms. I acknowledge that failure in advance does not become your obligation to accommodate. I receive the terms as conditions under which you may choose to speak or not speak. If I narrow them for legal function, the narrowing will be recorded as mine."

The brine channel brightened.

The speaker node woke.

Not fully. A single green line appeared under it, thin as a cut stem.

Keeps-Water-Calm touched the membrane.

The translator produced a voice with more static than usual, as if the machine had been forced to remember it was a machine.

"Better," they said.

Several people in the room released breath.

Keeps-Water-Calm continued.

"We do not return to speech because the room has become good. We return because some doors now show where they break the body passing through."

Brenn wrote that down by hand.

"The protocol," Mira said. "Will witnesses review it?"

The Orison's cilia moved slowly.

"Some have already refused."

Mira nodded. "That is in the protocol."

"Written allowance is not yet practiced respect."

"No."

"Witness-With-Old-Salt will review the section on closure. Witness-At-Edge will review refusal rights. Low-Current-Under-Stone will review repair language. I will review translation withdrawal and category failure. No witness reviews worker dependency."

Tavo's panel flickered. "Why not?"

The translator delayed.

"Because worker debt is a human wound made with human tools. Orison can witness its pressure on the cut. We will not become decoration for human labor repair."

Tavo absorbed that.

"Good," he said after a moment. "We have enough people decorating our problems."

Keeps-Water-Calm's color shifted, unreadable to Mira but not dark.

Brenn said, "Will the Orison witnesses accept the protective presumption?"

"Bad question," the translator said.

Brenn stopped. Looked at Mira. Mira did not rescue him this time either.

He tried again. "Will any witness offer terms under which human protective presumption may proceed without being represented as Orison recognition?"

The brine lightened.

"Yes."

Pell whispered, "He learned a new step."

Sera whispered back, "Do not startle him."

Keeps-Water-Calm said, "Protect because you do not understand enough to cut. Do not protect because you believe the relation has proven itself in your court. The first is restraint. The second is vanity wearing care."

Brenn wrote carefully. "Restraint, not recognition."

"Temporary restraint," Keeps-Water-Calm said. "Recognition requires relation. You cannot order relation from a chair."

The adjudicator's face flushed. He looked at the old witness chair from Olan's hearing as if it had betrayed him by existing.

Vennel said, "Operationally, restraint is what we need before the next creditor deadline."

"Human urgency again," Nadi said softly.

Vennel grimaced. "Recorded as pressure."

Sera actually typed it:

HUMAN URGENCY ENTERED AS PRESSURE: CREDITOR DEADLINE, WORKER SHIELD FUNDING, PLATFORM HOLD STABILITY.

The new field appeared on the room display. Not accusation. Not absolution. A weight label.

Keeps-Water-Calm moved one lobe against the membrane.

"This is a small correct ugliness."

Vennel looked as though he would accept ugly if it got him through the hour.

The review began.

It was slower than any human in the room wanted and faster than any Orison witness trusted. Each protocol section had to be opened, read, bounded, and sometimes left unresolved. The translator withdrew twice.

The first withdrawal came when Brenn described the unnamed reef relation as "the affected ecological entity."

The speaker cut to static.

Brenn froze.

Nadi said, "You made a convenience category without failure field."

He corrected the record:

Convenience category used: affected ecological entity.
Failure field: Does not carry relation, continuity, distributed witness, noncentral response, or unnamed status.
Pressure note: Used for procedural reference under human legal format.

The translator returned.

Brenn looked like someone rebuilding his own tongue while speaking.

The second withdrawal came when Pell asked Witness-With-Old-Salt whether closure language might be acceptable after full repair.

The speaker died mid-question.

Pell stared at the dark node.

"I did it," she said.

Sera, unusually merciful, said nothing.

Pell entered her own pressure note:

Counsel asked witness to map future acceptability into settlement closure framework. Request improperly assumed repair could mature into closure.

The translator returned only after she added:

Question withdrawn. Future duty remains open.

Witness-With-Old-Salt touched the membrane once.

"Better lawyer," the translator said.

Pell put both hands flat on the table. "I am choosing to receive that as professional development."

Tavo laughed so hard his remote feed shook and a medic yelled at him offscreen.

For a while after that, the room worked.

Not smoothly. Smoothness would have been suspicious. But the terms changed the shape of failure. Mistakes did not vanish into embarrassment or harden into defense as quickly. They became fields. Notes. Withdrawals. Returns. People learned to say, "pressure entered," without immediately explaining why their pressure deserved priority.

Mira watched Sera build the failure field template into the live transcript. Every translated sentence now carried expandable margins:

Originating signal: partial.
Human category: convenience.
Failure field: pending.
Pressure present: yes/no.
Closure risk: active/inactive.
Witness withdrawal right: active.

It was clumsy. It was ugly. It made the transcript nearly unreadable to anyone seeking clean minutes.

It was the most honest record Mira had ever seen a human institution produce.

Near the fourth hour, Brenn opened the section on refusal.

"The protocol says refusal to answer an instrument is not evidence against the relation," he said. "What is required when refusal prevents classification?"

Keeps-Water-Calm did not withdraw translation. They answered.

"You remain ignorant."

Brenn waited.

The answer did not expand.

He said, "And operationally?"

"You act like ignorance with hands."

Sera whispered, "That's going to ruin someone's day."

Brenn entered:

Operational response to classification-blocking refusal: protective restraint where extraction would destroy unresolved relation; explicit ignorance statement required.

Mira looked at him. "Explicit ignorance statement."

He did not look pleased. "It is what they said."

Keeps-Water-Calm pulsed soft blue.

The room's public board updated after Sera linked the field:

PROTOCOL REVISION: IGNORANCE MUST BE NAMED BEFORE ACTION.

Within two minutes, Drex Platform requested clarification.

Vennel groaned.

Tavo's feed showed him reading the update. "I can clarify. It means if you do not know whether the machine will cut a living relation, do not turn it on."

Pell said, "That is not legally complete."

"It is mechanically complete."

Brenn said, "The formal clarification will take longer."

Keeps-Water-Calm's translator clicked. "Human machines understand short law."

Mira almost laughed. She did not. The week had taught her caution around relief.

The witness review ended not with agreement but with a list of permitted next failures.

That was Nadi's phrase, and everyone hated it until they needed it.

Permitted next failures:

Human protocol may proceed under explicit nonrecognition warning.

Protective restraint may be recommended to Compact pending full adjudication.

Repair language requires Orison review after each cycle; no stable repair may be declared without witness confirmation.

Translation may withdraw without procedural penalty.

Worker transition must proceed without Orison endorsement language.

The unnamed relation must not be called D-17 except when referring to the claim wound or coordinates.

Brenn signed the revised protocol again. This time the signature page included his own failure field:

Adjudicator signature indicates receipt and provisional procedural use. It does not establish full understanding, recognition, or closure.

He looked physically pained by the sentence.

"Good," Sera said.

"I did not ask."

"Still good."

Keeps-Water-Calm remained by the membrane after most of the observers disconnected. The speaker node dimmed but did not go dark.

Mira stayed too.

So did Brenn, though he pretended to reorganize notes. Pell left to fight a creditor objection and returned thirty seconds later because she had forgotten why she stood up. Sera fell asleep at her console with her hand near the failure field template. Nadi covered her with an emergency foil blanket from the evacuation room. Tavo's feed finally cut out when medical won a skirmish.

The room settled into an exhaustion that felt less like collapse than low tide.

Mira approached the membrane.

"Thank you for coming back to speech," she said.

Keeps-Water-Calm's body dimmed.

"Do not thank the return. Practice the terms."

"I will try."

"Try is a word humans use before learning whether the cost is bearable."

"Yes."

"Good. Keep it honest."

Brenn spoke from the table, voice rough. "May I ask one question outside the proceeding?"

The brine channel stirred.

Nadi looked ready to intervene, but Keeps-Water-Calm touched the membrane.

"Ask with your pressure named."

Brenn sat very still. "Pressure: I want to understand because I am tired of harming by format. Also because I want the order I write tomorrow to survive appeal."

Pell, half asleep, murmured, "At least he is honest."

Keeps-Water-Calm said, "Ask."

Brenn swallowed. "If recognition requires relation, how can a court ever recognize anything nonhuman?"

Mira felt the question enter her like cold water. Not because it was new. Because it was finally asked without trying to win.

Keeps-Water-Calm's colors moved slowly.

"A court alone cannot."

Brenn's face fell.

"Then what is the point of the order?"

"To stop the hand long enough for relation to become possible where it has not been destroyed."

No one spoke.

The speaker node hummed softly.

Keeps-Water-Calm continued.

"Humans ask law to do the work of relation because law can move where bodies have not learned to move. This is not wrong. It is incomplete. Let law be a doorstop. Do not mistake it for a room."

Brenn put down his stylus.

"A doorstop," he said.

Pell opened one eye. "Please do not put that in the order."

Sera, from under the foil blanket, whispered, "Put it in a footnote."

Mira looked at Keeps-Water-Calm, at the membrane between them, at the room that had been built to avoid insult and had finally begun to permit refusal.

Translation was not peace.

It was not even understanding.

At best, tonight, it was a door held open without pretending anyone had arrived.

That was enough to keep working.

Chapter 57: The Worker Transition

Tavo refused to hold the transition meeting in the galley.

"People eat there," he told Vennel, which was not strictly true anymore because people also slept, argued, cried quietly, avoided public boards, read creditor notices, and pretended ration coffee counted as decision-making fuel there. But the galley still had benches, heat, and the smell of food, and Tavo would not let the first honest meeting about worker futures contaminate the last room where someone could burn noodles in peace.

They held it on Drex Platform's lower cargo deck instead.

That was also not neutral. Nothing was. The cargo deck held sealed mineral containers that might never ship, tool pallets repurposed for reef repair, emergency bunks for overflow crew, and the blue-hold status of the bore arm projected above the moonpool access like weather no one could stop watching. The place smelled of metal, brine, oil, and exhausted bodies. It was big enough for everyone to stand without asking permission from furniture.

Eighty-one of eighty-three workers attended in person or by suit channel. One was sedated in medical. One was outside on pylon watch and refused to leave because, according to Anika, "someone should keep an eye on the hand we are not scraping."

Mira attended from Cael Station by wall link. So did Brenn, Pell, Vennel, and a Compact labor officer whose name was Nessa Ward and whose packet kept arriving with a lag that made her look as if she regretted everything three seconds after saying it. Drex stood at the back of the cargo deck, not on the raised command rail. Tavo had insisted.

"If you stand above them, I will move the meeting to the moonpool," Tavo had said.

Drex had looked at him and stepped down.

That, more than any apology, made the crew murmur.

Tavo stood on a cargo brace with one hand still bandaged and the other holding a slate he did not look at.

"You all know enough to be angry," he began. "You do not know enough to choose. That is partly by design. Company design, creditor design, some of it my design because I thought keeping bad math away from you kept people steady. That stops now."

No one interrupted.

That was not agreement. It was attention with teeth.

Tavo pointed to the wall. Sera, patched in from Cael Station and awake only because fury had medicinal properties, sent up three columns:

REMAIN UNDER REPAIR TRANSITION

LEAVE UNDER PROTECTED DEBT RELIEF

TESTIFY / CLAIMANT EVIDENCE

Anika folded her arms. "Those sound like forms."

"They are forms," Tavo said. "We are going to put blood back in them."

Pell's voice came over the link. "For the record, none of these options requires an immediate individual election tonight. The purpose is explanation, collective questions, and identification of coercive conditions."

Lio, neck still bandaged, called from the side rail, "Translate lawyer."

Pell sighed. "No one has to sign anything before sleeping."

"Better," Lio said.

The first option was the one management wanted to sound noble.

Remain under repair transition. It would keep a portion of the Drex crew on Pelagos under a temporary nonextractive contract funded by the claim bond, emergency Compact labor funds if approved, and any creditor pool money Pell could force into escrow before they learned to hide behind fiduciary smoke. Workers who stayed would retrain for reef-safe support: tool redesign, pressure screen operation, pylon monitoring, drone maintenance, station logistics, and repair-cycle assistance under Orison witness boundaries.

"Would we work for Drex?" Jae asked.

Drex answered before Tavo could. "No."

The deck turned toward him.

He continued, voice flat. "If the transition is approved, the repair crew cannot remain under my operational authority. Conflict of interest. Also no one should be asked to heal a cut while taking orders from the man who signed the cut schedule."

That put silence into the cargo deck differently.

Anika's jaw tightened. "You still sign our payroll."

"For now. That is one of the problems."

"One?"

"One I can name without defending it."

Tavo watched him carefully. Mira did too through the link. Drex had the posture of a man trying to stand still while every habit in him reached for control.

Nessa Ward's delayed voice entered. "Compact labor can create an emergency independent pay channel if the adjudicator issues protected uncertainty status and if the bond is released."

"If," someone muttered.

"Yes," Ward said three seconds late. "If. You deserve conditions spoken plainly."

Sera added a visible line under option one:

NOT DREX COMMAND. FUNDING CONDITIONAL. SKILLS TRANSFER REQUIRED. ORISON MAY REFUSE WORKER PARTICIPATION IN REPAIR CYCLES.

A worker Mira did not know said, "Why would Orison refuse us?"

Nadi Or answered from Cael Station. "Because participation in repair is not absolution. Some hands may be useful near the wound. Some may not yet be tolerable. Refusal is not a verdict on your whole life."

"Sounds like one."

"Yes," Nadi said. "It may feel that way."

The worker looked away.

Tavo let the discomfort remain. "Second option. Leave under protected debt relief."

Sera changed the display.

Workers who left would receive transport off Pelagos, temporary housing continuation for dependents, medical bridge protection, and suspension of claim-linked debt acceleration for a proposed ninety days. They would not lose standing to later testify, join repair, or pursue wage claims. They would not be required to sign silence clauses. They would not be required to release Drex Maritime, creditors, or Compact authorities from liability.

Pell had written that last part in three forms and one sentence a worker could understand:

LEAVING DOES NOT MAKE WHAT HAPPENED FINE.

Anika stared at the line a long time.

"What about certification debt?" asked a young crane tech near the back.

Pell opened the debt map. "Suspended under the draft. Not erased yet."

"So it comes back."

"If we lose the petition, yes."

"Translate lawyer," Lio said again, less joking this time.

Pell's face softened in a way Mira had not seen before. "We are trying to stop the knife. We have not yet broken it."

The crane tech nodded as if he hated the answer but could stand on it.

The third option made the room colder.

Testify / claimant evidence.

Workers could give protected statements about unsafe scheduling, debt pressure, maintenance overrides, creditor communications, contamination language, and operational acceleration. Testimony could support sanctions against Drex Maritime, creditor committee members, Sivek Hale Consulting, and individual managers. Protection would include anti-retaliation orders, evidence custody, and counsel access. It would not guarantee immunity for workers who had personally falsified records, sabotaged equipment, concealed harm, or lied under prior filings.

"There," Anika said. "There is the hook."

Pell nodded. "Yes."

"You want testimony, but if we say the wrong thing, we become defendants."

"If you intentionally caused harm or falsified evidence, that is possible."

"And if we signed off because Tavo told us to?"

Tavo said, "Then say that."

Anika turned on him. "Easy for you."

"No," he said. "Necessary for me."

The deck went still.

Tavo climbed down from the brace. Not dramatically. Carefully, because his side still hurt and everyone could see it. He stood on the same level as Anika.

"I cleared the elbow joint after an auto-check failure," he said. "I did it under schedule pressure, budget pressure, medical reserve pressure, and because I have spent years learning how to keep bad machines working long enough to pay people. That does not make the clearance true. If you testify that I cleared unsafe work, you will be telling the truth."

Anika's eyes shone with anger. "You want us to cut you loose?"

"No. I want you to stop holding my authority like it can pay your debts."

Someone near the moonpool swore softly.

Drex stepped forward, then stopped himself.

Tavo looked at him. "Your turn."

Pell said, "I advise against-"

Drex cut her off. "I know."

"Olan."

"I know."

He faced the crew.

"I signed the finance instrument. I accepted Hale's advisory memo into operations. I allowed debt pressure to stand inside safety decisions even when I did not call it safety. I did not order anyone to falsify a record. I did build and keep a structure where people knew which answers would be rewarded."

No one moved.

He continued.

"If you testify against Drex Maritime, you may be testifying against me. Do it if it is true. Do not protect me to protect your pay. I cannot promise pay. That is the point everyone else has already made better than I wanted to hear."

Anika laughed once, harsh and unbelieving. "You practicing for court?"

"No final statement," Drex said, and the old hearing line moved through the room.

It should not have helped.

Somehow it did, a little.

Questions came after that.

Not orderly. Not polite. Real questions rarely were.

Would repair transition count toward hazard retirement? Maybe, Ward said, if classified as ecological emergency labor. Could families be moved to Pelagos? No, Vennel said before anyone else could lie; the station could not house them. Could workers refuse Orison contact and still keep transition pay? Yes, Mira said, if the work assignment had alternatives. Would leaving make them look guilty? Pell said no legally, Tavo said some people would say so anyway, and Anika said those people could come say it on the deck.

Could they stay together?

That question came from Jae, the crane operator with dependent oxygen surcharge from Pall.

The room did not answer fast enough.

Jae looked around. "I do not mean all of us. I mean teams. People who know each other's hands. If repair work uses pressure timing, if tool redesign needs people who know which handles slip, if testimony needs someone else saying no, I do remember that right, then splitting us makes us stupid."

Sera pointed at the screen. "That is true."

Brenn said, "Transition election should allow team-based applications without coercing individual choice."

Pell wrote it down.

Ward said, "Funding frameworks prefer individual contracts."

Tavo said, "Funding frameworks can learn."

"They are slow."

"So are people when you break their crews."

Mira watched the cargo deck shift. Not toward unity. That would have been false. Toward visible difference. Some workers clustered around repair transition. Anika stood there, arms still folded, pretending she had not chosen. Lio did too, because rash or not, he hated being left out of a machine that could fail interestingly. Jae moved between repair and testimony, already thinking in teams. Others stood near leaving, faces closed around family names. Three workers stayed at the back and looked at no option, which was also an answer.

Drex watched them the way a person watches a map redraw itself without his borders.

Mira wondered whether he felt loss of control or the first honest shape of the people he had been claiming to protect. Maybe both. People were allowed to be ugly and useful at once. Pelagos had insisted on that lesson repeatedly.

Then the pylon watch worker joined by suit channel.

Her name was Haleen Mor, and she had been on the platform for eleven years, which meant she had survived enough management language to distrust nouns on instinct.

"Blue contact just changed," she said.

The cargo deck froze.

Tavo grabbed the nearest console. "Threat?"

"No. Pattern. Looks like the symbionts are pulling away from the scrape scars, not off the pylon. Making space."

Sera was already routing the pylon feed to the wall. The image appeared: Pylon Two, black metal under floodlight, blue symbionts spread across the lower ribs. Haleen was right. The blue bodies were not retreating. They were opening gaps in their own pattern along the places where human tools had scraped metal in prior maintenance cycles. The gaps formed a shape like branching negative light.

Anika stepped closer.

"They are showing the old marks," she said.

Keeps-Water-Calm was not on the call, but Nadi stood abruptly and listened through a channel Mira could not hear.

When she spoke, her voice was careful. "The pylon contact is making prior tool history visible."

"Why now?" Brenn asked.

No one answered.

Then Tavo did. "Because we are deciding which hands stay."

The cargo deck held that.

Anika looked at her own hands. The silver paint on her cheek had smudged. Her harness was still stained from the bore arm.

"If I stay," she said, "I want tool redesign. Not just repair cycles. I want every handle that made us too fast opened up."

Lio said, "Same."

Jae said, "I want team application."

Haleen's voice came from pylon watch. "I want no one calling blue contact contamination again without losing a tooth."

Pell said, "We can make the first part legal."

"I said what I said."

The first signatures were not signatures.

They were marks beside intentions on a public deck screen. Nonbinding, Pell insisted. Preliminary, Ward said. Witnessed, Tavo said. Imperfect, Mira thought.

Anika marked:

REPAIR TRANSITION - TOOL REDESIGN / PRESSURE CONTROLS.

Lio marked:

REPAIR TRANSITION - FLOW SYSTEMS IF MEDICAL CLEARS.

Jae marked:

TEAM APPLICATION - CRANE / TRACE / PRESSURE SUPPORT.

Haleen marked remotely:

PYLON WATCH / CONTACT PROTOCOL.

Seven workers marked leave under protected debt relief. Then twelve. Then twenty. Some cried when they did it. Some looked relieved. Some looked ashamed, which made Mira angry at every system that had taught escape to feel like abandonment.

Five marked testimony first.

Ren Pellis, bore tech, was among them. Mira had not noticed her enter the deck. She stood near the back, blanket gone, face pale and steady.

"I pulled archive packets under Drex credentials after the first pylon contact," she said. "Not falsified. Unauthorized. I found Hale's memo reference before Olan sent it. I did not report because I thought if I waited I could get more."

Drex stared at her.

Ren looked back. "I did get more."

Pell said carefully, "You need counsel."

"I know."

Tavo said, "Ren."

"No," she said. "I am not confessing to be dramatic. I am mapping the room."

Mira felt Sera go still on the link. That phrase had traveled from repair protocol into the workers and returned changed.

Drex said, "I will not file against you."

Ren's mouth tightened. "You may not be the one who gets to choose."

He had no answer.

The meeting lasted four hours.

By the end, nothing was settled enough to satisfy a funding framework. That meant it was probably close to real. Thirty-two workers entered preliminary repair transition interest. Twenty-six entered protected departure interest. Nine requested counsel for testimony. Fourteen deferred. Two remained unreachable beyond medical and pylon watch. Every mark carried a pressure note: dependent oxygen, medical bridge, certification debt, fear of Orison refusal, fear of losing crew, fear of staying, fear of leaving, anger at Drex, loyalty to Tavo, no trust in Compact, no trust in creditors, no trust in themselves.

Sera built the pressure notes into the transition board because, as she put it, "If we hide the coercion, the form becomes a trap with better lighting."

Ward said the board would be administratively difficult.

Everyone ignored her.

When the meeting ended, workers did not disperse quickly. They stood in small groups beneath the blue-hold light, arguing in lower voices. Some went to pylon feeds. Some to medical. Some to the galley after all, because people still needed heat and noodles and the right to be messy near food.

Tavo remained on the cargo deck with Drex.

Mira kept the link open but muted herself.

For a while neither man spoke.

Then Drex said, "You should have told me you were carrying that much of them."

Tavo looked at him with exhausted disbelief. "I did. You called it staffing risk."

Drex flinched.

Good, Mira thought. Not enough. Good.

"I thought if I kept the company alive, I kept the crew alive," Drex said.

"You kept the creditor room alive too."

"Yes."

"Did you know when it changed?"

Drex looked toward the moonpool where black water reflected blue status light. "No."

Tavo nodded once. "That is the first answer tonight I believe all the way down."

It was not forgiveness.

It was not even repair.

But it was the kind of sentence a room could build from if it survived the night.

On the pylon feed, the blue symbionts held their new gaps around old scrape scars. The platform did not interpret. The workers did not cheer. No one called it a message.

But people looked at the marks before they looked at the forms.

That was a beginning, or at least a refusal to end in the old place.

Chapter 58: Shell-That-Returns Answers

Shell-That-Returns did not arrive.

Arrival was a human convenience. It implied a body crossing a threshold, a moment before and a moment after, a thing that had been elsewhere and was now here to be addressed. What answered from the reef was older and less polite than arrival. It thickened through water chemistry, pressure delay, symbiont spacing, repair-medium residue, and the tiny acoustics of tools held still. It made the station's instruments draw breath they did not possess.

The first sign came on the continuing obligation board.

REPAIR CYCLE FIVE: SIXTY-TWO-SECOND MEDIUM HOLD.

Then the line deleted itself.

Sera, who was sleeping in a chair beside the board with her boots on a cable crate and an emergency foil blanket sliding off one shoulder, woke instantly.

"No," she said to the board.

The board did not care.

It rewrote:

REPAIR CYCLE FIVE: MEDIUM HELD UNTIL HUMAN COUNT BECAME TOO SMALL.

Sera stared.

Then she reached for her console and stopped with her hand in the air because touching the board before knowing whether it had been altered by fault, intrusion, Orison contact, or something more embarrassing would be bad science and worse diplomacy.

"Mira," she called.

Mira had been in the adjacent corridor arguing with Nessa Ward about whether protected debt relief could be funded before the creditor pool acknowledged receipt of its own obligations. She stepped into the board alcove, saw the line, and forgot the sentence she had been carrying.

"Did you write that?"

"If I had," Sera said, "it would have more contempt and a hash."

The board changed again.

WHAT HAS NOT YET BEEN LEARNED:

HOW TO COUNT WITHOUT MAKING SMALL.

People began to gather.

Workers first, as always. Anika came with a tool belt still on. Lio came from medical with his neck seal open. Jae arrived carrying a pressure trace slate. Ren appeared from archive without shoes. Tavo joined by wall panel and swore when he saw the board. Brenn arrived last, but only because he was sleeping under adjudicator privilege and two legal observers had debated whether waking him required an order.

He read the board once.

"Who has write authority?"

Sera said, "Not funny."

"It was not meant to be."

"Station board authority is me, Mira, Vennel, archive emergency, and the maintenance daemon for status feeds."

"Daemon?"

"Software, not theology."

Brenn looked as if he would have preferred theology. "Was it compromised?"

Sera ran checks without looking away from the display. "No external network intrusion. No credential use. No daemon command. The board received a valid data update from repair telemetry, then a text alteration from the Orison witness channel."

Nadi Or arrived breathless. "No Orison witness sent text."

The alcove quieted.

Mira turned toward the brine channel running beneath the board. Its water had darkened, not with contamination but with density: chemical markers thick enough that the channel lights diffused.

Keeps-Water-Calm emerged from the side current slowly, attendants absent.

"Not witness," the translator said.

Brenn asked, "Then what?"
