Keeps-Water-Calm did not answer immediately. Their body remained half in shadow. When the translator spoke again, its voice carried layered static, as if too many source patterns were trying to pass through one throat.

"Shell-That-Returns touches the human board and finds it thin."

No one moved.

The name had appeared earlier in the week as an Orison reference almost too large to hold in human hearing. Shell-That-Returns: elder reef polity, old continuity, pressure memory, not individual, not committee, not ancestor in the finished sense. More environment than speaker. More speaker than environment. A political presence distributed through reef relation and Orison witness lines.

Mira had hoped, foolishly, that if it ever responded, it would respond through the treaty room.

Instead it had edited a status board in a corridor where workers tracked unfinished duties.

Sera whispered, "That is not allowed."

Keeps-Water-Calm's color shifted. "Correct."

Brenn said, "Is this a breach of station systems?"

"Human word."

"I need a human answer."

"Then yes and no. The board listened to repair telemetry. Repair telemetry listened to medium. Medium listened to carrier wound. Carrier wound listened to Shell-That-Returns. Your system was not broken. It was more connected than you admitted."

Brenn took that like a legal injury.

Sera was already copying every log. "The text alteration came through semantic mapping on the Orison witness channel. But no witness sent it. The channel inferred a translation from chemical density and board context."

Nadi's face tightened. "The machine translated without permission."

"It tried to," Sera said. "It may have succeeded badly."

Keeps-Water-Calm pulsed dark warning. "Bad success is dangerous."

The board changed:

BAD SUCCESS IS STILL A CUT.

Sera stepped back.

Anika said softly, "It is listening to this."

Mira said, "Or the relation includes this."

"That is not better."

"No."

Brenn looked at the brine channel. "Shell-That-Returns, if this is testimony, I need terms."

Keeps-Water-Calm's body tightened. "Do not summon."

The board flickered.

DO NOT SUMMON WHAT YOU HAVE ENTERED.

Brenn closed his mouth.

Tavo's wall panel crackled. "Can someone translate that for a person who works with bolts?"

Nadi said, "You cannot call it as a witness because you are already inside the relation that produced the answer."

Tavo considered. "So we do not ask it questions like a person on a chair."

"Yes."

"Then what do we do?"

The board held blank for several seconds.

Then:

STOP MAKING GRATITUDE.

The words struck the alcove harder than any alarm.

Mira understood too quickly and not enough. Human institutions loved gratitude from the harmed. Gratitude meant the order had worked. Gratitude meant protection could be narrated as benevolence. Gratitude meant the protected party had accepted the protector's centrality.

Brenn's face changed. He understood enough too.

Pell, arriving at the edge of the group with a half-open creditor packet in one hand, read the line and said, "Oh."

Anika looked at her. "Oh what?"

Pell swallowed. "Every draft response from Compact Public Continuity to the hold order uses gratitude language. Grateful cooperation. Orison gratitude for restraint. Workers grateful for relief. Public gratitude for resolution."

Tavo said, "Delete all of it."

"Gladly."

The board changed:

RESTRAINT IS NOT A GIFT TO THE WOUND.

Lio said, "I like it."

Sera looked at him.

"What? I can like something and be terrified."

The chemical density in the brine channel rose. The translator began producing fragments without prompting:

"Tool held above skin..."

"Hand says mercy..."

"Skin says the hand should not have come..."

"Do not ask the cut to praise the pause..."

Nadi raised both hands. "Stop the translator."

Sera cut the semantic layer. The speaker died, but the board remained lit.

Keeps-Water-Calm moved closer to the membrane. Without the semantic layer, their body had no voice. They touched the brine wall beneath the board once, then again, slower.

The board did not change.

Sera whispered, "It cannot hear them now."

"It may not need to," Mira said.

Brenn took one careful step forward. "What is the safest way to proceed?"

Nobody laughed. That was progress.

Nadi answered. "First, stop the board from translating. Not because the answer is unwelcome. Because translation without terms repeats the harm."

Sera nodded. "I can freeze text capture and keep raw telemetry."

"Second," Nadi said, "do not copy the displayed lines into order language as if Shell-That-Returns filed them."

Brenn lowered his stylus.

Nadi saw. "Yes, you were about to."

"I was about to preserve evidence."

"You were about to make it a quote."

He looked pained. "Yes."

"Preserve the occurrence. Not ownership of words."

Brenn entered instead:

Unsolicited semantic rendering event occurred through continuing obligation board during repair telemetry update. Rendering is not accepted as authorized testimony. Raw telemetry preserved. Translation terms unresolved. Institutional gratitude language suspended pending witness review.

Sera read over his shoulder. "That is... not terrible."

"Your confidence sustains me."

"It should not."

Pell was already editing Public Continuity drafts on her tablet with the expression of someone taking a blade to upholstery. "Removing gratitude. Removing healing. Removing partnership where it implies endorsement. Removing closure. Removing successful engagement. Removing mutually beneficial."

Anika said, "Mutually beneficial?"

Pell did not look up. "I said removing."

The board stayed frozen on STOP MAKING GRATITUDE.

Mira found herself unable to look away.

Keeps-Water-Calm drifted beside her in the brine channel.

"Did Shell-That-Returns mean to answer the hold order?" Mira asked.

No translator answered. Sera had cut the layer.

Nadi watched the Orison's colors and pressure movement. "They say: mean is narrow."

"Then why now?"

Nadi waited, reading. "Because human restraint had begun to become a story about human goodness."

Brenn said quietly, "It often does."

Tavo snorted from the wall panel. "Try always."

Mira thought of the order: no extraction, no scraping, no active pulse, no alteration of symbiont contact except life safety. She had wanted it desperately. She still wanted it. But desire could polish itself into virtue if no one stopped it. Shell-That-Returns had stopped it with four ugly words on a public board.

Stop making gratitude.

"The workers need relief," Ward said from delayed link, sounding more careful than she had earlier. "If we remove gratitude language from the funding request, some committees will consider it hostile."

Anika turned toward the labor officer's screen. "Then they want thanks more than they want us housed."

Ward's delay made the silence awkward.

"Yes," Ward said finally. "Some do."

It was the first useful thing she had said that no policy office would enjoy.

Pell said, "We can frame relief as obligation under protective uncertainty, not goodwill."

"Do that," Tavo said.

Ward nodded. "I can support that."

The board remained frozen, but the brine channel shifted again. Sera's raw telemetry display showed layered chemical gradients moving in a slow outward pulse from the repair channel. Not toward the station. Through it. The pattern touched pylon feed, repair medium logs, obligation board, worker transition marks, and the frozen text event as if all of them had become surfaces in the same water.

Sera opened a map of linked systems.

"This is not just the board," she said. "The relation is moving through every system we made carry obligation. Repair data. Pylon contact. Worker transition board. The hold order. Debt relief draft. It is not entering random systems."

Brenn leaned in. "So it is distinguishing relevance."

"Careful," Mira said.

Sera grimaced. "It is propagating along relevance we created."

Keeps-Water-Calm's body lightened.

Nadi said, "Better."

The corridor had filled. More workers. Station staff. Two Orison liaison interns. A medic still wearing gloves. Drex stood at the back, silent, face unreadable.

Mira had not seen him arrive.

He looked at the frozen board, then at Anika, Tavo's panel, Pell's redlined draft, Brenn's careful note, the brine channel, the workers who had marked leave and stay and testimony.

No one asked him what he thought.

After a while he said, "My board sent a draft public statement."

Pell did not look up. "I will enjoy hating it."

"It thanks the Orison for constructive engagement."

The corridor reacted in layers: groans, bitter laughter, one sharp curse from Lio.

Drex continued, "I have not released it."

Anika said, "Do you want praise?"

He looked at the board.

"No."

Tavo said, "Then what?"

"A replacement sentence."

That was not nothing.

Mira looked at Keeps-Water-Calm. No translation. No easy answer.

Brenn said, "Drex Maritime acknowledges that restraint under order does not repair harm, confer endorsement, or create gratitude duties from affected Orison witnesses, workers, or relations. DME remains under obligation to preserve evidence, fund protected transition as ordered, and support repair without claiming reconciliation."

Pell stared at him. "That is almost usable."

"Do not sound surprised."

"I am professionally required to be surprised when useful language enters from a bench."

Drex nodded once. "Send it."

Pell lifted a hand. "Not yet. Add creditor obligation."

Drex's mouth tightened. "Add it."

Pell did.

The replacement statement went out twelve minutes later.

It did not thank anyone.

Within five minutes, Harrow Meridian Underwriting objected to the phrase obligation to fund protected transition. Within seven, Public Continuity objected to lack of healing language. Within nine, Aster Vale Credit requested clarification on whether obligation implied admission. Within ten, the continuing obligation board updated again despite Sera's freeze.

Not text this time.

The line STOP MAKING GRATITUDE dimmed. Under it appeared a raw telemetry trace: a slow pressure curve from repair medium contact, crossed by symbiont spacing on Pylon Two and worker transition marks. No words. No translation. Just relation displayed without a sentence.

Sera checked the logs and looked stunned.

"It did not write," she said. "It changed which data the board foregrounded. The board is still doing board things."

"Is that safer?" Brenn asked.

"No. Maybe. It is less theft."

Nadi nodded. "Less theft is sometimes the first human art."

Sera looked wounded. "I am receiving that as technical feedback."

Workers moved closer to the board. They understood some of it and not all of it. That seemed better than the words had been. Lio pointed at the repair curve and argued with Jae about whether the medium hold had failed at sixty-two seconds or stopped being countable there. Anika studied the pylon spacing and then opened a tool redesign note. Ren began linking the raw event to archive without assigning a quote.

Drex remained at the back.

Mira approached him.

"Your board will hate the statement," she said.

"They hate most things they cannot monetize."

"Will they remove you?"

"They may try."

"Do you care?"

He looked tired enough for the question to hurt. "Yes."

She respected him more for not pretending otherwise.

"Will caring change what you do?"

His gaze moved to the workers, then to the board, then to the brine channel where Keeps-Water-Calm waited without translating the room for him.

"Less than it used to," he said.

That was not redemption. Mira had lost patience with redemption as a category. It made harm into a story about the person who caused it. But it was data. Useful data, if watched skeptically.

The corridor stayed full for a long time.

No ceremony formed. No one knew what to do with an answer that refused to become a message. That was the point, perhaps. Shell-That-Returns had not thanked them, blessed them, condemned them cleanly, or appeared as a witness to be entered into evidence.

It had made the systems carrying obligation show their own shape.

Late in the cycle, Repair Team Three reported a seventy-three-second medium hold.

The board displayed it as a curve, not a victory.

No one clapped.

Anika, watching the curve, said, "Again."

The word passed through workers first, then station staff, then the repair channel. Again as instruction, not triumph. Again as obligation, not ritual. Again without gratitude.

In the brine below the board, Keeps-Water-Calm's body softened into colors Mira still could not read.

Nadi stood beside her and did not translate.

For once, Mira did not ask.

Chapter 59: No Clean Claim

The final claim language died in revision.

Not with a verdict. Not with a speech. Not with a dramatic cancellation that would let anyone pretend a clean moral hand had descended from orbit and corrected the record. It died the way dangerous language often died when enough people were forced to look at every verb: by being crossed out, qualified, narrowed, annotated, and finally made too honest to perform the work it had been designed to do.

The document began the morning still titled:

DREX MARITIME EXTRACTION CONDITIONAL CLAIM D-17, SELECTIVE LATTICE SEPARATION AUTHORIZATION.

By the time Sera finished putting failure fields beside every noun, the title looked diseased.

DREX MARITIME EXTRACTION [RESPONSIBLE CLAIMANT / PRIOR CUTTING PARTY] CONDITIONAL CLAIM [CONTESTED / UNDER PROTECTIVE UNCERTAINTY] D-17 [COORDINATE AND CLAIM WOUND, NOT IDENTITY], SELECTIVE LATTICE SEPARATION [PROHIBITED PENDING RESOLUTION; TERM PRESERVES EXTRACTION ASSUMPTION] AUTHORIZATION [WITHDRAWN AS TO EXTRACTION; RETAINED AS TO LIABILITY AND DUTIES].

Sera stared at it with satisfaction that bordered on medical concern.

Brenn said, "No court can issue a title that long."

"Then no court should have made the original title carry that many lies."

"I did not make the original title."

"You inherited the rake."

"I am aware."

They were in Breakwater Court again, though no one called it a hearing at first. Hearing implied a shape. This was more like surgery performed on a contract while the contract's previous owners protested from sealed channels. The central evidence well displayed the claim file, the hold order, the nonextractive evidence protocol, worker transition board, creditor objections, repair telemetry, pylon contact logs, and Shell-That-Returns' unsolicited rendering event preserved as occurrence rather than quotation.

Mira sat at the scientific table with Sera, Nadi, and Ren. Pell sat opposite with a stack of respondent and labor documents dense enough to bend light. Drex sat beside her because he was still field director until someone found a legally useful way to remove him or he resigned in a manner that did not dump the crew into creditor custody. Tavo attended from Drex Platform, not medical this time. He had bullied his way onto the cargo deck and sat in a bolted chair with three workers making sure he did not stand.

Keeps-Water-Calm watched from the brine channel. The translator remained active under terms. Every translated sentence carried margins now. The record looked terrible. It looked true.

Brenn opened the session by removing the word authorization.

"Extraction authorization cannot survive the current record," he said.

Drex's creditor representatives objected before his sentence finished. They appeared as delayed packets in three windows: Harrow Meridian smooth and bloodless, Aster Vale all procedure, Pelagic Infrastructure pretending to care about worker stability now that worker stability had become a lever pointed back at them.

Harrow spoke first. "Adjudicator, withdrawal of authorization without final status determination constitutes uncompensated interference with a lawful mineral claim."

Brenn did not look impressed. "The authorization is conditional."

"The conditions have not been adjudicated to finality."

"The conditions have been shown insufficient to protect the subject of the claim from irreversible harm during adjudication."

"That statement assumes the subject is protectable."

"No. It assumes irreversible harm during uncertainty is a problem law can recognize before deciding which name it likes."

Sera whispered, "Doorstop."

Pell whispered back, "Footnote later."

Brenn continued. "The claim does not become void. It becomes nonextractive pending conversion into obligations."

Aster Vale's representative pounced. "Conversion into obligations is not contemplated under the mineral framework."

Ren put three archive references on the display before Brenn could answer: salvage claims converted to hazard stewardship after navigation contamination, terraforming leases converted to climate repair duties after unauthorized atmospheric seeding, and one ugly old precedent where a generation ship fuel claim had become a passenger survival trust after the fuel was found to be structural habitat mass.

"Human law does this when humans are harmed," Ren said. "It only acts surprised when the harmed relation is not shaped like a plaintiff."

The Aster representative hesitated. "Those precedents involve recognized parties."

Keeps-Water-Calm's translator clicked softly.

"Recognition often arrives wearing the clothes of who survived long enough to be inconvenient."

Brenn entered the line with failure fields. He did not treat it as final law. He did not ignore it either.

The second death was selective.

Selective lattice separation had sounded narrow, careful, almost surgical. In the original claim, it promised that Drex would remove only specific mineral architecture outside habitation zones, with buffers, monitoring, and reef-safe methods as then defined. After a week on Pelagos, the phrase no longer looked careful. It looked like a blade explaining its etiquette.

Mira stood to address it.

"Selective separation is physically misleading," she said. "The target lattice participates in adaptive acoustic relation, carrier continuity, symbiont response, repair medium behavior, and Shell-That-Returns' pressure field. Separation cannot be represented as selective when the relation damaged by separation is not bounded by the mineral lobe selected."

She displayed the latest repair telemetry: seventy-three-second hold, then eighty-one, then a failed cycle where the medium dispersed but the reef did not flare distress. Repair was not success. It was becoming less ignorant.

"The cut already made the boundaries we used to justify the cut less reliable," Mira said.

Drex looked down at his hands.

Pell said, "Respondent does not contest that selective was operationally incomplete."

The creditor windows lit up with objections.

Drex looked at Pell. "Respondent?"

"You are respondent today," she said. "Try to enjoy the variety."

He almost smiled. Almost.

Pelagic Infrastructure tried worker arguments next. "If extraction is prohibited, the worker transition funding must be guaranteed before claim conversion. Otherwise labor harm compounds ecological uncertainty."

Tavo leaned into his camera. "Correct."

Everyone turned toward his panel.

He shrugged. "Bad people can say useful sentences if the room is ugly enough."

Pelagic's representative looked briefly unsure whether to be offended.

Tavo continued. "But funding does not come after you keep a claim alive as hostage. It comes because the claim made the hostage. You bundled housing, remittance, medical bridge, and certification debt into mineral yield. That means when mineral yield becomes prohibited under protected uncertainty, those worker obligations do not disappear. They detach from extraction and stay with the money that tried to make extraction necessary."

Pell sat very still.

Brenn said, "Crew Chief Rusk, are you reading from prepared testimony?"

"No."

"You may want to."

"I was busy not dying."

"Understood."

Pell wrote quickly. "Say the last sentence again."

Tavo did.

The room changed around it.

Mira had watched legal shifts before. They were rarely dramatic. A phrase found a joint. A joint found pressure. A structure that had seemed immovable began, silently, to bear load in a new direction.

Worker obligations detach from extraction and stay with the money that tried to make extraction necessary.

Brenn entered it as argument, not finding. Then he built the finding around it.

"The recovery instrument tied worker dependency to claim yield," he said. "Where claim yield is suspended or converted because the claim cannot proceed without unresolved harm, worker dependency cannot be treated as external loss. It is internal to the claim structure."

Harrow objected. Aster objected. Pelagic requested recess. Brenn denied all three in different tones of boredom.

Drex spoke for the first time.

"Use the bond."

Pell closed her eyes.

The creditor windows went still.

Drex said it again. "Use the full claim bond. Environmental damage, labor transition, evidence preservation, repair support. Then come after DME assets for the rest."

Harrow's representative recovered first. "Field Director Drex lacks authority to voluntarily surrender bond priority."

Drex looked at the window. "Then record my lack of authority beside your refusal."

Pell opened one eye. "That is either reckless or useful."

"I am accepting both as possible."

Mira studied him. She did not forgive him. That was not her role and not the reef's burden. But she saw, with the same reluctant respect she had felt earlier, that he had stopped trying to make the record protect his shape.

The third death was dormant.

Brenn read the original clause aloud:

Target site classified as dormant or inactive mineral-biogenic architecture with no active mobile Orison presence within claim boundary.

He paused afterward.

"I cannot leave that sentence alive."

Sera said, "Frame it."

"No."

He replaced it with:

Prior classification of target site as dormant relied on metabolic thresholds insufficient to evaluate continuity relation, adaptive acoustic response, symbiont contact, repair-medium behavior, and Orison witness terms. Dormant is withdrawn as an operative classification. Low measured metabolic exchange remains a data point only and carries a prohibited inference warning.

Pell added her warning from the protocol:

The term low metabolic exchange does not establish absence of relation, witness, continuity, sovereignty, or harm.

Keeps-Water-Calm touched the membrane.

"Less wrong," the translator said.

Brenn looked up. "I will take it."

"Taking is not the word."

He sighed, then corrected the record. "Received."

The fourth death was clean.

It had hidden everywhere. Clean evidence. Clean claim. Clean extraction corridor. Clean compliance. Clean order. Clean record.

Ren found it first in the executive summary.

"Clean claim pathway," she read, and made a sound Mira had never heard from an archivist in public.

Pell said, "That was creditor language."

"Of course it was."

Drex said quietly, "I used it too."

Ren looked at him. "Yes."

Brenn brought up every instance. Twenty-seven. By the time the search finished, the word clean had become obscene.

Mira said, "Clean should be prohibited in the converted order."

Aster objected automatically. "Clean has established regulatory meaning."

"Not here," Mira said. "Here it concealed disputed harm, debt pressure, translation failure, prior tool marks, and category insufficiency. There is no clean claim."

The central display, still linked to the continuing obligation board in read-only mode, shifted foreground data without text: pylon scrape gaps, repair-medium curve, worker transition marks, creditor objection timestamps, low metabolic proxy, adaptive acoustic ladder. The board did not write. It did not need to.

Brenn removed clean from the order.

He replaced it with documented, limited, contested, preserved, or prohibited, depending on the sentence. Some sentences collapsed without clean. Those he deleted.

The room worked until station lights cycled toward evening.

By then the original claim had become something else:

PROTECTIVE CONVERSION ORDER: D-17 CLAIM WOUND AND ASSOCIATED CONTINUITY RELATION.

Drex Maritime's extraction claim was not approved, not denied as if denial alone solved harm, but converted. All extraction rights suspended indefinitely pending full Compact review under the new protocol. Claim bond released first to repair support, worker transition, evidence preservation, and Orison witness accommodation under terms that refused consent language. Creditor recovery subordinated to labor shield and ecological repair duties. Sivek Hale Consulting and the creditor committee named as interested pressure actors subject to evidence demand. Field Director Drex removed from unilateral operational authority but retained as compelled respondent for implementation until replacement governance could protect workers from creditor seizure. Crew transition recognized as internal remedy, not charitable mitigation. No gratitude language. No closure language. No clean claim.

It was not elegant.

It was not enough.

It was an order with mud on its boots.

Brenn read the operative paragraphs aloud because spoken law still mattered to humans even when it failed almost everything else.

When he finished, no one celebrated.

Harrow filed notice of challenge. Aster filed reservation of rights. Pelagic asked whether cooperation could reduce exposure. Pell smiled at that in a way that made Mira glad they were currently on the same side of a very crooked table.

Drex signed the implementation acknowledgment.

Tavo signed the worker transition witness line.

Anika signed as preliminary repair team representative, then stared at the title for a long time.

"It still says D-17," she said.

Brenn nodded. "Coordinates and claim wound only."

"Not name."

"Not name."

She signed.

Keeps-Water-Calm did not sign. Neither did any Orison witness. The order said why in three different places.

That absence felt, to Mira, like the most honest signature in the room.

After the session closed, Drex remained seated while the creditor windows went dark one by one.

Pell gathered her documents. "You may be removed before the next cycle."

"Yes."

"You may be sued by your own board."

"Likely."

"You may also face Compact sanction personally."

"I know."

She looked at him over the stack. "You understand I am still your counsel in some of that."

"Unfortunate for you."

"Frequently."

He almost smiled again, then did not.

Mira passed behind them on her way to the exit.

Drex said, "Doctor Cael."

She stopped.

"Is there anything I can do that does not make this about me?"

It was the first question from him that had no visible defense attached.

Mira considered lying kindly. She decided he had done enough harm to deserve a useful answer instead.

"Yes," she said. "Do implementation work no one will thank you for. Do not narrate it. Do not ask whether it repairs you. Let people leave. Let them testify. Fund what you can before someone takes your pen away. If you are removed, make the handover harder for the creditors and easier for the workers."

He nodded slowly.

"That is a long list."

"You asked for anything."

"Fair."

She left him there.

In the corridor, the continuing obligation board had updated.

No words from Shell-That-Returns. No statements. Just the order status, the repair curve, labor funding partially released, creditor challenges pending, and the line:

WHAT HAS NOT YET BEEN LEARNED:

HOW TO OBEY WITHOUT CLAIMING GOODNESS.

Sera stood in front of it, arms crossed.

"The board is getting personal," she said.

"Is that a technical finding?"

"Not yet."

Mira looked at the line. "Do we freeze it?"

"No. It came through authorized board edit."

"By whom?"

Sera pointed.

Ren stood at the archive station, not looking sorry.

"It seemed accurate," Ren said.

Mira let herself laugh, just once.

The sound startled her.

Keeps-Water-Calm moved in the brine below the board. The translator remained off. Their body did not brighten into approval or darken into rebuke. The relation did not become simple because human law had produced a more careful order.

But in the repair channel, medium held for ninety-one seconds before thinning.

In the cargo deck, workers argued over team assignments instead of whether they would be abandoned by morning.

In the legal docket, the word clean no longer had a place to hide.

That was not victory.

Mira was beginning to distrust victory almost as much as closure.

It was work remaining visible.

For Pelagos, for now, that had to be enough.

Chapter 60: Beneath the Reef of Glass

Mira returned to the Quiet Shelf after the claim stopped being a claim.

No one called the journey symbolic, which was how she knew they had learned at least one useful thing. It was not closure. It was not pilgrimage. It was not a victory inspection. It was a field visit under protective conversion order, repair cycle review, witness terms, worker transition observation, and station safety protocol. Every label was ugly. Every label prevented a cleaner lie.

She preferred ugly by then.

The sled moved slowly across the glass ice with Sera at the instrument rack, Anika at the redesigned pressure controls, Jae supervising tether balance, and Lio grumbling over flow canisters because medical had cleared him for "limited nonheroic work," a phrase he had already promised to disobey only in spirit. Tavo attended by suit channel from the cargo deck, where he was supposed to be seated and almost certainly was not. Brenn rode in the rear position, not as judge in session but as receiving witness under his own failure field. Nadi Or coordinated from the lower water. Keeps-Water-Calm moved beneath the ice.

Drex was not on the sled.

He had asked to join. Mira had said no. He had accepted the answer without argument, which did not make him transformed. It made him absent in the first useful way he had managed.

He had spent the previous ten hours doing work no one thanked him for. He signed bond release authorizations until the board revoked one channel and Pell found another. He sent worker transition payroll keys to Nessa Ward before Harrow Meridian could freeze them. He transmitted three internal communications showing Hale's office had warned the creditor committee that "relational evidence expansion" could become a claim threat if allowed to mature. He ordered Drex Maritime not to challenge the first twenty protected departures, then wrote the order again after counsel removed the word voluntary from places it did not belong. By morning, his board had suspended him from new claim operations and retained him under protest for implementation duties because firing him too quickly would make their own record uglier.

Pell called it the first honest personnel decision Drex Maritime had made in years.

Tavo called it "being useful while cornered."

Mira did not call it anything.

The ice under the sled creaked.

Sera looked up. "Normal microfracture."

"You said normal without flinching," Mira said.

"I am growing as a person and declining as a brand."

Brenn, stiff in a borrowed pressure suit, said, "Please do not develop humor in technical reports."

"Too late."

Anika adjusted the redesigned handle assembly with two gloved fingers. The old extraction-scale notch had been replaced by a smooth pressure arc and three tactile stops workers could feel through suit gloves. It looked temporary because it was. It also looked like someone had admitted the hand mattered.

"Control response is good," Anika said. "Still too much play at the lower end."

Sera checked the trace. "Because someone insisted on field-fitting it with a hand tool."

"Someone did not have a fabrication bay."

"Someone could say thank you for the damping collar."

Anika's helmet turned toward her. "Are we making gratitude?"

Sera paused.

Lio laughed over the channel.

Sera said, "No. I am making a petty invoice."

"Then rejected."

The exchange should have been ordinary. On Pelagos, ordinary had become precious. Not easy. Not safe. Precious.

Beneath the ice, the reef spread in pale branching darkness.

Mira had seen it dozens of times now through feeds, scans, pressure maps, acoustic returns, legal exhibits, repair telemetry, and the unwilling poetry of human category failure. None of that prepared her for crossing above it again after the order. The first descent had been toward a disputed mineral claim. This crossing was toward an unnamed relation under guard. The reef had not changed enough for human eyes to see. The room around it had.

"Quiet Shelf point three," Sera said. "Passive only. No active pulse. No semantic layer. Raw telemetry to three sealed archives, one Orison witness stream, one public obligation record. Failure fields preloaded. Human urgency entered as pressure."

Brenn said, "You enjoy saying that last part."

"I enjoy making it impossible to forget."

"That is different."

"Yes."

Mira watched his face through the suit reflection. Brenn had not slept. None of them had slept enough to make good decisions, but he had the special exhaustion of someone whose profession had survived by becoming less confident. His temporary order would be challenged. His name would become attached to an ugly procedural invention no one could cite cleanly and everyone would have to read. He would be accused of creating uncertainty, rewarding obstruction, exceeding authority, and letting metaphor into mineral law. Some of those accusations would not be entirely wrong.

He knew it.

"You could narrow the final filing," Mira said.

He glanced at her. "Is that advice?"

"No. A test."

"Then I decline to fail it." He looked down at the ice. "The order is already narrower than reality and broader than comfort. That may be the best law can do before it becomes honest enough to be replaced."

Sera's eyebrows rose.

Brenn sighed. "Do not make a face."

"I am enjoying your deterioration."

"Professional deterioration."

"Still."

They reached the scan point.

This time no one rushed to deploy instruments. The sled settled. The drones held. Under the ice, pale currents moved through unseen channels. Keeps-Water-Calm's presence registered only as chemical traces and pressure changes. No locator. No demand to be convenient.

Mira opened the field log.

"Field visit under Protective Conversion Order, cycle six review. Purpose: observe repair medium behavior at prior claim wound coordinates and evaluate whether nonextractive instrument chain can remain passive while maintaining public obligation record. No attempt to name unnamed relation. D-17 used only as coordinate and claim wound reference."

She stopped.

Old habits wanted a cleaner sentence.

She let the sentence remain ugly.

Sera deployed the first passive coupler. Anika tested the pressure arc without flow. Jae checked drone tether slack. Lio primed medium, not for full repair but for a controlled contact pass approved by Low-Current-Under-Stone and Witness-At-Edge. Nadi confirmed witness terms from below.

The line from Keeps-Water-Calm came through without semantic translation first: pressure, chemical density, a slow pulse in the channel. Nadi rendered only after receiving assent.

"Proceed if the hand remembers it has a back."

Lio said, "I need that translated for people with canisters."

Anika answered before Nadi could. "Do not push like the whole world is in your palm."

"That I can use."

They proceeded.

The repair medium entered the contact channel in a thin white thread. It moved differently than it had during the first repair choir. Not obedient. Not mastered. Better accompanied. Human pressure eased in small arcs. Orison repair bodies adjusted without converging too fast. The carrier edge accepted contact, rejected it, accepted again. The trace rose, held, thinned, braided.

Sera did not count aloud.

No one did.

The obligation board back at Cael Station displayed the curve without seconds. Ren had changed the default unit after Shell-That-Returns' answer. Time remained available in the raw data. The public line no longer made duration into the first truth.

Jae whispered, "It is holding."

Mira looked at him.

"Sorry," he said. "Not counting. Just... witnessing."

"Witnessing is allowed," Brenn said.

Everyone turned toward him.

He looked offended. "I am capable of learning vocabulary."

The medium held through one pressure shift, then another. A small flare passed along the reef edge, pale blue to amber to dark. Not distress. Not approval. A change. Mira forced herself not to reach for meaning faster than the record could bear.

Keeps-Water-Calm's pressure trace moved under the ice.

Nadi rendered: "Contact remains unfinished."

Anika's voice shook. "Good."

No one corrected her.

The medium thinned and broke.

Lio cut flow. Anika released pressure. Jae steadied drone tethers. Sera sealed the trace. The repair bodies did not recoil. The reef did not flare. The contact left a faint silver curve along the wound edge and then disappeared into the carrier stream.

The sled remained silent except for suit breath and ice.

Brenn said quietly, "How does the board display that?"

Sera checked. "Repair contact preserved. No new harm flag. Relation unresolved. Again pending witness terms."

"No duration?"

"Raw data only."

He nodded. "Good."

Mira heard the word differently now. Not approval. Recognition of less wrong.

They stayed above the reef for another hour. Not scanning for proof. Observing limits. Sera tested instrument silence and confirmed the passive chain could remain open without semantic inference. Anika found the lower pressure arc still too coarse and marked it for redesign. Jae identified two tether behaviors that made drones drift toward old extraction geometry and cursed at the software until Sera promised to help remove the pathing bias. Lio discovered the new canister throat clogged under slow flow and said several things formal procedure would not preserve except in spirit.

Under the ice, Orison repair bodies moved with and without human work.

That mattered most.

Mira watched a pale cluster approach the wound edge, pause near the silver residue, and move away without touching it. She did not know whether the movement meant refusal, recovery, caution, or something her species had not yet earned a word for. She entered:

Observed Orison repair body approach and withdrawal near prior human medium contact. No interpretation assigned.

It felt almost radical.

Near the end of the field window, the sled received a packet from Cael Station.

Ren had sent it under priority archive tag.

The first protected departure group had cleared funding.

Twenty-one workers.

Not all who wanted to leave. Not enough. But real. Transport slots assigned. Dependent remittance shielded for ninety days. Medical bridges funded. Certification debts suspended under challenge. No silence clauses. No release of liability.

Sera read the notice and looked away too fast.

Anika did not.

"Who?" she asked.

Mira hesitated.

Anika's helmet turned. "I can know."

She could. Mira sent the list to her private display.

Anika read it. Said nothing for a long time.

Then: "Haleen is leaving."

Tavo's channel opened. He had clearly been listening from cargo deck. "She marked pylon watch transition."

"She changed?"

"She has a sister on Pall whose oxygen account got flagged. Leaving is the right call."

Anika looked down through the ice at the reef and then at the tool handle in her own gloved hand.

"It feels like desertion," she said.

Keeps-Water-Calm's pressure trace moved before any human could answer.

Nadi rendered carefully. "A hand leaving a knife is not deserting the wound."

Anika bowed her helmet until it nearly touched the control panel.

"Tell them I heard," she said.

Nadi listened below the ice.

"They say: no gratitude."

Anika gave a short laugh that sounded like it hurt. "Fine. No gratitude."

Mira entered the exchange in the field log with permission and failure fields. Worker departure as transition, not abandonment. Orison witness statement not endorsement. Emotional interpretation human-assigned. No closure.

Brenn watched her write.

"You are going to be asked to lead this," he said.

Mira did not pretend not to understand. "Treaty science custodianship."

"That is the phrase Thane is using."

"Thane likes phrases that sound funded."

"This one may be."

Mira looked at him sharply.

He continued, "The Protective Conversion Order requires ongoing nonextractive evidence protocol administration, witness term review, worker transition linkage, and reporting to Compact review. Cael Station cannot absorb it informally. Drex cannot manage it. The Orison will not own a human institutional office. That leaves someone foolish enough to understand why the office should be distrusted."

"That was almost flattering."

"It was meant to be alarming."

She looked beneath the ice.

The first time she came to Pelagos, she had believed patient observation could teach humans to be less dangerous. The week had not disproved that. It had made the sentence heavier. Observation alone did nothing. Patience could become delay weaponized by those who profited from waiting. Science could become extraction with cleaner gloves. Law could become a cup declaring the ocean absent. Protection could become vanity wearing care. Every tool that helped could harm by believing itself innocent.

Custodianship sounded like responsibility.

It also sounded like a trap with a better title.

"Keeps-Water-Calm," she said, "Brenn says I will be asked to lead the human custodianship office."

Sera muttered, "Subtle."

Mira ignored her. "Do you object?"

The pressure trace remained quiet.

Then Nadi rendered: "Bad question."

Mira almost smiled. "Yes. I heard it as I said it."

She tried again. "What pressure would my accepting create?"

The trace changed. Slow. Considered.

"Humans will make your listening into authority. They will ask us to answer you because you have been less wrong. They will call your care proof that the room is good. You may begin to enjoy being necessary."

Mira closed her eyes.

There were knives more precise than accusation.

"Yes," she said. "Those are real pressures."

"Name them in the office."

"If I accept."

"If you accept."

"Would refusal reduce them?"

Long pause.

"Some. It would create others."

Of course.

Brenn said softly, "Welcome to governance."

Sera said, "Do not make it worse."

Mira opened her eyes. "I will accept only if the office charter includes refusal rights over my authority, public pressure fields, worker seats, Orison witness withdrawal without penalty, archive independence, and a mandatory line in every report: custodianship is not recognition."

Brenn was already writing. "That may make it difficult to staff."

"Good."

"Difficult to fund."

"Also good."

"Difficult to use."

"Best of all."

Sera looked pleased. "Now it sounds like a document raised feral."

Anika said, "Add tool authority. If we find a handle that makes people too fast, the office can stop the tool."

Jae added, "And pathing bias."

Lio said, "And canister throats."

Tavo said over the channel, "And no worker seat held by management."

Mira looked at Brenn.

He sighed. "I am writing."

Below the ice, Keeps-Water-Calm moved through the brine channel. Their body passed under a clear place in the glass shelf, visible for one breath as a pale knot of living blue-gray, attendants absent, self not simple, relation not reducible. Mira put her gloved palm against the ice.

Not ceremony.

Balance, perhaps.

The ice was cold enough that her suit warned her after two seconds.

She withdrew.

"Field visit concluding," Sera said. Her voice was gentler than the words. "Passive chain closing. No semantic inference. Raw telemetry sealed. Public board update pending witness review."

"Return route clear," Jae said.

"Medium secured," Lio said.

"Pressure controls marked for redesign," Anika said.

Mira looked one last time at the reef beneath the glass.

The wound was still there.

That mattered. The book of human institutions would try to turn the order into precedent, the precedent into process, the process into a success story, and the success story into a thing that could be exported without the ugliness that made it honest. Someone would write "Pelagos model" in a report and forget the smell of overheated pump gel, the worker who asked whether the scan could hear hungry children, the Orison silence that refused to become absence, the board that told them to stop making gratitude. Someone would try to make the reef useful.

Her job, if she accepted it, would include refusing that usefulness.

They turned the sled back toward Cael Station.

Behind them, under the ice, the repair bodies continued without watching them leave.

That was good.

Not gratitude. Not dismissal.

A relation that did not need human attention to remain real.

At the station, the continuing obligation board had updated before they arrived.

FIELD VISIT: CONTACT PRESERVED. NO NEW HARM FLAG. REPAIR UNFINISHED.

WORKER TRANSITION: FIRST PROTECTED DEPARTURE GROUP FUNDED.

CLAIM STATUS: EXTRACTION AUTHORIZATION WITHDRAWN; OBLIGATIONS ACTIVE.

WHAT HAS NOT YET BEEN LEARNED:

HOW TO BUILD AN OFFICE THAT CAN BE DISOBEYED.

Ren stood beneath the line with a cup of coffee and the look of someone who had absolutely written it herself.

Mira read it twice.

Then she went to Thane's office.

The station administrator had three screens open, two legal observers waiting, and the expression of a woman who had discovered that history generated paperwork faster than crisis generated heat.

"If this is about the custodianship office," Thane said, "I have not slept enough for dignity."

"It is."

"Good. Sit down."

"I have conditions."

"Everyone has conditions. The Orison have terms. The workers have seats. Brenn has failure fields. Pell has liability language. Sera has threatened to make the software ugly on purpose. Tavo wants management barred from pretending to be labor. Drex has offered implementation funds he may no longer control. Public Continuity wants a name I will never approve. What do you have?"

Mira sat.

"A refusal charter."

Thane stopped typing.

Mira laid it out. The office would not own the protocol. It would maintain records of where the protocol failed. It would not certify relation, only document restraint and unresolved obligations. Every report would carry pressure fields. Orison witnesses could withdraw without procedural penalty. Worker transition representatives would have veto authority over labor impact language. Archive independence would be protected. Instrument designers could be overruled by repair teams if controls made hands too fast. No success metrics based on closure, gratitude, or claim conversion speed. No use of Pelagos model outside Pelagos without a local witness body empowered to refuse the model.

Thane listened without interrupting.

When Mira finished, Thane said, "That is almost impossible to administer."

"Yes."

"Funders will hate it."

"Yes."

"Compact reviewers will try to standardize it."

"Yes."

"You still accept leadership?"

"Custodianship," Mira said. "Not leadership. And only interim, subject to review by people who can tell me no."

Thane leaned back. "You have become very annoying."

"Pelagos helped."

For the first time since Mira's return, Thane smiled.

"Good," she said. "Put it in writing."

Mira did.

The interim charter went to Brenn, Pell, Nadi, Tavo, Sera, Ren, Ward, and Keeps-Water-Calm's channel before station midnight. Drex received an implementation copy, not an approval request. The creditor committee received notice after the fact. Public Continuity received nothing because Thane said she had developed a medical sensitivity to their fonts.

Responses came in fragments.

Brenn: The phrase office that can be disobeyed is not formally usable, but the concept is retained.

Sera: Make the software uglier than the prose.

Tavo: Worker seats plural or I make noise.

Pell: Liability section needs teeth. I have teeth.

Ren: Archive refusal protected. Good. Needs deadman mirror.

Ward: Funding difficult. Pursuing.

Drex: Implementation funds released from remaining discretionary account. No statement.

Keeps-Water-Calm did not respond for three hours.

When the response came, it arrived as a pressure trace through the brine channel beneath the obligation board. Nadi rendered it under terms:

"The room may be entered if it remembers it is not the water."

Mira accepted that as provisional permission to begin work and not one thing more.

The first protected departure left before station dawn.

Cael Station had no departure lounge. Lounges belonged to ports that expected movement to be commercial, repeatable, and clean enough for advertisements. Pelagos had a transfer lock, a baggage scale, a pressure vestibule, and a strip of wall where someone had once painted arrows toward emergency suits and then, years later, taped a child's drawing over one of them because the arrow pointed at a sealed door. The departing workers gathered there with bags that looked too small for the lives they were supposed to carry away.

Haleen Mor stood at the front of the group with her pylon-watch gloves clipped to the outside of her duffel.

Anika stood three meters away and pretended to study the shuttle collar.

Tavo arrived late, which meant he had argued with medical, cargo control, and his own pride before appearing. He wore a brace under his coverall and walked like every step had paperwork attached. He carried no speech. That was wise. The transfer lock was already too full of things people could not say without turning them into traps.

Mira came because she had signed the field log that made the first departure possible. Sera came because she said someone had to verify the shuttle water packs were not "budget-criminal," and then checked them three times. Pell came with release packets and a face that dared anyone to call them releases. Ren came barefoot again until Tavo told her the deck was cold and she silently accepted a pair of station socks from Lio. Drex did not come. He sent payroll keys, transport confirmations, and no statement.

Haleen noticed his absence.

"Good," she said.

No one asked whether she meant good because he had learned not to center himself or good because she could leave without looking at him.

The shuttle crew called five minutes to seal.

People began doing the strange choreography of departures under pressure: checking straps already checked, handing over tiny items that should have been transferred hours earlier, promising to send messages through systems that might fail, making jokes too small to survive memory. Jae gave Haleen a tool charm made from a broken tether clip. Lio gave her a ration pack she immediately handed back because she knew he had stolen it from medical. Ren gave one departing hydro tech a copy of her own testimony request because the woman had not yet decided whether leaving meant losing the right to speak.

"You do not lose it," Ren said. "Leaving is not silence."

The woman nodded and put the file in her bag.

Anika had still not moved.

Haleen finally crossed the three meters between them.

"You are going to stare a hole in that collar," she said.

"It deserves one."

"Most things do."

They stood facing each other in the corridor noise. Haleen was older by fifteen years, broader in the shoulders, with the flat calm of someone who had survived too many platforms to trust any single crisis as the defining one. Anika had silver paint on her cheek again, fresh this time, a line drawn too straight to be accidental.

"You changed your mark," Anika said.

"Yes."

"You marked pylon watch."

"I know what I marked."

"Then you changed."

Haleen looked at her gloves clipped to the duffel. "My sister's boy had his oxygen account flagged. You heard."

"I heard."

"Then do not ask me to make it prettier."

Anika's jaw worked. "I was not."

"You were."

That struck. Anika looked away first.

Haleen softened by one degree, which on her looked like a structural failure. "Listen to me. Staying is not braver because it stays near the wound. Leaving is not cleaner because it gets away from Drex. Testifying is not purer because it hurts management. Every option is tied to a debt someone else helped build. We choose the knot we can pull without lying about the rest."

Anika said nothing.

Haleen unclipped one pylon-watch glove and held it out.

Anika stared. "No."

"Take it."

"You are leaving."

"Yes. The glove is not. It knows the pylon's bad edges. You said you wanted tool redesign."

"That is superstition."

"No. It is wear pattern."

Sera, from the water-pack station, called, "Wear pattern is admissible."

Pell said, "In limited contexts."

Haleen ignored them both.

Anika took the glove.

It was a small thing and therefore almost unbearable.

The brine channel beside the transfer lock shifted. Everyone noticed now when water changed its mind. Keeps-Water-Calm was not present, but a thin blue density moved through the channel, then cleared. No translator. No board. No words.

One of the departing workers whispered, "Is that goodbye?"

Nadi, who had arrived silently at the edge of the group, said, "Do not make it small."

The worker flushed. "Sorry."

"No. You reached for the nearest human shape. Put it down gently."

The shuttle crew called seal.

Haleen shouldered her duffel. Tavo stepped forward then stopped, as if he had reached the edge of his authority and found it marked.

"Chief," Haleen said.

"Mor."

"Do not let them turn pylon watch into a volunteer honor post. It is work. Pay it."

"I will."

"Do not say I served."

"I will not."

"Do not forget the gloves are not calibrated for the new contact protocol."

"Anika has one."

Haleen nodded. "Good."

Then she left.

Twenty-one workers passed through the lock. No applause. No blessing. No thank-you ceremony. The shuttle sealed, detached, and moved upward through the melt-bore corridor carrying people who had not been rescued so much as temporarily pried loose from one set of teeth.

Anika stood with the glove in both hands until the shuttle light disappeared.

Mira entered a note in the transition log:

Protected departure group one launched. No silence clauses. No release of liability. Witness rights preserved. Departure not recorded as gratitude, abandonment, or closure.

She hesitated, then added:

Observed transfer of pylon glove from departing worker to repair transition worker as tool-history continuity. Interpretation limited.

Anika looked at her. "Interpretation limited?"

"It means I am not stealing your moment for the record."

The welder looked back toward the empty lock.

"Good," she said, and walked away with the glove.

The second practical test came less than an hour later, when Drex Maritime tried to replace Olan Drex with a compliance administrator named Vey Marr.

Marr arrived by packet, not body, which was already a bad sign. People who intended to repair harm usually wanted to know what the air smelled like. The packet opened in Breakwater Court with Harrow Meridian copied, Aster Vale copied, Pelagic Infrastructure copied, Compact Claims copied, and labor counsel not copied until Pell forwarded it with a subject line that read:

TRY AGAIN WITH FEWER KNIVES.

Marr's face appeared above a credential block so polished it felt lubricated. They wore Meridian corporate gray and the expression of someone who had practiced empathy in a mirror with bullet points.

"Drex Maritime remains committed to full compliance," Marr began.

Pell muted the packet.

Brenn looked at her.

"I am saving the room," she said.

"Unmute, but preserve that explanation."

She did.

Marr continued, now with the slight delay of someone informed they had already irritated counsel. The board had suspended Olan Drex from discretionary authority and appointed Marr as interim compliance administrator for all nontechnical implementation tasks. Olan would remain available for factual inquiries. Marr would coordinate with creditors to ensure "efficient resolution of environmental, labor, and communications concerns." Marr proposed an implementation steering call in which worker representatives could provide "input," Orison channels could provide "cultural context," and Cael Station could provide "technical reporting."

By the time the packet reached the phrase efficient resolution, Tavo had joined from the cargo deck.

"No," he said.

Marr smiled with three seconds of delay. "Crew Chief Rusk, I appreciate that transitions can create anxiety."

"No," Tavo repeated, slower, as if speaking to a machine that had failed diagnostics.

Pell said, "The Protective Conversion Order prohibits management substitution that impairs worker transition, evidence preservation, or repair duties."

"Drex Maritime is not impairing," Marr said. "We are professionalizing."

Sera, who had been dragged into the room because "technical reporting" had offended her personally, muttered, "That word should require a license."

Brenn said, "Administrator Marr, do you accept the order's failure fields, refusal charter, worker seats, and prohibition on gratitude, closure, and endorsement language?"

Marr's smile held. "We accept the spirit of the order."

Everyone in the room reacted.

Not loudly. Worse.

Mira saw Tavo lean back. Pell's eyes went flat. Sera put both hands on her console as if restraining it from violence. Ren, who had installed herself near the archive mirror proposal, opened a new file titled SPIRIT_OF_ORDER_RISK.

Brenn removed his spectacles.

"No," he said.

Marr blinked. "Adjudicator?"

"You do not accept the spirit. You accept the text, the failure fields, and the duties, or you do not accept implementation authority."

"The board must retain ordinary management discretion."

"Ordinary management discretion is part of the pressure structure under review."

"Surely the court does not intend to paralyze lawful corporate governance."

Brenn looked tired enough to become dangerous. "I intend to prevent the same governance from renaming itself compliance and walking back into the room through a polished packet."

Pell smiled faintly.

Marr adjusted. "Then Drex Maritime requests clarification of what functions Field Director Drex may still perform."

That question had teeth. If Olan did too much, the old power remained. If he did too little, the worker transition keys and internal records could vanish behind board privilege. If Marr took over, creditors would put gloves on the knife and call it administration.

Mira looked at Drex's small side window. He had joined but not spoken. His face looked worse than before: hollow, unshaven, lit by command deck emergency blue. Behind him the platform moved without waiting for his humiliation to finish.

Brenn said, "Field Director Drex remains compelled implementation respondent for existing orders only: bond release documentation, transition payroll keys, evidence custody, board communications preservation, and technical handover subject to worker and station witness. He has no authority to restart extraction, narrow repair terms, negotiate witness language, or supervise repair transition workers."

Marr said, "That leaves no efficient managerial center."

Tavo said, "Correct."

Marr ignored him. "Who coordinates?"

Thane answered from Cael Station administration. "Interim custodianship office once chartered. Until then, implementation triad: station authority, worker transition seat, and adjudicator docket. Orison witness terms where relevant. Corporate representatives provide required records and funding, not direction."

Marr's expression flickered. "That is unworkable."

Ren said, "Document that they said unworkable when denied control."

Pell typed. "Already."

Drex finally spoke.

"Vey."

Marr turned toward his window. "Olan, the board appreciates your cooperation under difficult circumstances."

"Do not."

The word was quiet and did more than a shout would have.

Drex leaned toward his pickup. "I am sending the remaining implementation keys to the triad escrow now. Payroll, transition ledger, pylon maintenance history, creditor correspondence, and Hale advisory attachments. If the board wants to remove me afterward, it can explain why it waited until after compliance became possible."

Marr's smile vanished. "You do not have authority to transfer privileged board correspondence."

"Then sue me after the workers are paid."

Pell covered her mouth with one hand.

Mira was not sure whether she was hiding a smile or legal terror.

Data began arriving in the escrow. Sera's console pinged. Ren's archive mirror pinged. Pell's legal custody channel pinged. Tavo's worker seat received payroll confirmation and stared at it as if it might bite.

Brenn entered an emergency clarification:

Corporate implementation authority is limited to compliance delivery and may not displace worker transition governance, station safety authority, evidence custody, or witness terms. Attempts to substitute managerial control for ordered obligations are recorded as pressure events.

Marr objected.

The objection entered the record.

For the first time all morning, Mira felt the ugly room hold.

Drex's window remained open after Marr's packet closed.

No one spoke to him at first.

Then Tavo said, "You sent the keys."

"Yes."

"All of them?"

"All the ones I know about."

"That is not comforting."

"No."

Tavo looked down at something offscreen. Probably the payroll confirmation. Probably names. Always names.

"Haleen's account cleared," he said.

Drex closed his eyes.

"Good," he said.

Anika, who had come in halfway through and still carried Haleen's glove tucked under one arm, said, "No gratitude."

Drex opened his eyes. "No gratitude."

The words did not absolve him.

They did keep him from stealing the moment.

The third test belonged to Sera and Ren.

The archive deadman mirror began as an argument over a cable route and became, by late afternoon, the first infrastructure the custodianship office did not yet have permission to build. Ren wanted an independent mirror of all raw telemetry, pressure fields, order revisions, transition marks, and witness withdrawal events sent off-station in delayed encrypted packets to three repositories: Cael Station archive, Compact public evidence escrow, and a neutral Meridian academic node whose board had once sued two corporations and one ministry for data tampering. Sera objected because the mirror would duplicate sensitive Orison-adjacent telemetry before witness terms had fully bounded every data type.

Ren said, "If we do not mirror, the first appeal will bury the raw record under privilege."

Sera said, "If we mirror everything, we export relation traces like souvenirs."

Ren said, "Then filter."

Sera said, "Filtering is where theft learns manners."

They argued in the acoustic lab because both refused to let anyone call it merely technical. Mira listened for twenty minutes before realizing they did not need arbitration. They needed to hear each other's fear in its own discipline.

"Say the bad outcome," she told them.

Sera turned. "What?"

"Each of you. Say the bad outcome you are preventing."

Ren crossed her arms. "Fine. Bad outcome: creditor appeal says the board events were unauthorized, repair traces inconclusive, worker pressure anecdotal, and Orison witness terms procedurally irregular. Raw records remain in systems controlled by parties with incentive to narrow them. Five years later, the case becomes a footnote about evidentiary complexity and every future reef gets cut while courts request cleaner data."

Sera did not interrupt.

Mira looked at her.

Sera's jaw worked. "Bad outcome: in the name of preservation, we create a portable dataset that lets outside labs model relation persistence without Pelagos, without Orison terms, without worker context, without knowing which silences were refusals. They build simulations. They call it the Pelagos signal. Someone tries to reproduce it. Someone tries to sell nonextractive proof as a service. The reef becomes useful at interstellar scale."

Ren's face changed.

"Oh," she said.

"Yes," Sera said. "Oh."

They built the mirror differently after that.

Not cleanly. Differently.

Raw telemetry stayed local under witness term lock unless release was authorized by the triad and no Orison withdrawal field was active. External mirrors received cryptographic proofs that records existed and had not been altered, plus human legal documents, worker transition marks with consent, creditor pressure events, and instrument metadata stripped of relation-reconstructable signal. Any request for full data would have to enter pressure fields publicly. The system would prove that something had been preserved without handing the preserved thing to every hungry institution.

Ren hated the incompleteness.

Sera hated the trust it still required.

They both signed.

Ren named it the Pelagos Custodial Mirror.

Sera renamed the internal module UGLY_MIRROR_DO_NOT_POLISH.

Brenn saw the name and put his face in his hands.

Mira left it.

The first repair-transition shift began without waiting for the office charter.

That was Tavo's fault, Anika's fault, Sera's fault, and technically a violation of three scheduling assumptions no one had yet had time to write into a procedure. Mira found out because the continuing obligation board changed from PLANNING to ACTIVE while she was in Thane's office signing the refusal charter.

She read the line.

"They started."

Thane did not look up. "Of course they did."

"You are very calm about that."

"I have managed a research station under ice for seventeen years. Calm is what panic looks like after it learns payroll."

Mira went to the repair observation bay.

The lower channel was already lit. Not in celebration. Work light. Honest light. The kind that made every scratch on a tool visible. Anika stood at the pressure board with Haleen's glove clipped to her belt, not worn. Jae managed drone trace from a side station. Lio handled the modified canister throat, neck wrap visible above his suit ring. Two workers who had marked protected departure interest stood in observer positions because leaving in three days did not erase what their hands knew today. Low-Current-Under-Stone waited beyond the membrane with two Orison repair bodies, one of whom had startled in the first repair cycle and had chosen to return.

That choice sat in the room like a live wire.

"Who authorized active shift?" Mira asked over the channel.

Anika did not turn. "No extraction. No new procedure. Tool familiarity pass."

"That is not an answer."

Tavo's voice came from the cargo deck. "I authorized."

"Under what authority?"

"Bad habit."

Mira closed her eyes.

Sera, already in the observation bay because of course she was, said, "Before you become institutional, look at the setup."

Mira looked.

The pressure controls were not connected to repair medium flow. The canister was loaded with inert practice gel, dyed green and chemically blocked from entering the carrier stream. The drones were tethered to a dead zone above the wound, recording hand timing without touching the relation. Low-Current-Under-Stone had positioned the Orison repair bodies outside contact range. The public board listed the pass as:

WORKER TRANSITION TRAINING: NO REPAIR CLAIM. NO CONTACT. TOOL MEMORY SESSION.

Under pressure fields, Sera had entered:

Human urgency: workers need to learn before knowledge disperses through departure.

Orison tolerance: limited presence, no contact, withdrawal active.

Authority gap: custodianship charter not yet ratified.

Mira read the last line twice.

"You put authority gap on the public board."

Sera looked defensive. "It existed."

"Good."

"Do not say good like that. It makes me nervous."

Mira opened the live log. "Then the first custodianship act is recording that the office was bypassed before it existed."

Tavo said, "That sounds bad."

"It is accurate."

"Bad and accurate."

"Yes."

Anika finally looked up from the pressure board. "Should we stop?"

The question was the first proof that stopping had become thinkable.

Mira let herself feel the significance before answering. "No. Continue under recorded authority gap. No contact. If Low-Current withdraws, stop without debate."

Low-Current-Under-Stone pulsed through the translator.

"Debate may happen after stopping."

Anika said, "Fair."

The training pass began.

It would have bored anyone looking for drama. That was one of its virtues. Anika moved the pressure arc through tiny increments while Jae tracked the difference between intended motion and actual hand drift. Lio opened and closed the inert gel flow at delayed intervals. Sera mapped timing errors without turning them into grades. Low-Current-Under-Stone and the Orison repair bodies remained beyond contact range, not supervising, not endorsing, simply present enough to refuse if the room became too large.

The first error came from Lio.

He opened inert flow early, barely. The practice gel struck the catch screen and spread in a green smear.

"Abort," Jae said.

Lio cut flow.

No one groaned.

That, too, had to be learned.

Anika said, "Cause."

Lio flexed his gloved hand. "I anticipated your pressure drop."

"Why?"

"Because last cycle you were late."

Anika's shoulders tightened. "So you corrected me without telling me."

"Yes."

"Do not do that."

He nodded. "Cause logged."

Sera entered:

ERROR: FLOW ANTICIPATED PRESSURE BASED ON PRIOR HUMAN DELAY. TEAM MEMORY BECAME UNSPOKEN OVERRIDE.

She stared at the sentence. "I hate that this applies to more than tools."

Mira said, "Leave it."

The second pass failed because Anika hesitated after Low-Current-Under-Stone shifted color.

"I thought they were withdrawing," she said.

Nadi rendered from the wet side. "They were reducing presence, not refusing."

"Those look similar."

"Yes."

"Can we mark the difference?"

Low-Current pulsed.

The translator said, "Sometimes."

Anika swore under her breath. "Fine. Sometimes gets a column."

Sera added a column.

Brenn arrived midway through the third pass and said nothing for almost seven minutes, a restraint so visible it deserved its own entry. He watched the workers fail without punishment, the Orison witnesses remain without endorsement, the board expose the authority gap, and Mira log the fact that the session should have been requested before it began.

At last he said, "This is what implementation will look like."

Mira did not ask whether he meant ugly or useful.

"Yes."

"Courts prefer orders."

"Workers prefer hands."

"And Orison?"

Keeps-Water-Calm had entered the side brine channel without announcement. The translator remained off. Nadi watched their color and said, "They prefer not being turned into the answer to your contrast."

Brenn accepted the hit. "Received."

The fourth pass did not fail.

It also did not succeed. The inert gel crossed the practice gap, touched the catch screen, and held as a clean narrow line through three human hand adjustments and two Orison presence shifts. No carrier wound. No repair claim. No contact. But the timing changed. Human hands stopped arriving first by habit. Orison bodies stopped needing to flare warning before the room noticed its own size.

Anika did not smile.

She said, "Again."

Lio said, "Again."

Jae said, "Log drift before again."

That was how Mira knew they were learning.

At the end of the session, Low-Current-Under-Stone approached the membrane. The repair body that had startled in the first cycle moved with them, staying slightly behind and lower in the current.

Anika saw and went still.

"Are they..." She stopped.

Nadi said, "Ask better."

Anika swallowed. "Pressure entered: I want to know whether my being here increases harm for the startled repair body."

The translator clicked. Low-Current touched the membrane. The smaller body shifted, sending a faint blue-white pulse through the water.

"Your presence is large," the translator said. "It is not today too large."

Anika bowed her helmet.

"Received."

No gratitude.

No apology, either, not yet. Apology would have asked the repair body to do something with her guilt. Received was smaller. Better.

Mira entered the exchange in the custodianship log:

First active worker-transition training occurred before charter ratification. Authority gap disclosed. No contact with unnamed relation. No new harm flag. Orison withdrawal right remained active. Human error logged without punishment. Training to continue only under ratified charter or explicit provisional authorization.

Tavo read it from the cargo deck and said, "You make us sound undisciplined."

"You were."

"Fair."

"You also made stopping thinkable."

He was quiet a long moment.

"Put that in too."

She did:

Observed positive procedural change: workers asked whether to stop before being ordered.

Sera leaned over her shoulder. "That is dry enough to be heartbreaking."

"Good."

"Your prose is deteriorating into governance."

"Yours is deteriorating into conscience."

Sera recoiled. "Take that back."

Mira almost laughed.

The obligation board updated:

WORKER TRANSITION TRAINING: AUTHORITY GAP DISCLOSED. NO CONTACT. STOPPING PRACTICED.

WHAT HAS NOT YET BEEN LEARNED:

HOW TO MAKE PERMISSION SLOWER THAN HARM WITHOUT MAKING HELP TOO LATE.

No one knew who had written the last line.

For once, the investigation could wait.

The interim charter went public at station dusk.

Thane insisted on calling it publication. Sera called it "letting the document get yelled at in daylight." Brenn called it provisional posting. Pell called it discoverable. Tavo called it "finally putting the rules where people can kick them." Keeps-Water-Calm did not translate a response, which the charter recorded as neither approval nor objection.

They posted it in three places: the continuing obligation board, Drex Platform's cargo deck, and the local packet channel where creditors, committees, station crew, workers, Orison witnesses, and every opportunist with a stamp could see it fail in public.

It failed immediately.

The first objection came from a station hydroponics supervisor named Pellam Jui, who had not been part of any hearing and therefore spoke with the clean irritation of someone whose life had been disrupted by other people's revelations.

"This charter gives worker transition seats veto authority over labor impact language," Jui said in the public comment channel. "What about station labor? My people are now maintaining extra water controls, pressure monitors, and brine filters because the repair channel cannot be treated like ordinary infrastructure. We are not Drex workers. We are still being turned into support for the wound."

Mira read the objection twice.

It was correct.

That was inconvenient and therefore useful.

Thane looked at her. "Custodian?"

"Interim," Mira said.

"Interim custodian."

"Record the objection as valid pressure. Add station labor seat for infrastructure load."

Pellam's reply came back fast. "Not one seat. Rotating seat. Hydroponics, maintenance, safety, medical. The burden moves."

Sera said, "I hate when people are better at this than the first draft."

Mira amended the charter:

Station labor rotating seat added for infrastructure burdens created by repair, evidence preservation, witness terms, water isolation, and public obligation systems. Burden movement must be logged; no department may be treated as background support without pressure entry.

Brenn approved the provisional amendment.

Pell wrote, "This will multiply meetings."

Tavo answered, "So did the harm."

The second objection came from an Orison liaison intern, human-born, twenty years old, and visibly terrified on the packet video.

"The charter says Orison witnesses may withdraw without penalty," he said. "But human liaison staff are evaluated on maintaining communication. If a witness withdraws during my shift, my performance metrics mark unresolved contact. I can support withdrawal and still be punished by my own training program."

Nadi closed her eyes.

Mira felt old anger rise. Not dramatic anger. Administrative anger. The kind that had learned to read harm in checkboxes.

Thane said, "That metric is not from my office."

"Whose?"

"Compact liaison training."

Brenn rubbed his forehead. "Of course."

The charter gained another amendment:

No human liaison staff may be penalized, downgraded, or marked deficient because an Orison witness exercises withdrawal, refusal, silence, nonanswer, or partial translation terms. Evaluation must distinguish support for witness boundary from failure of communication.

Keeps-Water-Calm's channel remained silent.

Nadi said, "The silence is active."

Brenn said, "Do I enter that?"

"Enter that I said it, not that they did."

He did.

The third objection came from Drex Platform in a cluster rather than a sentence.

Workers were reading the charter on the cargo deck and marking places where it assumed literacy, patience, and trust they did not have. Anika objected to "instrument governance" because it sounded like tools got meetings and hands got blamed. Jae objected to "team-based applications" because the phrase did not say who could not be separated without consent. Lio objected to "medical clearance" because medical had once cleared him for a shift with a rash that later put him in isolation. Haleen, already in transit, sent a delayed objection to "protected departure" because protection lasting ninety days sounded like a timer on a bomb.

Tavo sent the cluster without smoothing it.

Pell read through the comments and said, "This is a massacre."

Mira said, "Good."

"You are becoming predictable."

"Still good."

The charter changed again:

Tool governance became hand-tool relation review.

Team-based applications became crew continuity applications, with explicit individual opt-out and no forced retention.

Medical clearance gained worker appeal rights and independent review for any clearance tied to transition eligibility.

Protected departure became initial protected departure, with renewal trigger thirty days before expiration and public pressure entry if funding was not secured.

Lio sent back: "Less bad."

Anika sent: "Still ugly."

Jae sent: "Workable if no one lies."

Haleen sent, after a longer delay: "Thirty-day trigger or I come back angry."

Tavo replied publicly: "Accepted."

Mira watched the exchange and realized that the charter was becoming less like a foundation and more like a tool handle shaped by use. That was better. Foundations hid their pressure underground. Handles showed wear.

The fourth objection was from Public Continuity.

They objected to almost everything.

They objected to refusal language as "potentially alienating." They objected to no-closure clauses as "emotionally difficult for public audiences." They objected to the absence of a commemorative name. They objected to the phrase no gratitude duties because it might "inhibit positive interspecies relations." They objected to the ugly mirror's name, which made Sera so happy she had to leave the room for water.

Thane read the objections in a flat voice.

Brenn said, "Denied."

Pell said, "You should give reasons."

"No."

Mira said, "Reasons matter."

Brenn sighed and entered:

Public discomfort with unresolved obligation is not a sufficient basis to rename, soften, close, commemorate, aestheticize, or gratitude-frame the charter. Positive relation cannot be required as an output of harm response.

Sera returned with water. "I missed something good."

"Brenn used aestheticize," Mira said.

"He is deteriorating beautifully."

The fifth objection came from the creditor committee and was the only one everyone expected.
