Harrow argued the charter created an unbounded cost center. Aster argued refusal rights impaired asset certainty. Pelagic argued labor transition without productivity metrics would create moral hazard. Leontes Recovery Trust, which had been quiet enough that Mira disliked it most, argued that preserving unresolved obligation as an operational category would undermine all future financing in contested nonhuman environments.

Mira looked at that last sentence a long time.

"They understand it," she said.

Pell nodded. "Yes. That is why they hate it correctly."

Brenn entered the creditor objections as pressure events.

Then he added:

Financing structures that require erasure of unresolved nonhuman relation, worker coercion, or premature closure to remain viable are not protected interests under this provisional order.

Pell stared at the line.

"That will be appealed before the ink dries."

"There is no ink."

"Do not become Sera."

He looked offended.

The charter stayed public.

Not final. Not stable. Not clean. By station midnight it had already accumulated twelve amendments, nineteen pressure notes, six refusal fields, four deferred terms, two unresolved Orison witness questions, and one line in the margin from Ren:

DOCUMENT SURVIVES FIRST CONTACT WITH PEOPLE WHO HAVE TO LIVE UNDER IT.

Mira read that line when she finally thought the day's work had stopped.

It had not.

A private packet waited from Drex.

No subject.

Inside was a single implementation record: he had transferred control of the discretionary Meridian account he had once used for quiet platform repairs into the transition escrow. Not enough to solve the funding problem. Enough to extend four medical bridges, including Lio's if complications returned, and six dependent oxygen accounts for workers in protected departure group two.

There was no note.

Mira forwarded the record to Pell, Ward, Tavo, and the worker transition seat.

She did not answer Drex.

That felt right.

Ten minutes later, Tavo sent one line to the public transition board:

FUNDS RECEIVED. SOURCE RECORDED. NO PRAISE ENTERED.

Anika added:

SPEND IT BEFORE THEY CLAW.

Pell added:

ALREADY MOVING.

For the first time all day, the station channel produced something close to collective breathing.

Not relief.

Relief was too smooth.

But the sense that a thing had moved before it froze.

The money moved ugly, which was how everyone trusted it.

Ward's office tried to label the account emergency humanitarian bridge. Pell objected because humanitarian made workers sound like a disaster zone instead of people whose contracts had been built into the claim. Tavo objected because bridge sounded like something temporary enough to collapse politely. Anika objected because none of the words said oxygen. Lio objected because, from medical, he had discovered that objecting while horizontal made administrators underestimate him.

The label became:

WORKER DEPENDENCY RELEASE - INITIAL OXYGEN, MEDICAL, HOUSING, AND CERTIFICATION SHIELD.

It was too long for the finance display.

Sera fixed the display by making the font smaller.

Ward protested readability.

Sera said, "Then stop making harm so verbose."

By the time the first six dependent oxygen accounts cleared, the transfer lock had filled again, not with departing workers but with people watching numbers become air somewhere else. No ceremony formed. The board showed account codes, not names, because the families had not consented to becoming moral exhibits. Each cleared line changed from amber to blue-white. Not the blue of Orison contact. Not the blue of platform hold. A finance blue, ordinary and ugly and better than amber.

Anika stood with Haleen's glove tucked into her belt.

"That one is her sister's boy?" she asked Tavo.

Tavo checked his private slate. "Yes."

The line turned blue-white.

Anika looked away.

Tavo said, "No gratitude."

"I know."

"You can still be glad."

She glared at him as if he had made an indecent technical suggestion.

He shrugged. "Different column."

That, too, went onto the board after Ren heard it.

EMOTIONAL STATE NOTE: GLADNESS IS NOT GRATITUDE.

Brenn saw the update from Breakwater Court and filed no objection. That restraint was becoming almost flamboyant.

Mira watched from the corridor edge and felt something in the station alter by a fraction. Not repair. Not trust. A fraction. A child three systems away had oxygen because a claim had been made less clean. A worker who might never return to Pelagos had not been turned into a symbol to make the rest of them feel brave. A bad account label had been fought until it said enough of the truth to move money before freezing.

This was not the kind of thing histories liked.

Histories preferred orders, discoveries, first contacts, named precedents, speeches from people in rooms with flags. They rarely knew what to do with font size, account labels, tool gloves, medical bridges, and the moment a supervisor told a worker she could be glad without owing thanks.

Mira made a note for the custodianship office:

Preserve small mechanisms. Major orders fail without them. Do not summarize them into outcome.

Ren, reading over her shoulder because archivists appeared wherever notes were born, said, "That is almost an archive principle."

"Almost?"

"Needs teeth."

Mira added:

Any report omitting the small mechanisms by which obligation became material must be marked incomplete.

Ren nodded. "Better."

In the brine channel, a faint pressure pulse moved under the lock, then faded.

No one translated it.

No one needed to.

Later, Sera found Mira still standing there and handed her a sealed cup of station coffee.

"Do not thank me," Sera said.

"I was going to assess whether this qualifies as coffee."

"Also do not do that."

They stood shoulder to shoulder, watching the last blue-white finance line settle. Sera had grease on one sleeve, brine salt dried at her collar, and the haunted alertness of someone whose instruments had begun teaching her manners.

"I used to think good data would make bad arguments lose," Sera said.

Mira held the cup in both hands. "It can."

"That is not what I said."

"No."

Sera looked toward the transfer lock. "Now I think good data needs shelter, witnesses, ugly labels, paid workers, hostile lawyers, and someone willing to make the font smaller."

"That is a publishable finding."

"I will deny it."

Mira sipped the coffee. It was bad, warm, and real.

"When Book Three begins," Sera said, not knowing she was using a phrase that belonged to no one in the room, "someone will try to make the Pelagos protocol sound like a triumph."

"Yes."

"What do we do?"

"We keep the ugly parts attached."

"And if they cut them off?"

Mira looked through the corridor glass. Somewhere beyond wall, water, ice, and pressure, the unnamed relation continued without waiting for human strategy.

"Then we put them back in the record."

Sera nodded. "Again tomorrow."

"Again tomorrow."

Sera finished her coffee and grimaced. "This is terrible."

"Yes."

"No prohibited inference warning?"

"Low measured coffee quality does not establish absence of caffeine, intention, hospitality, or harm."

Sera stared at her, then laughed so suddenly that two workers near the lock turned to look. The laugh did not last. It did not need to. For three seconds, the corridor held something that was not relief, not closure, not gratitude, and not denial. It was only a human body remembering it could make sound without asking law what category the sound belonged to.

Mira entered none of that in the record.

Some things deserved to remain unuseful.

Then the transfer lock display chimed one last time.

Not an alarm. Not a claim. A mundane routing notice: the shuttle carrying Haleen's group had cleared the outer relay and entered the courier lane. No ceremony attached to it. No one on the station channel announced it. The line appeared, waited for acknowledgment, and would have disappeared if Ren had not quietly pinned it under worker transition for one hour.

Sera read it and said nothing.

Mira read it and thought of a boy on Pall whose oxygen account had turned blue-white while he was probably asleep, unaware that a reef under another moon had entered the accounting of his lungs.

The universe did not become just because one account cleared.

But injustice had lost one small mechanism.

That counted, if counting did not become a cage.

Mira closed the charter file after copying every amendment into the archive mirror. She had imagined, once, that governance began when a document was approved. Pelagos corrected her. Governance began when the first person harmed by the document could change a sentence and have the change remain visible.

Late that night, after the station dimmed and Drex Platform's blue-hold lights softened to maintenance glow, Mira returned alone to the observation blister.

She was not alone, of course. No one on Pelagos was alone in the human sense. Water moved through exchange channels. The reef persisted below. Workers argued in cargo decks. Orison witnesses refused and answered and refused again. Creditors sharpened appeals. Public boards carried obligations. Instruments listened, sometimes too much. The station breathed because hundreds of systems did their jobs imperfectly and people kept noticing when they failed.

But she was the only human in the blister.

That was close enough.

Below, through layered water and ice and glass, the reef showed as pale branching density. Not a city. Not a grave. Not an archive. Not an organ. Not a monument. Not property. Not proof of human goodness. Not a lesson waiting to improve them.

Beneath the reef of glass, relation continued.

Mira thought of the title the catalog had given this book before any of them knew what it would cost. Beneath the Reef of Glass. Humans had meant below the visible structure, below the ice, below the thing that looked fragile from orbit. They had been wrong even in the metaphor. The reef was not the surface. Glass was not the barrier. Beneath was not only a place.

Beneath was where the categories failed and the obligations remained.

Her slate chimed.

Sera had sent the final field dataset with a note:

No clean ending. Best I can do.

Mira answered:

Good.

Then, after a moment, she added:

Again tomorrow.

She sent it.

In the observation blister, no board updated. No Orison translated the words. No public record made them useful.

Outside, Pelagos held its pressure and its water and its wounded, continuing reef.

Mira stayed until the station lights cycled toward morning, not to witness an ending, but to keep herself from mistaking the absence of ceremony for emptiness.

When she finally left, the reef remained beneath the glass.

Unthanked.

Unfinished.

Not alone.
